Definite vs Indefinite in Oaths: Critique of Sadr al-Shari'ah's Reasoning
Critique of Sadr al-Shari'ah's reasoning on oath rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between definite and indefinite references in oaths involving 'house' and 'dwelling', and identify the four errors in Sadr al-Shari'ah's reasoning.

Core Idea
For definite oaths, the description is irrelevant; for indefinite, the complete form is intended.

This passage from Umdat al-Ri'ayah critiques Sadr al-Shari'ah's reasoning on oaths. The correct Hanafi position, from al-Hidayah, differentiates between definite and indefinite references: when the oath is definite (e.g., 'this house'), the description is ignored and even a ruined house counts; when indefinite (e.g., 'a house'), the description is considered and only an inhabited house counts. Sadr al-Shari'ah made several errors in his critique, which this passage systematically refutes.

Guided Path
1
State the established Hanafi position
Recall the ruling from al-Hidayah: for definite 'this house', the description is irrelevant; for indefinite 'a house', the description (being a house) is considered. Entering a ruined definite house breaks the oath; entering a ruined indefinite house does not.
2
Identify Sadr al-Shari'ah's objection
Sadr al-Shari'ah argued that the same reasoning should apply both ways, leading to a contradiction. He claimed that if 'dar' means just the plot, then building a bathhouse should still count, which he thought was wrong.
3
Analyze the four errors
The commentator lists four errors: (1) Misunderstanding the Hidayah's phrase; (2) ignoring that building is a description in 'dar'; (3) confusing 'house' (where being a house is a final cause) with 'dwelling' (where building is a description); (4) falsely claiming that if 'dar' means plot, bathhouse would count, when custom changes the name.
4
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: according to the correct position, a person who swears 'I will not enter this house' and enters after it collapses is sinful (must pay kaffarah). According to Sadr al-Shari'ah's flawed reasoning, he might not be. The correct ruling is mufta bihi.

ثُمَّ هذا المعنى يوجبُ الحنث في لا يدخلُ هذا البيت، وعدمه في لا يدخل بيتاً إن دخله منهدماً صحراء ؛ لأنَّ البيتوتة وصفٌ فيلغو في المشار إليه ، فزوال اسم البيت ينبغي أن لا يعتبر في المشار إليه. وإذا كانت الدار اسماً للعرصة، وكان البناء وصفاً، وكانت الدار منكرة كانت غائبة ، فيعتبر فيها البناء، فإذا لم يوجد لم يحنث، وإذا كانت معرّفة، كانت حاضرة فلا يعتبر فيها البناء، وإذا لم يوجد يحنث.
فإذا عرفت هذا فاعلم أنّ ما صدر هنا من صدر الشريعة له من الغرائب ؛ لأنه خالف جمهور الأئمة برأي غير صائب.
أما أولاً فلأن قوله : «فهذه العلة توجب.... الخ ؛ ناشئ من الغفلة من قول صاحب «الهداية»: «غير أن الوصف في الحاضر لغو». وأما ثانياً فلان قوله : «لأن معناه أنه إذا وصف ... الخ ؛ ناشئ من الغفلة عن معنى الوصف، وقد مرَّ أنَّ البناء وصفٌ في الدار، كما صرح به في «الهداية». وأما ثالثاً فلأن قوله : «ثم هذا المعنى ... الخ ، غلط محض ناشئ من عدم التفرقة بين البيت والدار ؛ لأنه كما عرفت عبارة عن أمر زائد على الذات قائم به ، والبيتوتة ليست كذلك، بل هي علّة غائيّة لبنائه بخلاف الدار ، فإنّ البناء زائد على الدار التي هي العرصة.
وأما رابعاً فلان حاصل قوله : «ثم قالوا... الخ ؛ إنّ الدار إذا كانت عبارة عن العرصة كان ينبغي أن يحنث فيما إذا بنيت حماماً ؛ لوجود العرصة، وهو فاسد ؛ لأنَّ الدار يطلق على العرصة المجرّدة، وعلى عرصة مع ما بني عليها من بناء الدار، فأما إذا بني . عليها من بناء غير الدار وتصرّف فيها تصرّفاً يزولُ اسم الدار عنه عرفاً، فلا يكون داراً، وكأن هذا الفاضل لم ينظر في ألفاظِ «الهداية» وعباراته ، فضلاً عن التأمل والتفكر في عباراته». انتهى كلامه(۱).
[١]قوله : إن دخله ؛ قيد لكلّ من الحنث في المعرف، وعدمه في المنكر.

ثم قالوا في : لا يدخل هذه الدار ، فدخلها بعدما بُنيت حماماً أنه لا يحنث لأنه لم يبق دارا.
أقول: لفظ الدار في الدار المعمورة غالب الاستعمال، وقد يطلق أيضاً على المنهدمة، فإذا قيل : لا أدخل دارا ، فالأولى أن يراد الدار المعمورة، وأيضاً وجوب صرف المطلق إلى الكامل، أوجَبَ إرادة المعمورة وإذا قيل : لا يدخلُ هذه الدار، فانهدم بناؤُها، فصحة إطلاقها على المنهدمة
(٣)
[١) قوله : أقول ...الخ ؛ لما كانت الوجوه التي ذكرها الفقهاء في هذا المقام مخدوشة عنده بوجوه ذكرها، أراد تعليل المسائل بوجه آخر صاف عن الكدورة. [٢]قوله : فالأولى أن يراد ؛ لأنَّ اللفظ يحمل على ما يستعمل فيه غالباً، ما لم يصرف عنه صارف. (٣) قوله : وأيضاً وجوب... الخ ؛ ظاهر هذا الدليل يخالف الدليل الأول من حيث أن الأول يثبت الأولوية، وهذا الدليلُ يثبت وجوب حمل دار منكرة على المعمورة، إلا أن يقال : المراد بالوجوب هو الوجوب الاستحساني، فيوافق الأولوية، أو يقال : المراد بالأولوية الأولوية البالغة إلى حد الوجوب، فيوافق الوجوب الأوّل أولى ؛ لأن صرف المطلق إلى الكامل ليس بواجب، بحيث لا يصح خلافه، وإنما هو من المستحسناتِ التي يتبادر الذهن إليها. ٤١ ] قوله : إلى الكامل ؛ أي إلى الفرد الكامل منه ، وهذا لتبادر الذهن إليه، ومن ثم حملوا قولهم : من السنّة كذا ، على السنّة المؤكدة، وقولهم : هذا مكروه، على المكروه تحريماً، وأمثال هذه كثيرة.
[0]قوله: فصحة...الخ ؛ حاصلُ الفرق بين الدار المنكرة وبين الدار المعرفة أنه إذا قال : لا أحلف داراً حمل الدارُ فيه على المعمورة ؛ لكونها فرداً كاملاً للدار، ولكونها كثير الاستعمال فيها ، فلا يحنث إن دخلها منهدمة، وأما المعرفة فلما كانت مشاراً إليها، والإشارة إنما تكون إلى الذواتِ مع قطع النظر عن غيرها، فحملت على الدار المطلقة معمورة كانت أو منهدمة.
Then this meaning necessitates being sinful in not entering this house, and its absence in not entering a house if he enters it while it is ruined in an empty land; because 'being a house' is a description, so it is irrelevant in the referred-to thing. So the removal of the name 'house' should not be considered in the referred-to thing. And if 'dwelling' is a name for the plot, and the building is a description, and if the dwelling is indefinite, it is absent, so the building is considered; if it is not present, he is not sinful. And if it is definite, it is present, so the building is not considered; if it is not present, he is sinful.
Since you know this, know that what has come from Sadr al-Shari'ah here is among the strange things; because he opposed the majority of the imams with an unsound opinion.
As for first: because his saying: 'This cause necessitates... etc.' arises from heedlessness of the author of al-Hidayah's statement: 'But the description in the present is irrelevant.' As for second: because his saying: 'Because its meaning is that if he describes... etc.' arises from heedlessness of the meaning of description, and it has passed that the building is a description in the dwelling, as declared in al-Hidayah. As for third: because his saying: 'Then this meaning... etc.' is pure error arising from not distinguishing between 'house' and 'dwelling'; because as you know, 'dwelling' is an expression for something superadded to the essence subsisting in it, and 'being a house' is not so, but rather it is a final cause for its construction, unlike the dwelling, for the building is superadded to the dwelling which is the plot.
As for fourth: because the gist of his saying: 'Then they said... etc.' is that if the dwelling is an expression for the plot, then he should be sinful if it is built as a bathhouse due to the existence of the plot, and that is corrupt; because 'dwelling' is applied to the bare plot and to the plot with what is built on it of dwelling construction. But if built on it with non-dwelling construction and he disposes of it in a way that removes the name 'dwelling' from it according to custom, then it is not a dwelling. And it is as if this virtuous man did not look at the expressions of al-Hidayah and its phrases, let alone pondering and reflecting on its phrases.' His speech ends.
[1] His saying: 'If he enters it' is a condition both for being sinful in the definite and for its absence in the indefinite.

The Book of Oaths: The oath regarding entering, dwelling, leaving, coming, and riding
51
Then they said regarding: 'Do not enter this dwelling', and he entered it after it was built as a bathhouse, that he is not sinful because it did not remain a dwelling.
I say: The word 'dwelling' in the inhabited dwelling is most common in usage, and it may also be applied to the ruined one. So if one says: 'I will not enter a dwelling', the first thing is that the inhabited dwelling is intended. Also, the obligation to direct the unqualified to the complete requires intending the inhabited dwelling. And if one says: 'Do not enter this dwelling', and its building collapses, so the correctness of applying it to the ruined one...
(3)
[1] His saying: 'I say... etc.': Since the aspects that the jurists mentioned in this place are flawed in his view with aspects he mentioned, he wanted to explain the issues with another aspect pure from turbidity. [2] His saying: 'The first thing is that it is intended': because the word is interpreted according to what it is commonly used for, as long as something diverts from it. (3) His saying: 'Also, the obligation... etc.': The apparent meaning of this evidence contradicts the first evidence in that the first establishes priority, while this evidence establishes the obligation of interpreting an indefinite 'dwelling' as the inhabited one, unless it is said: what is meant by 'obligation' is istihsani obligation, so it agrees with priority, or it is said: what is meant by 'priority' is priority that reaches the level of obligation, so it agrees with the first obligation better; because directing the unqualified to the complete is not obligatory such that its contrary is invalid, rather it is among the istihsanat that the mind immediately thinks of. [4] His saying: 'to the complete': i.e., to the complete individual of it, and this is due to the mind immediately thinking of it. Hence they interpreted their saying: 'such and such is from the sunnah' as the stressed sunnah, and their saying: 'this is disliked' as the prohibitively disliked, and similar examples are many.
[0] His saying: 'So the correctness... etc.': The gist of the difference between the indefinite 'dwelling' and the definite 'dwelling' is that if he says: 'I will not take an oath regarding a dwelling', the word 'dwelling' therein is interpreted as the inhabited one because it is the complete individual of the dwelling and because it is frequently used in it, so he is not sinful if he enters it ruined. As for the definite, since it is pointed to, and pointing is only to the essences without consideration of other things, it is interpreted as the absolute dwelling, whether inhabited or ruined.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) from Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah, addressing the rulings for oaths involving 'house' (bayt) and 'dwelling' (dar). The author critiques the reasoning of Sadr al-Shari'ah regarding the distinction between definite and indefinite references.

The established Hanafi position from al-Hidayah is that when a person swears not to enter 'this house' (definite), the description of being a house is irrelevant because the oath refers to the specific entity. Hence, even if the building collapses, entering the plot still constitutes a breach (hanth). Conversely, when swearing not to enter 'a house' (indefinite), the description is considered; if the building is ruined, entering does not break the oath because the essence of 'house' no longer exists.

Sadr al-Shari'ah made several errors according to the commentator:
1. He misconstrued the Hidayah's statement that 'the description in the present (definite) is irrelevant' by applying it incorrectly.
2. He failed to distinguish between 'house' (where 'being a house' is a final cause for construction) and 'dwelling' (where the building is a description superadded to the plot).
3. He incorrectly argued that if 'dar' means just the plot, then building a bathhouse would still count as 'dar', but custom changes the name.
4. He neglected the actual expressions of al-Hidayah.

The commentator then clarifies the correct position: For indefinite 'dar', the word is more commonly used for inhabited dwellings, so it is preferred (or even obligatory in an istihsani sense) to interpret it as the complete (inhabited) form. For definite 'this dar', the oath is about the specific entity regardless of its current state, so entering it even if ruined breaks the oath.

Key terms: 'bayt' is a house (structure), 'dar' is a dwelling that includes the plot. 'Ba'ytuta' (being a house) is a description that is irrelevant in definite oaths.

Key Terms
المشار إليه (al-mushār ilayh): The specific entity pointed to in a definite reference (e.g., 'this house'). In oaths, it refers to the actual object regardless of its attributes.
وصف (wasf): An attribute or description, such as 'being a house' (baytūtah) or 'being a building'. In oaths, if the noun is definite, the description is considered irrelevant (laghw); if indefinite, the description is considered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I will not enter this house.' Later, the house collapses into ruins. Does entering the plot break his oath according to the correct Hanafi position?
A) Yes, because the description is irrelevant in definite references.
B) No, because the house no longer exists.
C) Yes, but only if he intends to rebuild it.
D) No, because 'house' in oaths always means an inhabited house.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, definite oaths point to the entity itself (description irrelevant), while indefinite oaths consider the description, affecting whether entering a ruined structure breaks the oath.

Defining Ghadā' and 'Ashā' in Hanafi Oaths
Defining time and quantity for meals in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criteria for what constitutes a valid lunch (ghadā') or dinner ('ashā') in an oath, and distinguish between the meal name and the act of eating.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'lunch' means eating more than half of satiety according to local custom, not just any food at midday.

When someone swears to eat lunch or dinner, the legal definition of these meals becomes crucial. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah clarifies the temporal boundaries, the requirement of continuity, and the threshold of satiety. Understanding these distinctions prevents common mistakes in oath fulfillment.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the legal terms
The matn defines 'ghadā'' and 'ashā'' as nouns for the food eaten at specific times. The commentator recommends using the verbal nouns 'taghaddī' and 'ta'ashshī' for the act of eating. Note that the vowels differentiate 'ashā'' (dinner) from 'ishā'' (night prayer).
The hadith cited uses both meanings with different vowels.
2
2. Determine the temporal boundaries
The base text sets ghadā' from dawn to noon and 'ashā' from noon to midnight. A minority view from al-Khulāsa considers the start from sunrise, which the author of al-Bahr recommends adopting based on custom.
The variation is due to customary practice, not a legal dispute.
3
3. Analyze the quantitative and qualitative requirements
'Eating' (al-akl) is interpreted as consecutive eating aimed at satiety. A few disconnected bites do not count. The meal must exceed half of the customary fullness (akthar min nisf al-shab'), as established in al-Hidayah.
Custom of each locality is considered.
4
4. Apply the definition to a scenario
Given a specific scenario (e.g., a person swears to eat lunch then eats a date and stops), determine whether the oath is fulfilled or broken based on the criteria.

Source Text

والغداء الأكل من طلوع الفجر إلى الظهر (والغداء الأكل من طلوع الفجر إلى الظهر). [1] قوله : والغداء ؛ الأولى أن يقول بدله : التغدي، وبدل العشاء : التعشي؛ فإنّ الغداء - بالفتح - اسم لما يؤكل من الفجر إلى الزوال، والعشاء - بالفتح - اسم لما يؤكل منه إلى نصف الليل، ومنه قوله : إذا حضر العشاء والعشاء - بالكسر : أي صلاة العشاء - فابدؤوا بالعشاء» (۳). والتغدي والتعشي مصدران بمعنى الأكل في الوقتين المذكورين، وقال في «الفتح» : إنه تساهل معروف المعنى لا يعترض به. [2] قوله : الأكل ؛ أي المترادف، فلو أكل لقمتين ثم فصل بزمن يعد فاصلاً، ثم أكل لقمتين لا يُسمى تغدياً، والمعتبر في التغدّي والتعشي أكل ما يقصد به الشبع عادة، وتعتبر عادة أهل كل بلدة في حقهم، ويشترط أن يكون الغداء والعشاء أكثر من نصف الشبع. كذا في «الهداية» وحواشيها. [3] قوله : الفجر ؛ أي الصبح الصادق، وفي «الخلاصة»: من طلوع الشمس، قال صاحب «البحر» : وينبغي اعتماده للعرف. [4] قوله : إلى الظهر؛ أي إلى أول وقته وهو زوال الشمس.
And 'ghadā'' is eating from the break of dawn to noon. [1] His statement: 'And ghadā'' – it would be better to say in its place 'taghaddī', and in place of 'ashā'' say 'ta'ashshī', because 'ghadā'' (with fatha) is the name of that which is eaten from dawn to noon, and 'ashā'' (with fatha) is the name of that which is eaten from noon to midnight. From it is his saying: 'When the evening meal (al-‘ashā’) and the night prayer (al-‘ashā’ – with kasra, i.e. the ‘ishā’ prayer) are present, begin with the evening meal.' 'Taghaddī' and 'ta'ashshī' are infinitives meaning eating in the two mentioned times. He said in al-Fath: It is a leniency with known meaning and is not objected to. [2] His statement: 'eating' i.e., consecutive eating. If he eats two bites, then pauses for a time that counts as a break, then eats two bites, it is not called 'taghaddī'. Considered in 'taghaddī' and 'ta'ashshī' is eating that aims at satiety customarily. The custom of each town's people is considered regarding them. It is stipulated that 'ghadā'' and 'ashā'' be more than half of fullness. Thus in al-Hidayah and its glosses. [3] His statement: 'dawn' i.e., the true dawn (al-subh al-sādiq). In al-Khulāsa: from sunrise. The author of al-Bahr said: One should adopt that for custom. [4] His statement: 'to noon' i.e., to its beginning time which is the sun's zenith.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage from the commentary defines the terms 'ghadā'' (lunch) and 'ashā'' (dinner) as they pertain to oaths. The matn states that ghadā' is eating from dawn to noon. The commentator clarifies that the nouns 'ghadā'' and 'ashā'' refer to the food eaten, but the intended meaning is the act of eating lunch and dinner (taghaddī and ta'ashshī). This is a common leniency in language. For an oath to be broken by not eating lunch or dinner, the eating must be consecutive and intended to reach satiety according to local custom. A mere snack or interrupted eating does not count. The time for ghadā' starts at true dawn, but some scholars consider sunrise as the start based on custom. The end time is the beginning of noon (zawāl).

Key Terms
غداء (ghadā'): The midday meal, eaten between dawn and noon; in oaths, the act of eating lunch (taghaddī) with the intention of satiety.
عشاء (ashā'): The evening meal, eaten between noon and midnight; in oaths, the act of eating dinner (ta'ashshī) with the intention of satiety.
تغدي (taghaddī): The verbal noun meaning eating lunch, considered more precise than 'ghadā'' for the act of eating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum amount of eating required for an act to be considered 'taghaddī' (eating lunch) in an oath?
A) Any amount, even a single bite.
B) More than half of satiety according to local custom.
C) At least three bites taken consecutively.
D) Eating until completely full.

Key Takeaway
A valid lunch or dinner in a Hanafi oath requires consecutive eating aimed at satiety, exceeding half of the customary full meal, within the defined time window.

Defining Meal Times and Custom in Oaths
Defines meal terms and applies custom to oath rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of ghada', 'asha', and suhur, and apply the principle that oaths are interpreted according to custom ('urf) to determine if an oath is broken.

Core Idea
Oaths are governed by custom — raw meat does not break an oath against eating meat.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah refines the definitions of meal times in the context of oaths. It clarifies that terms like ghada' and 'asha' refer to customary meals, and that 'urf (custom) is the governing principle for interpreting what an oath actually prohibits. The commentator also addresses an important intra-Hanafi dispute: does eating raw meat break an oath not to eat meat? The relied-upon answer is no, because custom dictates that 'meat' means cooked meat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms and their definitions
The passage defines three meal terms: ghada' (from dawn to noon), 'asha' (from noon to midnight), and suhur (the last sixth of the night). Note that the author points out linguistic preferences but does not alter the legal ruling.
The terms are nouns for the food; the verbal nouns taghaddi and ta'ashshi are more precise for the act of eating.
2
Extract the key legal principle
The commentator asserts that 'oaths are based on custom' (mabna al-ayman 'ala al-'urf). This principle overrides literal meanings when the swearer's intention is tied to customary practice.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-'Utabi reported a contrary view that eating raw meat breaks the oath. The commentator rejects this, citing al-Kafi and Fath al-Qadir, and upholds the custom-based view as mufta bihi (the operative fatwa).
The opponent's reasoning: literal meaning includes raw meat. Hanafi response: the oath is contextual; the swearer intends what is normally eaten.
4
Apply the principle to other meal terms
The passage also notes that 'asha' includes the time for the 'ishya' prayer, and suhur is tied to sahar. Local customs (e.g., Egypt and Syria) can override these general definitions.
This flexibility is central to Hanafi treatment of oaths.

والغداء الأكل من طلوع الفجر إلى الظهر. والغداء الأكل من طلوع الفجر إلى الظهر. ولذا نقل العتابي خلاف ما هاهنا، فقال: قيل: الحالف إذا كان مسلماً ينبغي أن لا يحنث؛ لأنّ أكله ليس بمتعارف ومبنى الأيمان على العرف، وهو الصحيح، وفي «الكافي»: وعليه الفتوى، كذا حققه في «فتح القدير» ويؤيده ما في «الذخيرة» وغيرها: إنّه لا يحنث في لا يأكل لحماً بأكل اللحم الني؛ لأنه عقد يمينه على ما يؤكل عادة، فينصرف إلى المعتاد، وهو الأكل بعد الطبخ. قوله: والغداء؛ الأولى أن يقول بدله: التغدي، وبدل العشاء: التعشي؛ فإنّ الغداء - بالفتح - اسم لما يؤكل من الفجر إلى الزوال، والعشاء - بالفتح - اسم لما يؤكل منه إلى نصف الليل، ومنه قوله: إذا حضر العشاء والعشاء - بالكسر: أي صلاة العشاء - فابدؤوا بالعشاء. والتغدي والتعشي مصدران بمعنى الأكل في الوقتين المذكورين، وقال في «الفتح»: إنه تساهل معروف المعنى لا يعترض به. قوله: الأكل؛ أي المترادف، فلو أكل لقمتين ثم فصل بزمن يعد فاصلاً، ثم أكل لقمتين لا يُسمى تغدياً، والمعتبر في التغدي والتعشي أكل ما يقصد به الشبع عادة، وتعتبر عادة أهل كل بلدة في حقهم، ويشترط أن يكون الغداء والعشاء أكثر من نصف الشبع. كذا في «الهداية وحواشيها. قوله: الفجر؛ أي الصبح الصادق، وفي «الخلاصة»: من طلوع الشمس، قال صاحب «البحر»: وينبغي اعتماده للعرف. قوله: إلى الظهر؛ أي إلى أول وقته وهو زوال الشمس. والعشاء منه إلى نصف الليل والسحور منه إلى الفجر. قوله: منه إلى نصف الليل؛ أي من زوال الشمس إلى نصف الليل؛ ولذا تسمى صلاة الظهر والعصر صلاة العشي، كما ورد في الحديث في «الصحيحين». قوله: والسحور؛ هو - بالفتح - اسم لما يؤكل وقت السحر، وهو السدس الأخير من الليل، وبالضم مصدر بمعنى أكل الطعام في ذلك الوقت، وقد قرئ بهما السحور في حديث: 'تسحروا فإنّ في السحور بركة'. وفي عرف مصر والشام ما يؤكل من طلوع الشمس إلى ارتفاع الضحى يسمى فطوراً، والعشاء ما بعد صلاة العصر، فيعمل بعرفهم.
And al-ghada' is eating from the break of dawn to noon. And al-ghada' is eating from the break of dawn to noon. For this reason al-‘Utabi transmitted a contrary opinion here, saying: It was said: if the swearer is a Muslim, he should not be considered to have broken his oath, because his eating is not customary and oaths are based on custom, and this is correct, and in al-Kafi: the fatwa is upon it. This is verified in Fath al-Qadir, and it is supported by what is in al-Dhakhirah and others: that he does not break his oath by eating raw meat when he swore not to eat meat, because he formed his oath based on what is normally eaten, so it is interpreted by the customary, which is eating after cooking. His statement: 'wa al-ghada'': it would be better to say instead: al-taghaddi, and instead of al-‘asha': al-ta‘ashshi; because al-ghada' (with fatha) is the name for what is eaten from dawn to noon, and al-‘asha' (with fatha) is the name for what is eaten from there to midnight, and from it the hadith: 'If the evening meal is served and the ‘isha' prayer (with kasra: meaning the ‘isha' prayer) begins, start with the evening meal.' Al-taghaddi and al-ta‘ashshi are verbal nouns meaning eating in those two times. And in al-Fath he said: this is a leniency with a well-known meaning that cannot be objected to. His statement: 'al-akl' means continuous eating; if he eats two mouthfuls, then separates by a time considered a significant gap, then eats two more, it is not called taghaddi. What is considered in taghaddi and ta‘ashshi is eating that is intended for satiety customarily, and the custom of the people of each locality is considered regarding them, and it is required that the ghada' and ‘asha' exceed half of satiety. This is in al-Hidayah and its commentaries. His statement: 'al-fajr' means the true dawn, and in al-Khulasah: from sunrise, and the author of al-Bahr said: one should adopt this due to custom. His statement: 'to al-zuhr' means to the beginning of its time, which is the sun's zenith. And al-‘asha' is from there to midnight, and al-suhur is from there to dawn. His statement: 'from there to midnight' means from the sun's zenith to midnight; therefore the zuhr and asr prayers are called the two 'ashiy prayers, as in the hadith in the Sahihayn. His statement: 'and al-suhur' is (with fatha) a name for what is eaten at the time of sahar, which is the last sixth of the night, and with damm it is a verbal noun meaning eating food at that time. Both readings of al-suhur occur in the hadith: 'Take suhur for there is blessing in suhur.' And in the custom of Egypt and Syria, what is eaten from sunrise until the time when the sun rises high is called 'futur', and the evening meal is after the asr prayer, so they act according to their custom.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is an expansion and refinement of the matn's definitions of meal times (ghada', 'asha', suhur) in the context of oaths. The commentator begins by clarifying that the matn uses 'al-ghada'' as a noun for the meal eaten from dawn to noon, but linguistically the verbal noun 'al-taghaddi' would be more precise for the act of eating; however, this is a minor issue. The key ruling discussed is whether eating raw meat breaks an oath not to eat meat. The commentator reports a contrary view from al-'Utabi but affirms that the correct position — and the one upon which fatwa is based — is that it does not, because oaths are interpreted according to custom ('urf), and raw meat is not customarily eaten. This is supported by the principle that the swearer's intention is tied to customary practice. The passage then details that 'al-ghada'' and 'al-'asha'' refer to meals intended for satiety, with a requirement that they exceed half of satiety. It also notes that the time for 'asha' extends from noon to midnight, and for suhur from midnight to dawn. The commentator cites a hadith about suhur and an anecdote about the 'ashiy prayers. Additionally, it acknowledges that local customs may override these general definitions, as in Egypt and Syria.

Key Terms
الغداء (al-ghada'): The meal eaten from the break of dawn to noon; a noun for the food itself.
العشاء (al-‘asha'): The meal eaten from noon to midnight; also a noun for the food.
السحور (al-suhur): The meal eaten at the time of sahar (the last sixth of the night). With fatha: noun; with damm: verbal noun.
العرف (al-‘urf): Custom or common practice; the basis for interpreting oaths regarding what is eaten.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, does eating raw meat break an oath sworn as 'I will not eat meat'?
A) Yes, because the literal meaning includes raw meat.
B) No, because custom interprets 'meat' as cooked meat that is normally eaten.
C) It depends on the intention of the swearer.
D) Only if the raw meat is from an animal prohibited to eat.

Key Takeaway
Oaths are interpreted by custom: raw meat does not break an oath against eating meat because custom restricts 'meat' to cooked, normally consumed meals.

Oath to Drink Water: Conditions of Birr
Ruling on oath validity when birr is impossible or time-bound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the validity of an oath to perform a specific act, distinguish between time-bound and absolute oaths, and explain the Hanafi qiyas that determines the timing of obligation.

Core Idea
If birr is impossible at the oath, the oath is void; if possible, the obligation is expansive until the last moment of the specified time or until death.

This lesson examines a classical Hanafi ruling on oaths, focusing on a hypothetical oath to drink water from a cup. The key question is whether the oath is valid when fulfillment (birr) is impossible from the start or when the time for fulfillment is limited. The Hanafi school uses qiyas (analogy) to time-bound obligatory acts to determine when the obligation becomes due and when the oath is breached.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition for validity
The act is drinking water. The condition for a valid oath is that birr must be possible at the time of the oath or within the specified time.
2
Analyze the case with no water
If the cup has no water, birr is impossible from the start, so the oath is invalid. No sin or expiation is required.
This is the straightforward case: 'la tan'aqid al-yamin'.
3
Analyze the time-bound case
If the swearer mentions a specific day, birr is only due in the last part of that day. Pouring out the water before that time makes birr impossible, but since the obligation was not yet due, the oath becomes void without hanth.
The analogy (qiyas) is to other time-bound acts like fasting, which are expansive (musawwa').
4
Analyze the absolute case
If no day is mentioned, birr is due immediately after the oath but in an expansive manner throughout life. If the water is deliberately lost before death, the obligation becomes restrictive immediately, causing hanth at that moment.
The refinement from Radd al-Muhtar clarifies that loss of the locus (mahal) reveals the true nature of the obligation.
5
Follow the qiyas argument and rebuttals
The expansive nature of time-bound oaths is supported by qiyas to other time-bound obligations. An objection from al-Fath is answered by al-Nahr and further refined by Radd al-Muhtar, establishing the Hanafi position.
This step ties the ruling to usul al-fiqh principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان/ تعريفه وأقسامه
بشرط أن لا فالبر غير ممكن سواء ذكر اليوم أو لا، وإن كان فيه ماء فإن ذكر اليوم فالبر إنما يجب عليه في الجزء الأخير من اليوم، فإذا صب لم يكن البر متصوراً. وإن لم يذكر اليوم فالبر إنما يجب عليه إذا فرغ من التكلم، لكن موسعاً يفوته في مدة عمره، والبر متصور عند الفراغ من التكلم فانعقد اليمين. قوله: فالبر غير ممكن؛ لعدم وجود الماء في الكوز، فلا تنعقد اليمين فلا الحنث الموجب للكفارة. قوله: إنما يجب عليه...الخ؛ وذلك لأنه لما وقته بوقت يفضل عنه صار موسعاً، فلا يجب عليه إلا في الجزء الأخير منه، كما في سائر المؤقتات الموسعة. قوله: فإذا صب؛ أي قبل تمام اليوم وغيره من الوقت الذي عينه. قوله: فالبر...الخ؛ اعترض عليه في «الفتح»: «بأن وجوبه في الحال بمجرد فراغه من التكلم إن كان بمعنى تعينه حتى يحنث في ثاني الحال، فهو ليس بصحيح، وإن كان بمعنى الوجوب الموسع إلى المؤقت، فيحنث في آخر جزء من الحياة. فاليمين المؤقتة كذلك؛ لأنه لا يحنث لا في آخر جزء من الوقت الذي ذكره فذلك الجزء بمنزلة آخر جزء من الحياة، فلأي معنى تبطل اليمين عند آخر جزء من الوقت في المؤقتة، ولم تبطل عند آخر جزء من الحياة في المطلقة» وأجاب عنه في «النهر» بأن الحالف في المؤقتة لم يلزم نفسه بالفعل إلا في آخر الوقت بخلاف المطلقة؛ لأنه لا فائدة في التأخير. وقال الشيخ أمين في رد المحتار: «أنت خبير بأنه غير دافع مع استلزامه وجوب البر في المطلقة على فور الحلف وإلا فلا فرق، ويظهر لي الجواب بأن المقيدة لما كان لها غاية معلومة لم يتعين الفعل إلا في آخر وقتها، فإذا فات المحل فقد فات قبل الوجوب فتبطل ولا يحنث؛ لعدم إمكان البر وقت تعينه. أما المطلقة فغايتها آخر جزء من الحياة، وذلك الوقت لا يمكن البر فيه، ولا خلفه، وهو الكفارة، فيبقى في الإثم فيتعين الوجوب قبله، ولا ترجيح لوقت دون آخر، فلزم الوجوب عقب الحلف موسعاً بشرط عدم الفوات، فإذا فات المحل ظهر أن الوجوب كان مضيقاً من أول أوقات الإمكان، ونظيره ما قرروه في القول بوجوب الحج موسعاً.
Book of Oaths / Its Definition and Sections. On condition that not... The fulfillment is not possible, whether he mentioned the day or not. And if there is water in it, and he mentioned the day, then fulfillment (birr) is only obligatory upon him in the last part of the day. So if he pours it (the water) out, then fulfillment is not conceivable. And if he did not mention the day, then fulfillment is only obligatory upon him when he finishes speaking, but it is expansive (musawwa'), it escapes him during his lifetime, and fulfillment is conceivable at the moment of finishing speaking, so the oath is valid. His statement: 'The fulfillment is not possible' because there is no water in the cup, so the oath is not valid, so there is no violation (hanth) that necessitates expiation. His statement: 'It is only obligatory upon him...etc.' because when he tied it to a time that exceeds him, it becomes expansive, so it is not obligatory upon him except in the last part of it, as in all other expansive time-bound obligations. His statement: 'So if he pours it' meaning before the completion of the day or other time he specified. His statement: 'The fulfillment...etc.' An objection was raised against it in al-Fath: 'If its obligation immediately upon his finishing speaking is in the sense of specification so that he violates (yanth) in the next moment, it is not correct; and if it is in the sense of expansive obligation until the specified time, then he violates in the last part of life. The time-bound oath is similar because he does not violate except in the last part of the time he mentioned, that part is like the last part of life. So for what reason does the oath become void at the last part of time in a time-bound oath, and not become void at the last part of life in an absolute oath?' And it was answered in al-Nahr that the swearer in a time-bound oath did not obligate himself to the action except in the last of the time, unlike the absolute oath because there is no benefit in delaying. And Shaykh Amin said in Radd al-Muhtar: 'You are aware that it is not a repellant, since it necessitates the obligation of birr in the absolute oath immediately upon the oath, otherwise there is no difference. The answer appears to me that since the limited oath has a known end, the action is not specified except in its last time. So if the locus (mahal) is lost, it is lost before obligation, so it becomes void and he does not violate because birr is impossible at the time of its specification. As for the absolute oath, its end is the last part of life, and that time birr is not possible in it, nor its substitute, which is expiation. So he remains in sin, and the obligation is specified before it, and there is no preference for one time over another. So the obligation immediately after the oath is expansive on condition of not being missed. And if the locus is lost, it becomes clear that the obligation was restricted from the first time of possibility. And its example is what they established in the obligation of Hajj being expansive.'
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof discussion on the validity of an oath to drink water from a cup, analyzing when fulfillment (birr) is possible. It begins by stating the rule: if there is no water in the cup, birr is impossible, so the oath is not valid and no sin occurs. If there is water and the swearer mentioned a specific day, birr is only obligatory in the last part of that day—so if he pours out the water before that, birr becomes impossible and the oath is void. If he did not mention a day, birr is obligatory immediately after the oath but in an expansive (musawwa') manner throughout his life. Since birr is conceivable at the moment of finishing the oath, the oath is valid. The commentary then explains these points: the impossibility of birr when no water exists; the expansive nature of time-bound obligations by analogy to other time-bound acts; and that pouring out water before the deadline eliminates the possibility. An objection from al-Fath questions why an absolute oath remains valid until death while a time-bound oath becomes void at the end of its specified time. The answer in al-Nahr distinguishes time-bound oaths (self-imposed only for the last part) from absolute oaths (where delay yields no benefit). Shaykh Amin in Radd al-Muhtar refines this: for limited oaths, the action is not specified until the last moment, so loss of the locus (e.g., water poured) before that time prevents obligation entirely—no sin. For absolute oaths, death is the limit, at which birr is impossible but expiation serves as a substitute, so the swearer remains in sin and the obligation is specified immediately after the oath in an expansive manner, becoming restrictive only when the locus is lost. This parallels the ruling on Hajj being expansive.

Key Terms
البر (al-birr): Fulfillment of the action sworn to do, e.g., drinking the water.
موسع (musawwa'): Expansive; an obligation that can be performed at any time within a broader window, not immediately due.
حنث (hanth): The occurrence of sin/breach of the oath due to failure to fulfill birr when possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I will drink the water in this cup.' The cup has no water. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The oath is valid; he must sin and pay kaffarah.
B) The oath is not valid; no sin or kaffarah.
C) The oath is valid only if he mentioned a day.
D) The oath is valid but he must drink water from another source.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of an oath depends on the possibility of fulfillment (birr); if birr is impossible at the outset, the oath is void, and if it is possible but time-bound, the obligation is expansive until the last part of the time.

Oath to Walk for Hajj: Dispute on Wording Scope
Ruling on oath to walk for Hajj/Umrah with intra-Hanafi differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the obligation of walking to the Haram and explain the basis of each view, and apply the ruling to different oath wordings.

Core Idea
Walking to the House of Allah obligates Hajj/Umrah; walking to the Haram is disputed between Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn.

When a person swears an oath to walk to a sacred site, the precise wording determines the legal obligation. The Hanafi school agrees that 'upon me is walking to the House of Allah' obligates Hajj or 'Umrah on foot. However, if the wording refers to 'the Haram' or 'the Sacred Mosque', a difference arises between Imam Abu Hanifah and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath categories
Separate oaths that mention 'Bayt Allah' or 'Ka'bah' from those mentioning 'al-Haram' or 'al-Masjid al-Haram'.
These are treated differently in the school.
2
Apply the agreed ruling for 'Bayt Allah'
When the oath specifies 'Bayt Allah' or 'Ka'bah', the obligation to perform Hajj or 'Umrah on foot is agreed upon by all Hanafi imams.
3
Identify the disputed wording
When the oath says 'al-Haram' or 'al-Masjid al-Haram', the ruling is disputed. Step through each imam's position.
Al-Safa and al-Marwah are also mentioned but agreed not to obligate.
4
Compare proofs of Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifah argues wording is not conventional (ghayr muta'arif); the Sahibayn argue the Haram includes the Ka'bah so it implies the same obligation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifah, no action required; under the Sahibayn, Hajj or 'Umrah walking is obligatory.
The later view may follow the Sahibayn as per 'difference of time and era'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
افصل اليمين في الحج والصلاة والصوم [ثم قال:] وبعلي المشي إلى بيت الله تعالى، أو إلى الكعبة، يجب الحج أو عمرة مشياً.

[قال الشارح:] قوله: وبعلي المشي؛ هو عبارة عن السير على الأقدام من دون ركوب، وهذه المسألة وإن كانت من مسائل النذر لوجودِ صيغة الالتزام فيه، لكنّ النذر لما كان يحتمل اليمين كما مرّ ذكره في هذا البحث.

قوله: إلى بيت الله؛ المراد به إذا أطلق الكعبة، وأضاف البيت إلى الله للتشريف؛ ولهذا المعنى يقال للمسجد: بيت الله، ولسيدنا عيسى على نبينا وعليه السلام روح الله وكلمته ولنورِ نبينا أنه خلق من نور الله، أو أنه نور من نور الله، وليس معناه ما تتسارع إليه أفهام العوام من أنّ الله أخذ قبضة من ذاته التي هي نور بحت وجعله نور حبيبه بحيث تكون الذات الإلهية مادة للذات المحمدية تعالى الله عن ذلك.

قوله: يجب الحج أو عمرة؛ لأنه تعرف إيجاب أحد التسكين [كذا] بهذا اللفظ، فصار فيه مجازاً لغوياً، وحقيقة عرفية، مثل ما لو قال: علي حج أو عمرة، وإلا فالقياس أن لا يجب شيء؛ لأنه التزم ما ليس بقربة مقصودة ولا واجبة، وهي المشي. (كذا في «الفتح»).

قوله: مشياً؛ أي من بلدة، قال في «النهر»: «إن لم يكن بمكة لزمه المشي من بيته على الراجح، لا من حيث يحرم من الميقات والخلاف فيما إذا لم يحرم من بيته، فإنّ أحرم منه لزمه المشي منه اتفاقاً، وإن كان بمكّة وأراد أن يجعل الذي لزمه حجاً، فإنّه يحرم من الحرم ويخرج إلى عرفات ماشياً، إلى أن يطوف طواف الزيارة، وإن أراد إسقاطه بعمرة فعليه أن يخرج إلى الحل ويحرم منه.»

ودم إن ركب، ولا شيء بعلي الخروج، أو الذهاب إلى بيت الله تعالى، أو المشي إلى الحرم، أو المسجد الحرام، أو الصفا والمروة. ولا يُعتق عبد قيل له: إن لم أحج ودم إن ركب، ولا شيء بعلي الخروج، أو الذهاب إلى بيت الله تعالى، أو المشي إلى الحرم، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وأما عند أبي يوسف ومحمد فيلزمه حج أو عمرة مشياً، أو المسجد الحرام، أو الصفا والمروة. ولا يُعتق عبد قيل له: إن لم أحج.

وهل يلزمه المشي في ذهابه فيه خلاف، والوجه يقضي أنه يلزمه؛ إذ الحاج يلزمه المشي من بلدته مع أنه ليس محرماً، بل ذاهب إلى محل الإحرام، ليحرم منه، فكذا هذا.

قوله: ودم؛ أي يجب دم إن ركب في جميع الأوقات أو في أكثرها لإدخاله النقص، وقد مرّ «بحث وجوب الدماء» في «كتاب الحج».

قوله: هذا؛ أي عدم وجوب شيء في: علي المشي إلى الحرم، وأما عدم وجوب شيء فيما قبله فاتّفاقي، وكذا في قوله: علي المشي إلى الصفا والمروة.

قوله: وأما عند أبي يوسف ومحمد؛ الأولى أن يذكر هذا الاختلاف بعد قوله: المسجد الحرام؛ لوجود الخلاف فيه أيضاً.

ووجه قولهما: إنّ الحرمَ شامل على الكعبة وكذا المسجد الحرام، فصار ذكرهما كذكره، بخلاف الصفا والمروة؛ لأنهما منفصلان عنه. وله: إنّ التزام الإحرام بهذه العبارة غير متعارف. كذا في «الهداية»، وذكر في «الفتح» وغيره: إنّ هذا الخلاف خلاف عصر وزمان.

قوله: ولا يعتق... إلخ؛ يعني لو قال المولى: إن لم أحج في هذه السنة فأنت
The section on oaths concerning Hajj, prayer, and fasting. [Then he said:] And upon me is walking to the House of Allah Almighty, or to the Ka'bah; Hajj or 'Umrah walking becomes obligatory. The commentator said: His statement: 'And upon me is walking' means traveling on foot without riding. This issue, although it belongs to the issues of vows due to the presence of a binding formula, but the vow can contain an oath as previously mentioned in this discussion. His statement: 'to the House of Allah' means the Ka'bah when used absolutely, and attributing the house to Allah is for honor. Thus, the mosque is called the House of Allah, and our master Jesus (peace be upon him) is called the Spirit of Allah and His Word, and the light of our Prophet is that he was created from the light of Allah, or it is light from the light of Allah. It does not mean what common people think that Allah took a handful of His essence which is pure light and made it the light of His beloved so that the divine essence becomes the material for the Muhammadan essence; Allah is exalted above that. His statement: 'Hajj or 'Umrah becomes obligatory' because the obligation of one of the two things is recognized by this wording, so it becomes a linguistic metaphor and a customary reality, like if he said: 'Upon me is Hajj or 'Umrah'. Otherwise, by analogy nothing should be obligatory because he committed himself to something that is neither a intentional act of worship nor obligatory, namely walking. (Thus in al-Fath). His statement: 'walking' means from his town. He said in al-Nahr: 'If he is not in Makkah, walking from his house is necessary according to the preponderant view, not from where he enters ihram. The dispute is when he does not begin ihram from his house; if he begins ihram from his house, walking from there is agreed upon. If he is in Makkah and wants to make what obligated him as Hajj, he enters ihram from the Haram and goes out to 'Arafat on foot until he performs Tawaf al-Ziyarah. If he wants to discharge it by 'Umrah, he must go out to the Hill and enter ihram there.' And a sacrificial animal if he rides, and nothing is required by 'upon me is going out' or 'going to the House of Allah' or 'walking to the Haram' or 'the Sacred Mosque' or 'al-Safa and al-Marwah'. Nor is a slave freed if it is said to him: 'If I do not perform Hajj'. And a sacrificial animal if he rides, and nothing is required by 'upon me is going out' or 'going to the House of Allah' or 'walking to the Haram'. This is according to Abu Hanifah. As for Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, Hajj or 'Umrah walking becomes obligatory, or to the Sacred Mosque, or to al-Safa and al-Marwah. Nor is a slave freed if it is said to him: 'If I do not perform Hajj'. And is walking incumbent upon him in his going? There is dispute, but the preferred view is that it is incumbent upon him, because the pilgrim is required to walk from his town even though he is not in ihram, but rather going to the place of ihram to enter ihram from there, so likewise here. His statement: 'and a sacrificial animal' means a sacrificial animal becomes obligatory if he rides in all times or in most of them because of the deficiency introduced. The discussion on obligation of sacrificial animals has passed in the Book of Hajj. His statement: 'this' means the non-obligation of anything in 'upon me is walking to the Haram'. As for the non-obligation of anything before that, it is agreed upon, and similarly in his statement: 'upon me is walking to al-Safa and al-Marwah'. His statement: 'As for according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad' – it is better to mention this difference after his statement: 'the Sacred Mosque' because there is also disagreement concerning it. The reasoning of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is that the Haram includes the Ka'bah and also the Sacred Mosque, so mentioning them is like mentioning it, unlike al-Safa and al-Marwah because they are separate from it. His reasoning: committing to ihram by this expression is not conventional. Thus in al-Hidayah, and it is mentioned in al-Fath and others that this disagreement is a disagreement of time and era. His statement: 'Nor is a slave freed...' means if the master says: 'If I do not perform Hajj this year then you are...'
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah, discussing oaths related to walking for Hajj or 'Umrah. The passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The original matn states: 'And upon me is walking to the House of Allah, or to the Ka'bah, Hajj or 'Umrah walking becomes obligatory.' The commentator first defines 'mashyan' (walking) as traveling on foot. He clarifies that this issue is related to vows but can also be an oath. He then explains 'Bayt Allah' means the Ka'bah, and refutes anthropomorphic interpretations. The commentator explains why saying 'upon me is walking' obligates Hajj or 'Umrah: because the phrase is conventionally understood as vowing to perform one of these acts of worship. By analogy, nothing would be obligatory since walking itself is not a designated act of worship. However, custom (urf) transforms it into a vow for Hajj or 'Umrah. The commentator then discusses details: walking must be from one's town (not from miqat) according to the preponderant view; if one rides, a sacrificial animal (dam) is required. Then the intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: According to Imam Abu Hanifah, if one says 'upon me is walking to the Haram, the Sacred Mosque, or al-Safa and al-Marwah,' nothing is obligatory. But according to Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn), it does obligate Hajj or 'Umrah with walking, because 'the Haram' includes the Ka'bah and the Sacred Mosque, so mentioning them is like mentioning the Ka'bah itself. Al-Safa and al-Marwah are separate, so even the Sahibayn agree they do not obligate. The preferred view (al-mukhtar) is that walking is required from one's town. The text notes that this disagreement is a matter of time and era.

Key Terms
المشي (al-mashy): Walking on foot as opposed to riding; in this context, it is the mode of travel for Hajj or 'Umrah vowed in an oath.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath; here it refers to a conditional oath or vow expressed with phrases like 'علي المشي'.
الحرم (al-haram): The sacred precincts of Makkah, including the Ka'bah and the Sacred Mosque.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifah, if someone says 'علي المشي إلى الحرم', what is the ruling?
A) Hajj or 'Umrah becomes obligatory with walking
B) Nothing is obligatory
C) Only a sacrificial animal is required
D) It is considered a vow but not binding

Key Takeaway
The obligation to walk for Hajj depends on the wording: 'to the House of Allah' is agreed, but 'to the Haram' is disputed between Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn.

Breaking an Oath Not to Pray with One Rak'ah
Ruling: one rak'ah with prostration breaks an oath not to pray.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum act that breaks an oath not to pray according to the Hanafi school and explain why a single rak'ah with prostration suffices.

Core Idea
A single rak'ah with prostration breaks an oath not to pray — the essential actions complete the prayer.

This lesson clarifies a practical issue: if someone swears not to pray and then performs only one rak'ah, does the oath break? The Hanafi school rules that it does, because a rak'ah is the minimal unit containing the essential acts of prayer. We will explore the legal reasoning, including a comparison with fasting and an objection about the sitting (qad'a). By the end, you will understand the precise definition of 'prayer' in the context of oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
What act breaks the oath according to the text? The answer: one rak'ah with prostratration (sajdah). The commentator emphasizes that the rak'ah is completed by prostration itself.
Note the key phrase: 'completion is by prostration itself'.
2
Understand the reasoning
Why is a single rak'ah sufficient? Because prayer is a composite of different actions (qiyam, ruku, sujud). Until one performs them all, it is not called prayer. A rak'ah includes all these essential elements.
Compare with fasting: fasting is a single continuous act, so any part counts. Prayer is different.
3
Address the objection
Objection: The sitting (qad'a) is a pillar of prayer and is absent in a single rak'ah. Therefore, the complete reality of prayer is missing. The Hanafi response: the rak'ah is defined by its essential elements (standing, bowing, prostration); the sitting is not required for the minimal unit. The completion of the rak'ah is achieved by prostration.
This objection is hypothetical; the Hanafi position is clear.
4
Confirm the Hanafi position
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that one rak'ah with prostration breaks the oath. This is based on the understanding that prayer is a composite act and the rak'ah is its smallest valid unit.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is mentioned; all agree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤]قوله : بركعة ؛ تمامها بنفس السجود ؛ أي وضع الجبهة على الأرض من غير توقف على رفع الرأس والوجه فيه : إنّ الصلاة عبارة عن أفعال مختلفة، فما لم يأتِ بها لا تسمى صلاة ، بخلاف الصوم، فإنّه ركن واحد ويتكرّر بالجزء الثاني. فإن قلت : القعدة من أركان الصلاة ولا توجد في ركعة واحدة، فيجب أن لا يحنث بها لعدم وجود تمام حقيقتها.
His statement: 'by a rak'ah' — its completion is by prostration itself, i.e., placing the forehead on the ground without waiting to raise the head. The point is that prayer consists of different actions, and unless one performs them, it is not called prayer, unlike fasting, which is a single pillar and is repeated by the second part. If you say: The sitting (qad'a) is one of the pillars of prayer and it is not present in a single rak'ah, so he should not be guilty because the complete reality is absent.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on oaths regarding prayer. The author explains that if someone swears 'I will not pray' and then prays a single rak'ah (with prostration), the oath is broken. This is because a rak'ah is the minimal unit of prayer that includes the essential acts (standing, bowing, prostration). Unlike fasting, which is a single continuous act (refraining), prayer is composed of multiple distinct actions; performing a full rak'ah constitutes the act of praying. The commentator notes that the completion of a rak'ah is achieved by prostration (sajdah), not necessarily by the sitting (tashahhud). An objection is raised: the sitting is a pillar of prayer and not present in a single rak'ah (since the sitting is after the second rak'ah), so how can one rak'ah be valid? The answer is implied: the minimum for a valid prayer is one rak'ah with sajdah, though the sitting is not required for the definition of 'prayer' in the context of oaths. The Hanafi position is that a single rak'ah includes the essential elements and thus qualifies as prayer for oath purposes.

Key Terms
ركعة (rak'ah): A unit of prayer consisting of standing, bowing, and two prostrations. In this context, a single rak'ah includes prostration as its completion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the minimum act that breaks an oath not to pray?
A) One prostration only.
B) One rak'ah with its prostration.
C) Two rak'ahs with sitting.
D) The entire prayer including tasleem.

Key Takeaway
A single rak'ah with prostration is sufficient to break an oath not to pray in the Hanafi school.

One Rak'ah Breaks a General Oath Not to Pray
Ruling on breach of oath with one rak'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that one rak'ah breaks a general oath not to pray and explain why the sitting (qa'dah) is not required.

Core Idea
A single rak'ah is a valid prayer in oaths because the essential pillars exclude sitting.

When someone swears 'I will not pray' and then prays one rak'ah, does the oath break? Hanafis say yes, because a rak'ah contains the essential elements of prayer. This lesson unpacks the proof and the classification of pillars that leads to this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the act
Start with the phrase 'لا أصلي' (I will not pray) and the act of one rak'ah. Determine the legal question: does this act constitute prayer (salat) for oath purposes?
The term 'salat' in Shari'ah can refer to any valid portion.
2
Analyze the objection and response
The objection: sitting (qa'dah) is a pillar; one rak'ah has no sitting, so it cannot be prayer. The response: sitting is an additional pillar (ركن زائد), not essential. Only recitation, standing, bowing, and prostration are real pillars.
This distinction is key: hadd vs. fard.
3
Locate the Hanafi position and authority
The text cites 'al-Fath' (Ibn al-Humam) to support the view that one rak'ah suffices. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.
4
Note the thamarah (practical effect)
If a person swears 'I will not pray' and then merely stands, recites, bows, and prostrates once, the oath is broken. He does not need to perform a complete two-rak'ah prayer with sitting.
This applies only to general oaths; specific prayers are different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبركعة في لا يصلّى لا بما دونها 
فإن قلت القعدة من أركان الصلاة ولا توجد في ركعة واحدة، فيجب أن لا يحنث بها لعدم وجود تمام حقيقتها. قلت الأركان الحقيقية هي القراءة والقيام والركوع والسجود، والقعدة ركن زائد، وجب للختم فلا يعتبر في الحنث. كذا في «الفتح». 
ولو ضم صلاة فيشفع لا بأقل. 
وهل يتوقف حنثه على قعوده قدر التشهد بعد الركعتين اختلفوا فيه : فذكر في «العناية ) : أنه يتوقف عليه ؛ لأن الصلاة لا تعتبر شرعاً بدونه، وصلاة الركعتين عبارة عن صلاة تامة، وتمامها شرعاً لا يكون إلا بالقعدة. وذكر في «الفتح») : أن الأظهر أنه أن عقد يمينه على مجرد الفعل يحنث قبل القعدة لما مرّ من أنها ركن زائد، وإن عقدها على الفرض كصلاة الصبح أو ركعتي الفجر ينبغي أن لا يحنث حتى يقعد.
And by one rak'ah in 'I will not pray' he breaks, not by less. If you say: The sitting is among the pillars of prayer and is not found in a single rak'ah, so he should not break the oath due to the absence of its complete reality. I say: The real pillars are recitation, standing, bowing, and prostration, and the sitting is an additional pillar, required for completion, so it is not considered in breaking the oath. This is in 'al-Fath'. And if he adds 'prayer', he breaks with two rak'ahs, not less. And whether his breaking the oath depends on his sitting for the amount of tashahhud after the two rak'ahs is disputed: In 'al-Inayah' it is mentioned that it depends on it, because prayer is not considered legally without it, and a two-rak'ah prayer is a complete prayer, and its completion legally is only with the sitting. And in 'al-Fath' it is mentioned that the more apparent is that if he made his oath on the mere act, he breaks before the sitting, as it was stated that it is an additional pillar, and if he made it on the obligation like Fajr prayer or the two rak'ahs of Fajr, he should not break until he sits.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning oaths involving prayer. The first issue: If someone swears 'I will not pray' and then performs one rak'ah, the oath is broken. This is because the term 'prayer' (salat) in its Shari'i sense includes a single rak'ah (like witr), so the minimal act suffices. The objector argues that the sitting (qa'dah) is a pillar and missing in one rak'ah, but the commentator distinguishes: the essential pillars are recitation, standing, bowing, and prostration; the sitting is an additional pillar for completion and does not affect the existence of the prayer. Thus, one rak'ah is a valid prayer. The second issue: If the oath specifies 'صلاة' (as in 'I will not perform a prayer'), then the full prayer of at least two rak'ahs is required because the unqualified term 'salat' refers to the complete prayer. There is also a dispute about whether the sitting (qa'dah) after two rak'ahs is required to break the oath. According to 'al-Inayah', sitting is necessary for the prayer to be complete, so the oath is only broken after sitting. According to 'al-Fath', if the oath was on the mere act of praying, the one rak'ah already breaks it, but if the oath was on a specific prayer (e.g., Fajr), then sitting is required.

Key Terms
ركعة (rak'ah): A unit of prayer consisting of standing, bowing, and two prostrations.
قعدة (qa'dah): The sitting after the second rak'ah for tashahhud; considered an additional pillar for completion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the minimum number of rak'ahs that breaks a general oath 'I will not pray'?
A) One rak'ah
B) Two rak'ahs
C) Three rak'ahs
D) Four rak'ahs

Key Takeaway
A single rak'ah is sufficient to break a general oath not to pray because it contains the essential pillars.

When the Oath Specifies a Prayer: Two Rak'ahs and the Sitting Dispute
Ruling on oath specifying a prayer, and the sitting requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general and specified prayer oaths, and apply the intra-Hanafi ruling on the requirement of sitting for breaking such oaths.

Core Idea
Specified prayer oaths require a complete two-rak'ah prayer; sitting is required unless the oath is on the mere act.

If someone swears not to perform a specific prayer like Fajr, or uses the word 'صلاة' (salah) alone, the needed act changes. This lesson covers the rule that two rak'ahs are required and the dispute over whether sitting is necessary to break the oath.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between general and specified oaths
The first part of the passage dealt with 'لا أصلي' (general). Now it addresses 'ولو ضم صلاة' – if he adds the word 'prayer' (صلاة), meaning a specific prayer act. For specified oaths, two rak'ahs are required.
The word 'صلاة' is interpreted as a complete prayer (at least two rak'ahs).
2
Understand the sitting dispute positions
Two views are cited: 'al-Inayah' says sitting is necessary because a complete prayer legally requires it. 'al-Fath' says if the oath is on the mere act (عقد يمينه على مجرد الفعل), sitting is not required; but if on the specific obligation (كالفرض كصلاة الصبح), then sitting is needed.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The more apparent view (الأظهر) per 'al-Fath' is that if the oath is on the act itself, one rak'ah breaks it as before; but if on a specific prayer, the complete prayer with sitting is required.
This reconciles the two sources.
4
Apply to scenarios
Example: Swearing 'I will not pray the Fajr prayer' – must do two rak'ahs with sitting to break the oath. Swearing 'I will not engage in prayer' (general act) – one rak'ah suffices without sitting.
The intention (niyyah) matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage is a ruling with proof concerning oaths involving prayer. The first issue: If someone swears 'I will not pray' and then performs one rak'ah, the oath is broken. This is because the term 'prayer' (salat) in its Shari'i sense includes a single rak'ah (like witr), so the minimal act suffices. Key Terms
صلاة (salah): A complete prayer, typically of at least two rak'ahs, with obligatory recitations and actions.
قعدة (qa'dah): The sitting after the second rak'ah for tashahhud; considered an additional pillar for completion.
الحنث (al-hanath): The breach or violation of an oath.

The Minimum Prayer for an Oath: Two Rak'ahs Required
Ruling on oath including a prayer, requiring at least two rak'ahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum number of rak'ahs required to fulfill an oath to pray and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement of the final sitting (qa'dah).

Core Idea
An oath to pray is fulfilled by at least two rak'ahs; a single rak'ah is deficient.

When someone swears to pray, what exactly fulfills that oath? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies that the minimal complete prayer is two rak'ahs (a shaf'). It also explores an internal Hanafi debate about whether the final sitting (qa'dah) is necessary for the oath to be broken. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings on oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the minimum number of rak'ahs required to fulfill an oath to pray. The passage states it is a pair (shaf'), which is two rak'ahs.
2
Understand the reasoning
The word 'prayer' in the oath implies a complete prayer, and the complete prayer is at least two rak'ahs. A single rak'ah is considered a deficient prayer (batira').
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute on qa'dah
There is a difference of opinion on whether the final sitting (qa'dah) for tashahhud is required for the oath to be broken. Al-Inayah requires it; al-Fath says it depends on the intention of the oath.
The core of the dispute: is qa'dah an additional pillar (zā'id) or essential for completion?
4
Apply the distinction by intention
According to al-Fath (the azhar view), if the oath was on the mere act of praying, qa'dah is not required; if the oath was on a specific obligatory prayer (e.g., Fajr), then qa'dah is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ضم صلاة فيشفع لا بأقل. قلت : الأركان الحقيقية هي القراءة والقيام والركوع والسجود، والقعدة ركن زائد، وجب للختم فلا يعتبر في الحنث. كذا في «الفتح». 11) قوله فيشفع ; أي فيحنث بشفع، وأقله ركعتان لا بأقل منهما ; لأنه لما زاد [١] لفظ صلاة حمل على الكامل، والكامل من الصلاة هو الشفع، وما دون ذلك صلاة بتيراء، وهل يتوقف حنثه على قعوده قدر التشهد بعد الركعتين اختلفوا فيه : فذكر في «العناية ) : أنه يتوقف عليه ; لأن الصلاة لا تعتبر شرعاً بدونه (1) وصلاة الركعتين عبارة عن صلاة تامة، وتمامها شرعاً لا يكون إلا بالقعدة. وذكر في «الفتح») : أن الأظهر أنه أن عقد يمينه على مجرد الفعل يحنث قبل القعدة لما مرّ من أنها ركن زائد، وإن عقدها على الفرض كصلاة الصبح أو ركعتي الفجر ينبغي أن لا يحنث حتى يقعد.
And if he includes a prayer, then [he breaks the oath] with a pair, not less. I say: The true pillars are recitation, standing, bowing, and prostration, and the sitting is an additional pillar, obligatory for completion, so it is not considered in the breaking of the oath. As in al-Fath. 11) His saying 'with a pair' means he breaks the oath with a pair, the minimum of which is two rak'ahs, not less than that, because when he added the word 'prayer', it is taken to mean the complete prayer, and the complete prayer is a pair, and what is less than that is a deficient prayer. It is disputed whether his breaking the oath depends on his sitting for the amount of tashahhud after the two rak'ahs. In al-'Inayah it is mentioned that it does depend on it, because prayer is not considered legally without it. The prayer of two rak'ahs is an expression of a complete prayer, and its completion legally is only with the sitting. And in al-Fath it is mentioned that the more apparent is that if he made his oath on the mere action, he breaks the oath before the sitting because of what has passed that it is an additional pillar; and if he made it on the obligation like the morning prayer or the two rak'ahs of Fajr, he should not break the oath until he sits.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage deals with an oath where someone swears to include a prayer (e.g., 'I will pray'). The minimum that fulfills the oath is two rak'ahs (a pair), because the word 'prayer' implies a complete prayer, and the complete prayer is at least two rak'ahs. A single rak'ah is not considered a complete prayer. The author then discusses whether the sitting (qa'dah) for tashahhud is required for the oath to be broken. There is a difference of opinion: In al-Inayah, it is required because prayer is not valid without it. In al-Fath, the stronger view is that if the oath was on the mere act of praying, the sitting is not necessary because the sitting is an additional pillar not essential for the oath; but if the oath was on a specific obligatory prayer like Fajr, then the sitting is required. The Hanafi position: the ruling depends on intention.

Key Terms
شفع (shaf'): A pair, specifically two rak'ahs of prayer considered as a complete unit.
بتيراء (batira'): A deficient or incomplete prayer, here meaning less than two rak'ahs.
القعدة (al-qa'dah): The sitting posture during prayer for tashahhud, considered by some as an additional pillar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this text, what is the minimum number of rak'ahs that must be performed to fulfill an oath to pray?
A) One rak'ah
B) Two rak'ahs
C) Four rak'ahs
D) It depends on the intention

Key Takeaway
An oath to pray requires at least two rak'ahs; the requirement of the final sitting depends on the intention behind the oath.

Oath Not to Speak to a Sleeping Person: Condition of Waking
Ruling on an oath with condition of waking a sleeping person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when an oath conditional on waking a sleeping person breaks and explain why waking is required.

Core Idea
An oath to a sleeping person only breaks if the sleeper is woken.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya deals with a specific oath scenario: a person swears not to speak to someone if that someone speaks to him while sleeping, with the condition that he wakes the sleeper. The key question is whether the oath breaks immediately upon speech or only after waking. The Hanafi chosen view requires waking, because speech inherently requires hearing, and a sleeping person cannot hear without being alert.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
Focus on the conditional structure: 'If he speaks to me while sleeping, I will not speak to him, provided I wake him.' The condition of waking is explicit.
The ruling hinges on this condition.
2
Define speech in Hanafi law
Speech (takallum) is defined as making the listener hear. For a sleeping person, hearing is not actualized without waking.
This definition is key to the dispute.
3
Recognize the scholarly dispute
Many mashayikh did not require waking, arguing that the external act of speaking (voice reaching the ear) suffices. The chosen view (al-mukhtar) requires waking.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the chosen view
If the swearer wakes the sleeping person and then the sleeper speaks, the oath breaks. If the sleeper remains asleep, the oath does not break.
This is the mufta bihi position.
5
Understand the reasoning
Without waking, the sleeping person is like someone far away who cannot hear. The condition of waking ensures the listener is capable of hearing.
The example of a distant call clarifies the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحلف بالقول (وحنث في : حلف لا يكلّمُهُ إن كلّمه نائماً بشرط إيقاظه). قوله : بشرط إيقاظه ؛ أي ذلك النائم ، وكثير من المشايخ لم يشترطوا الإيقاظ ووجهـوه عـلـى مـا في «النهاية»: إن التكلّم عبارة عن استماعه كلامه كما في تكلمه نفسه، فإنه عبارة عن إسماع نفسه، إلا أن إسماع الغير أمر باطن لا يوقف عليه، فأقيم السبب المؤدي إليه مقامه. وهو أن يكون بحيث لو أصغى إليه أذنه، ولا يكون به مانع من السماع لسمع، ودار الحكم معه، وسقط اعتبار حقيقة الإسماع، والمختار أنه لو أيقظه حنث، وإلا لا ؛ لأنه إذا لم يتنبه كان كما إذا ناداه من بعيد وهو بحيث لا يسمع صوته.
Chapter of oath by speech. (And he breaks his oath in: he swore not to speak to him if he spoke to him while sleeping, with the condition of waking him). His statement: 'with the condition of waking him' means that sleeping person. Many of the mashayikh did not stipulate waking and explained it according to what is in al-Nihayah: that speaking is [defined as] him hearing his speech, just as when he speaks himself, it is [defined as] making himself hear. However, making another hear is an internal matter that cannot be known, so the cause leading to it is placed in its stead. That is, that he is such that if he inclined his ear to him, and there is no impediment to hearing, the ruling is attached to that, and the consideration of actual hearing falls away. The chosen view is that if he wakes him, he breaks his oath; otherwise he does not, because if he did not become alert, it is as if he called him from afar while he is such that he cannot hear his voice.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and commentary on an oath concerning a sleeping person. The ruling from the matn is that if someone swears, 'I will not speak to him if he speaks to me while sleeping,' and then the sleeping person does speak, the oath is only broken if the other person wakes the sleeping person first. The commentator notes that many earlier scholars did not require waking, as per al-Nihayah, arguing that 'speaking' fundamentally means being heard, and the cause of hearing is the speaker's voice reaching the listener. However, the internal act of hearing is hidden, so the law attaches to the external cause. But the chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that waking is necessary because if the sleeping person is not alert, it is like calling someone from afar who cannot hear. Thus, the Hanafi position is that waking is required for the oath to be broken.

Key Terms
إيقاظ (iqāẓ): Waking someone up; making a sleeping person alert.
تكلّم (takallum): Speaking; in this context, it means the act of uttering words that the listener hears.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen Hanafi view, when does an oath not to speak to a sleeping person break if the sleeping person speaks?
A) Immediately when the sleeping person speaks.
B) Only if the one who swore wakes the sleeping person and then the sleeper speaks.
C) Only if the sleeping person speaks while awake, regardless of waking.
D) The oath never breaks if the person is sleeping.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath conditional on waking a sleeping person only breaks if the sleeping person is woken, because speech requires hearing.
