Oath with Exception of Permission: Knowledge Requirement
Hanafi dispute on whether knowledge of permission is necessary for oath to break

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that knowledge of permission is necessary for an oath with exception to be broken and explain the linguistic dalil distinguishing idhn from rida.

Core Idea
An oath with exception of permission is only broken when the swearer knows of the permission; ignorance preserves the oath.

We take up a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: does an oath that includes the exception 'except with his permission' require the swearer's knowledge of that permission to be broken? The source text, from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, presents both the view of Abu Hanifa (that knowledge is required) and the view of Abu Yusuf (that it is analogous to consent, requiring no knowledge). Understanding the linguistic and logical distinction between permission and consent is key to mastering this ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the relied-upon Hanafi view is that the oath is not broken unless the swearer knows of the permission. The text says 'and we say', indicating this is the preferred position.
This is the view of Abu Hanifa.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Abu Yusuf argues by analogy to consent (rida): if someone consents without the swearer knowing, the oath is not broken by agreement, but permission should be the same.
He uses the case of 'except with his consent' as evidence.
3
Follow the opponent's reasoning
In the consent case, the oath is not broken even if the swearer does not know. Abu Yusuf extends this to permission.
The default assumption is that similar terms have similar rulings.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes permission from consent linguistically: 'idhn' derives from 'adhan' (informing) or from falling into the ear, so it inherently requires communication. Consent is based on mutual agreement and does not require knowledge.
Thus, without knowledge, it is not called permission.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the person permits but the swearer does not know, under Abu Hanifa the oath is not broken; under Abu Yusuf it is broken.
This highlights the importance of precise definitions in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وفي : إلا بإذنه) : أي وحنت في : حلف لا يكلمه إلا بإذنه. [٣]قوله : إلا بإذنه ؛ ولو قال : إلا برضاه فرضي المحلوف عليه بالاستثناء، ولم يعلمه الحالف لا يحنث اتفاقاً، واستشهد به أبو يوسف الله فيما نحن فيه، فقال لا يحنث في صورة الإذن أيضاً إذا لم يعلمه الحالف، ونحن نقول : الرضـا يـتـم بالتراضي ولا يتوقف وجوده على علم الغير، فلا يحنث بوجوده عَلِمَ أو لم يعلم. بخلاف الإذن فإنه مشتق من الأذان الذي بمعنى الإعلام، أو من الوقوع في الإذن، وكلّ ذلك لا يتحقق إلا بالسماع، وما في حكمـه فـمـا لـم يـصـل خـبر الإذن إلى الحالف لا يُسمّى ذلك إذناً. كذا في «النهاية» و«العناية». إن أذن ولم يعلم به فكلمه. وفي : لا يكلم صاحب هذا الثوب فباعه فكلمه. وفي : لا يكلّمُ هذا الشاب فكلمه شيخاً إن أذن ولم يعلم به فكلّمه ؛ لأنَّ الإذن إعلام، فإن أذن ولم يعلم، فهذا لا يكون إذنا، وعند أبي يوسف الله لا يحنث ؛ لأنَّ الإذن هو الإطلاق.
(And in: except with his permission): That is, he breaks his oath in: he swore not to speak to him except with his permission. His statement: 'except with his permission' – and if he said: except with his consent, and the person sworn about consented to the exception, but the swearer did not know of it, then he does not break his oath by agreement,. Abu Yusuf cited this as evidence in this issue, saying that he does not break his oath in the case of permission either if the swearer did not know. And we say: consent is completed by mutual agreement and its existence does not depend on the knowledge of another, so he does not break his oath by its existence whether he knew or not. Unlike permission, for it is derived from adhan which means informing, or from falling into the ear. All of that is not realized except through hearing or its equivalent. So unless the news of the permission reaches the swearer, it is not called permission. This is stated in al-Nihayah and al-Inayah. If he permitted and he did not know of it, then he spoke to him. And in: he swore not to speak to the owner of this garment, then he sold it and he spoke to him. And in: he swore not to speak to this young man, then he spoke to him as an old man. [Commentary on the first case]: 'and he did not know of it' – there is an indication that mere hearing is not sufficient; rather knowledge is necessary. Also, hearing is not obligatory for the occurrence of permission; rather it is sufficient to know by writing or gesture. [Commentary on second]: 'permission is release' i.e., authorization and permission, and it is completed by permission like consent, and the difference between them has passed.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding an oath that excepts permission. The matn states that if someone swears 'I will not speak to him except with his permission,' the oath breaks only if the swearer knows of the permission. Abu Yusuf argues that just as in the case of 'consent' (rida), where knowledge is not required, the same should apply to permission. The Hanafi response (attributed to Abu Hanifa and his school) distinguishes: consent is a matter of mutual agreement independent of the other's knowledge, whereas permission is derived from 'adhan' (announcement) and requires that the information reaches the swearer. Thus, if the person permits but the swearer does not know, it is not considered permission. The commentator adds that knowledge is required, not just hearing, and that writing or gestures suffice. This shows the Hanafi emphasis on the semantic root of legal terms.

Key Terms
إذن (idhn): Permission; a legal authorization that requires the recipient to be informed.
رضاء (riḍā): Consent; agreement without the necessity of the other party's knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position adopted by the author, if a man swears not to speak to Zayd except with his permission, and Zayd permits but the man does not know, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because permission was given.
B) No, because knowledge of permission is necessary.
C) Yes, following Abu Yusuf.
D) No, because the oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, permission (idhn) requires the recipient's knowledge; without it, the oath with exception is not broken.

Oath Tied to a Changing Descriptor: The Rule of Laghw
Ruling on oath tied to a descriptor that changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath that mentions a descriptive attribute and explain why the oath binds the person, not the attribute, when the attribute is not the motive.

Core Idea
If a descriptor in an oath is not the motive, it is laghw – the oath attaches to the person, not the property.

This passage from Al-Wiqaya explains a critical principle in Hanafi oath law: when someone swears not to speak to 'the owner of this garment', and the owner sells the garment, the oath is still broken if he speaks to that person. Why? Because the descriptor 'owner of this garment' is considered irrelevant (laghw) if it is not the motive for the oath. The oath thus attaches to the person's essence, not to the temporary attribute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
Consider a person who swears, 'I will not speak to the owner of this garment.' The owner later sells the garment to someone else, and the swearer speaks to the original owner.
The key is that the descriptor 'owner of this garment' has changed due to the sale.
2
Understand the legal analysis
The Hanafi jurists reason that the phrase 'owner of this garment' is merely for identification (ta'rif), not the motive (ba'ith) for the oath. Since a person does not become an enemy solely because he owns a particular garment, the description is considered laghw (irrelevant).
Laghw means the description has no effect on the ruling.
3
Derive the ruling
Therefore, the oath attaches to the person of the original owner. Speaking to him breaks the oath. But speaking to the new owner (the buyer) does not break the oath, even though he now owns the garment.
This is because the oath was on the person, not on the attribute.
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
The same ruling applies to any similar descriptor like 'owner of this house', 'owner of this place', etc. The principle is general.
If the descriptor is the actual motive (e.g., 'I will not speak to the person who sells alcohol'), it may be different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وفي : لا يكلم صاحب هذا الثوب فباعَه فكلمه) [٣] قوله : وفي لا يكلم صاحب هذا الثوب... الخ ؛ يعني لو قال : لا أكلم صاحب هذا الثوب فباع ذلك الرجل ذلك الثوب ، وكلّمه الحالف بعد ذلك حنث ؛ لأن الإضافة للتعريف، والوصف لغو إذا لم يكن باعثاً على اليمين كما مرّ. ومن المعلوم أن الإنسان لا يعادى بمعنى كونه مالكاً لثوب خاص، فصار كأنه قال : لا أكلم هذا الرجل، فتعلقت يمينه بذاته، ولذا لو كلّم المشتري لذلك الثوب لم يحنث، وإن صار صاحب ذلك الثوب، ومثله صاحب هذه الدار، وصاحب هذا المكان، ونحو ذلك.
(And in: he swore not to speak to the owner of this garment, then he sold it and he spoke to him) His statement: 'and in: he swore not to speak to the owner of this garment...' – meaning if he said: 'I will not speak to the owner of this garment,' then that man sold that garment, and the swearer spoke to him after that, he breaks his oath; because the addition is for identification, and the description is irrelevant if it is not the motive for the oath, as passed. It is known that a person is not hostile merely because he is the owner of a particular garment, so it is as if he said: 'I will not speak to this man,' so his oath is attached to his person. Therefore, if he spoke to the buyer of that garment, he does not break his oath, even though he became the owner of that garment. The same applies to the owner of this house, the owner of this place, and the like.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage explains a ruling on an oath tied to a changing descriptor. If someone swears, 'I will not speak to the owner of this garment,' and the owner later sells the garment, the oath is broken if he speaks to that person. Why? Because the phrase 'owner of this garment' is merely descriptive and not the cause of the oath. Since one does not take an oath because of mere ownership, it is as if he swore not to speak to that individual. The oath attaches to the person, not the property. Therefore, if he speaks to the new owner (the buyer), he does not break his oath. This principle applies to any similar description like 'owner of this house' etc. The commentator notes this is because the description is 'laghw' (irrelevant) if it is not the motive.

Key Terms
لغو (laghw): Irrelevant; a description that is not the motive for the oath and thus does not affect the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears, 'I will not speak to the owner of this house,' and the owner sells the house, which of the following would break his oath?
A) Speaking to the new owner.
B) Speaking to the original owner.
C) Both A and B.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath law, a descriptor that is not the motive for the oath is considered laghw, and the oath binds to the person, not the attribute.

8 min
Oath Not to Speak to a Young Man Who Becomes Old
Ruling on oaths with age descriptions that change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi ruling for an oath tied to an age description that changes, and identify when the description is considered laghw (irrelevant) versus dā'iyah (motive).

Core Idea
An oath breaks when the described attribute changes unless the attribute was the motive for the oath.

When someone swears an oath that includes a description of the subject—like 'young man' or 'lamb'—and that description later changes, the default ruling is that the oath is broken. This lesson examines the Hanafi principle that such descriptions are 'laghw' (irrelevant) unless they were the dā'iyah (motive) for the oath. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings on broken oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its description
Read the oath statement and note the descriptive term attached to the object of the oath (e.g., 'this young man', 'this boy', 'this lamb').
The description can be age, size, or any changeable attribute.
2
Assess whether the description was the motive (dā'iyah) or merely incidental (laghw)
Determine if the swearer's reason for the oath was specifically tied to that description. Was it the cause for the oath, or just a casual identifier?
In the text, the default is that descriptions in present time are laghw unless proven otherwise.
3
Apply the default rule
If the description is laghw, the oath is broken when the person or thing changes to no longer fit that description. The oath attaches to the essence, not the attribute.
Examples: a young man becomes old → oath breaks; a lamb becomes a ram → oath breaks.
4
Consider the exception
If the swearer had a specific intent tied to the description, such as avoiding a boy's bad manners or a lamb's poor meat, then the description becomes the dā'iyah, and the ruling may differ.
The text mentions this exception but does not fully elaborate.
5
Note the court's default assumption
In the absence of evidence of a specific motive, the default ruling is that the description is laghw and the oath is broken when the attribute changes.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وفي : لا يكلّمُ هذا الشاب فكلمه شيخاً) [٤] قوله : وفي لا يكلّم ؛ يعني إذا حلف لا يكلّم هذا الشاب فكلّم ذلك الرجل زمان شيخوخته حنث، و وكذا في لا يُكلّم هذا الصبي، فكلمـه بعـدمـا صـار شـاباً أو شيخاً، حنث لما مر من أن الوصف في الحاضر لغو إذا لم يكن داعياً إلى اليمين. ونحوه: الحلف بقوله : لا يأكل من لحم هذا الحَمَل – وهو بفتحتين ولد الشاة فأكله بعدما صار كبشاً حنث ؛ لأن هذه الصفات غير داعية إلى اليمين، فإن وصف الصبا أو الشباب لا دخل له في امتناع تكلّمه ، وكذا وصف الصغر في الحمل. وهذا كله إذا لم ينو الحالف شيئاً ، فإن كان حالفاً عرف عدم طيب لحم الجمل ما دام حَمَلاً ، فحلف بعدم أكله من حيث كونه مضراً أو عرف من الصبي سوء أدب
(And in: he swore not to speak to this young man, then he spoke to him as an old man) His statement: 'and in: he swore not to speak' – meaning if he swore not to speak to this young man, then he spoke to that man in his old age, he breaks his oath. Similarly, if he swore not to speak to this boy, then he spoke to him after he became a young man or old, he breaks his oath, because the description of the present is irrelevant if it is not a motive for the oath. And similar to it: the oath by saying: 'I will not eat from the meat of this lamb' – it is a young sheep – and he ate it after it became a ram, he breaks his oath; because these descriptions are not motives for the oath. For the description of childhood or youth has nothing to do with his refraining from speaking, and similarly the description of smallness in the lamb. All this is if the swearer did not intend anything specific. But if he was swearing knowing the meat of the lamb is not good while it is a lamb, and he swore not to eat it because it is harmful, or he knew the boy's bad manners...
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage addresses oaths that use age-related descriptions. If someone swears 'I will not speak to this young man,' and later the young man becomes an old man, speaking to him breaks the oath. Likewise, swearing about a 'boy' who becomes a young man or old. Why? Because the description of youth or childhood is not the motive for the oath; it is merely a present descriptor. Since the swearer's aversion is to the person, not the age, the oath attaches to the person's essence. The same applies to a lamb: if one swears not to eat its meat, and it grows into a ram, eating it breaks the oath. However, the text adds a condition: this applies unless the swearer had a specific intent, such as knowing the meat is bad only while small, or the person's bad manners are tied to youth. In such cases, the description may be the cause, and the ruling might differ. But by default, the description is considered irrelevant.

Key Terms
لغو (laghw): Irrelevant description; one that is not the motive for the oath.
داعية (dā'iyah): Motive or cause that prompted the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the default Hanafi ruling if a man swears not to speak to 'this boy' and later speaks to him after he becomes a young man?
A) He does not break his oath because the person is no longer a boy.
B) He breaks his oath because the description is not the motive.
C) He breaks his oath only if he intended the boyhood state.
D) The oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
An oath tied to a changeable attribute breaks when the attribute changes unless the attribute was the reason for the oath.

Descriptions in Oaths: When Not the Motivating Factor
Explaining rulings on oaths involving descriptions like 'owner' or 'young'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a description (waṣf) in an oath is legally irrelevant (laghw) if it is not the motivator (bā‘ith), and distinguish between permission requiring knowledge (Abu Hanifa) vs absolute permission (Abu Yusuf).

Core Idea
A description in an oath that is not the motivator is legally irrelevant—the oath attaches to the essence.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya expands on the matn of al-Wiqaya regarding oaths containing descriptions such as 'owner of this garment' or 'young man'. The central ruling is that when a description is not the reason for the oath, it is considered laghw (irrelevant), and the oath applies to the person's essence. We will also examine an intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on whether permission (idhn) requires knowledge or is absolute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is taking an oath with a description (e.g., 'I will not speak to the owner of this garment'). The ruling: if the description is not the motivator, it is laghw; breaking the oath triggers penalty. If the description is the motivator, the oath is restricted accordingly.
The ruling applies to all similar descriptions like 'young', 'old', 'owner'.
2
Locate the dalil for the description ruling
The passage states that the addition (idāfa) in 'owner of this garment' is for identification (taʿrīf), and the description is laghw if not a motivator. This is based on the principle that the oath attaches to the essence (dhāt) of the person.
The proof is linguistic reasoning: people are not opposed due to ownership.
3
Examine the idhn dispute
The passage discusses permission (idhn) in the context of oaths. Abu Hanifa holds that idhn requires knowledge because it is iʿlām (informing); mere hearing without understanding is not permission. Abu Yusuf holds that idhn is iṭlāq (absolute).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
For idhn: the linguistic meaning of idhn as informing (iʿlām) yields the ruling that knowledge is necessary. For descriptions: the fact that a person is not opposed due to ownership shows ownership is irrelevant; thus the description is laghw.
The proof for the description ruling also relies on the absence of the description as motivator.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
In the idhn dispute: if someone permits in a language the other does not understand, according to Abu Hanifa the oath is not broken; according to Abu Yusuf it is broken. The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's.
The practical consequence depends on whether knowledge is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن أذن ولم يعلم به فكلمه. وفي : لا يكلم صاحب هذا الثوب فباعه فكلمه. وفي : لا يكلّمُ هذا الشاب فكلمه شيخاً إن أذن ولم يعلم به فكلّمه ؛ لأنَّ الإذن إعلام، فإن أذن ولم يعلم، فهذا لا يكون إذنا، وعند أبي يوسف الله لا يحنث ؛ لأنَّ الإذن هو الإطلاق. (وفي : لا يكلم صاحب هذا النوب" فباعَه فكلمه. وفي : لا يكلم هذا الشاب فكلمه شيخاً) [١]قوله : ولم يعلم به ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أنه لا يكتفي بمجرد السماع، بل لا بد معه من العلم حتى لو خاطبه بلغة لا يفهمها لا يعتبر ذلك، وإلى أن السماع ليس بواجب في حصول الإذن، بل يكفي العلم به ولو بالكتابة أو الإشارة. [٢] قوله : هو الإطلاق ؛ أي إجازة وإباحه، وهو يتم بالإذن كالرضاء، وقد مرّ الفرق بينهما. [٣] قوله : وفي لا يكلم صاحب هذا الثوب... الخ ؛ يعني لو قال : لا أكلم صاحب هذا الثوب فباع ذلك الرجل ذلك الثوب، وكلّمه الحالف بعد ذلك حنث ؛ لأن الإضافة للتعريف، والوصف لغو إذا لم يكن باعثاً على اليمين كما مرّ. ومن المعلوم أن الإنسان لا يعادى بمعنى كونه مالكاً لثوب خاص، فصار كأنه قال : لا أكلم هذا الرجل، فتعلقت يمينه بذاته، ولذا لو كلّم المشتري لذلك الثوب لم يحنث، وإن صار صاحب ذلك الثوب، ومثله صاحب هذه الدار، وصاحب هذا المكان، ونحو ذلك. [٤] قوله : وفي لا يكلّم ؛ يعني إذا حلف لا يكلّم هذا الشاب فكلّم ذلك الرجل زمان شيخوخته حنث، وكذا في لا يُكلّم هذا الصبي، فكلمـه بعـدمـا صـار شـاباً أو شيخاً، حنث لما مر من أن الوصف في الحاضر لغو إذا لم يكن داعياً إلى اليمين. ونحوه: الحلف بقوله : لا يأكل من لحم هذا الحَمَل – وهو بفتحتين ولد الشاة فأكله بعدما صار كبشاً حنث ؛ لأن هذه الصفات غير داعية إلى اليمين، فإن وصف الصبا أو الشباب لا دخل له في امتناع تكلّمه ، وكذا وصف الصغر في الحمل. وهذا كله إذا لم ينو الحالف شيئاً ، فإن كان حالفاً عرف عدم طيب لحم الجمل ما دام حَمَلاً ، فحلف بعدم أكله من حيث كونه مضراً أو عرف من الصبي سوء أدب الكلام معه ، أو عَرَفَ أن الكلام مع الشاب ما دام شاباً يضر في دينه أو دنياه فامتنع منه تحمل هذه الأيمان على مدة بقاء تلك الأوصاف، كذا حققه في «الفتح» (۲)
If he permitted and he did not know of it, then he spoke to him. And in [the book]: 'He does not speak to the owner of this garment', so he sold it, and he spoke to him. And in [the book]: 'He does not speak to this young man', so he spoke to him when he was an old man. If he permitted and he did not know of it, then he spoke to him: because permission is informing. If he permitted and he did not know, then this is not permission. According to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, he does not break his oath, because permission is absolute permission. (And in [the book]: 'He does not speak to the owner of this garment', so he sold it, and he spoke to him. And in [the book]: 'He does not speak to this young man', so he spoke to him when he was an old man.) [1] His saying: 'and he did not know of it' – there is an indication that mere hearing is not sufficient; rather knowledge is necessary, so if he addressed him in a language he does not understand, it is not considered. And also that hearing is not obligatory for the occurrence of permission; rather knowledge is sufficient, even if by writing or gesture. [2] His saying: 'it is absolute permission' – i.e., permission and authorization. It is completed by permission like consent, and the difference between them has passed. [3] His saying: 'And in [the book]: He does not speak to the owner of this garment... etc.' – meaning, if he said, 'I will not speak to the owner of this garment,' then that man sold that garment, and the oath-taker spoke to him after that, he breaks his oath because the addition is for definition, and the description is irrelevant if it was not a motivator for the oath, as has passed. And it is known that a person is not opposed by virtue of being the owner of a specific garment, so it is as if he said, 'I will not speak to this man,' so his oath is attached to his essence. Therefore, if he spoke to the buyer of that garment, he does not break his oath, even though he became the owner of that garment. Similarly, the owner of this house, the owner of this place, etc. [4] His saying: 'And in [the book]: He does not speak' – meaning, if he swore he would not speak to this young man, and he spoke to that man when he was old, he breaks his oath. Likewise, in 'he does not speak to this boy,' and he spoke to him after he became a young man or an old man, he breaks his oath, due to what has passed that the description in a present [person] is irrelevant if it was not a motivator for the oath. And similar: swearing by saying: 'He does not eat from the meat of this lamb' – and it is the offspring of a sheep, with two fatha [vowels] – and he ate it after it became a ram, he breaks his oath, because these descriptions are not motivators for the oath. Indeed, the description of youth or boyhood has no bearing on his refraining from speaking, and likewise the description of smallness in the lamb. All this if the oath-taker did not intend anything. If he was swearing, knowing that the meat of the lamb is not good as long as it is a lamb, so he swore not to eat it due to being harmful, or he knew from the boy bad manners in speech with him, or he knew that speaking with the young man as long as he is a young man harms him in his religion or his worldly affairs, so he refrained from it, then these oaths are interpreted for the duration of the existence of those descriptions. Thus verified in 'al-Fath' (2).
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is an expansion of the matn of al-Wiqaya regarding oaths. It addresses cases where the oath contains a description (such as 'owner of this garment' or 'this young man'). The central ruling: if the description is not the motivating factor (bā‘ith) for the oath, it is considered irrelevant (laghw), and the oath attaches to the essence (dhāt) of the person or thing. For example, if one swears not to speak to 'the owner of this garment' and the owner sells it, speaking to him still breaks the oath because the description 'owner' was only for identification, not motivation. Similarly, if one swears not to speak to 'this young man' and later speaks to him when he is old, the oath is broken unless the youthfulness was the reason for the oath (e.g., harm in speaking to a young man). The passage also discusses the nature of permission (idhn): it requires knowledge; mere hearing is insufficient. Abu Yusuf holds that permission is absolute (iṭlāq) and does not require knowledge. The final part notes that if the oath-taker's motivation is known, the oath is interpreted according to that motivation, as verified in al-Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
وصف (waṣf): A description or attribute attached to the subject of the oath, e.g., 'owner', 'young'.
باعث (bā‘ith): The motivating factor or reason that prompted the oath.
إذن (idhn): Permission; in this context, permission given to the oath-taker to speak.
لغو (laghw): Irrelevant or inconsequential; in oaths, a description that does not affect the ruling because it is not the motivating factor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not speak to the owner of this garment,' and then the owner sells the garment, does speaking to the previous owner break the oath?
A) Yes, because the description 'owner' is irrelevant if not the motivating factor.
B) No, because he is no longer the owner.
C) Yes, only if the oath-taker knew of the sale.
D) No, because the oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath law, descriptions that are not the motivator are irrelevant; the oath applies to the essence unless the intent indicates otherwise.

Conditional Emancipation upon Sale or Purchase with Khiyar
Ruling on conditional emancipation with khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional emancipation when the condition involves a sale or purchase with khiyar al-shart, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding ownership during the option period.

Core Idea
Even with khiyar, the sale or purchase itself triggers emancipation.

This lesson examines a nuanced case in conditional oaths: a person says 'If I sell my slave, he is free' or 'If I buy a slave, he is free,' and then makes the transaction with khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation). Does the condition count as fulfilled immediately, or is emancipation delayed until the option expires? The Hanafi school addresses this by analyzing how khiyar affects ownership transfer. We will explore the positions of Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, and the underlying principle that a description that is not the motivator of the oath is disregarded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and action
The condition is either 'sale' or 'purchase'. The action is making that transaction with khiyar al-shart. Determine what exactly triggers the emancipation in the speaker's intent.
The condition is the contract itself, not the transfer of ownership.
2
Understand the role of khiyar in ownership
Khiyar al-shart gives the right to cancel the contract within a specified period. Hanafis debate whether it prevents ownership from leaving the seller (during seller's option) or from entering the buyer (during buyer's option). This debate directly impacts whether the emancipation condition is met.
Abu Hanifa holds that khiyar for the seller prevents exit; the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that khiyar for the buyer does not prevent entry.
3
Trace the two scenarios: sale with khiyar and purchase with khiyar
For sale: Abu Hanifa rules that the slave remains in the seller's ownership, so the condition of sale occurs while the owner still owns the slave, freeing him. For purchase: the two rule that the slave enters the buyer's ownership immediately, freeing him; Abu Hanifa also frees him but based on the purchase act itself, not ownership transfer.
The outcome is the same in both scenarios: emancipation occurs.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference and the principle of motivator
The disagreement is about the reasoning. The commentary reiterates a principle: if a description (like 'with option') is not the motivator of the oath, it is irrelevant. Here, the motivator is the sale or purchase itself, not the option. Therefore, the condition is fulfilled regardless of khiyar.
This principle helps in other similar oaths.
5
Apply the ruling to concrete examples
Practice with examples: If a seller says 'If I sell him, he is free' and sells with seller's option, the slave is freed immediately. If a buyer says 'If I buy him, he is free' and buys with buyer's option, the slave is freed immediately (per the two) or upon purchase (per Abu Hanifa).
If the sale is binding (no option), emancipation does not occur because ownership is lost entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب الحلف بالقول [۲] وفي : هذا حر إن بعثه أو اشتريته إن عقد بالخيار. لأنَّ الوصف المذكور لا يصلح مانعاً من التكلم فيرادُ الذات. [۲] ۱۲۱ ( وفي : هذا حرّ إن بعثه أو اشتريته إن عقد بالخيار ) : أي إذا قال : إن بعته فهو حرّ، فباعَهُ على أنه بالخيار يعتق ؛ لأنه لم يخرج عن ملكه ، وقد وُجِدَ الشَّرط ، وهو البيع ، ولو قال : إن اشتريته ، فهو حر فشراه على أنه بالخيار عُتِق. أما على أصلهما ؛ فلأنه دخل في ملك المشتري. وأما على أصل أبي حنيفة له ، فلأنه علق العتق بالشراء ، فكأنه قال بعد الشراء بالخيار، فهو حر فيعتق. [١]قوله : لا يصلح مانعاً... الخ ؛ أشار به إلى ضابطة مر ذكرها غير مرة : وهي أن الوصف إذا كان باعثاً على اليمين كان معتبراً ، وإذا لم يكن باعثاً صار لغواً. [٢] قوله : إن عقد ؛ أي باع أو اشترى بالخيار، وأما إذا عقد عقداً تاماً ، ففي صورة البيع لا يعتق لزوال العبد عن ملكه بمجرد البيع ولا عتق فيما لا يملك ابن آدم بنص الحديث، وفي صورة الشراء يعتق لوجود الشراء والملك، كذا في «العناية»، وغيرها [٣]قوله : بالخيار ؛ أي خيار الشرط ثلاثة أيام فما دونها، وحكمه أنه يمنع خروج المبيع إن كان الخيار للبائع اتفاقاً، وإن كان للمشري لا يمنع خروجه عن ملك البائع اتفاقا، وهل يمنع دخوله في ملك المشتري، فيه خلاف، فعنده يمنع، وعندهما: لا، وستطلع على تفصيل هذا البحث في موضعه من كتاب البيوع» إن شاء الله تعالى. [٤]قوله : لم يخرج عن ملكه ؛ بناء على أن خيار البائع يمنع خروجه عن ملكه. [0]قوله : فلأنه علق العتق ... الخ ؛ حاصلة أنه علّق العتق بالشراء حيث قال : إن (1) أما إن باعه بيعاً لازماً أو باطلاً لم يعتق، أما في البات فلأنه كما تم البيع زال الملك والجزاء لا ينزل في غير الملك. ينظر : فتح باب العناية»(۲ : ۲۸۷).
The Book of Oaths, Chapter on Swearing by Speech [2] And in [the book]: 'This one is free if I sold him or bought him, if he made a contract with option.' Because the mentioned description is not suitable as a preventer of speech, so the essence is intended. [2] 121 (And in [the book]: 'This one is free if I sold him or bought him, if he made a contract with option') – i.e., if he said: 'If I sell him, he is free,' and he sold him on the basis that he has the option, he becomes free, because he has not left his ownership, and the condition (which is sale) has occurred. And if he said: 'If I buy him, he is free,' and he bought him on the basis that he has the option, he becomes free. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): because he entered the ownership of the buyer. And according to the principle of Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him: because he suspended emancipation upon purchase, so it is as if he said after purchase with option, he is free, so he becomes free. [1] His saying: 'not suitable as a preventer' etc. – he indicated by it a principle mentioned many times: that the description, if it is a motivator for the oath, is considered, and if it is not a motivator, it becomes irrelevant. [2] His saying: 'if he made a contract' – i.e., he sold or bought with option. If he made a complete contract (without option), then in the case of sale, he does not become free because the slave leaves his ownership by the mere sale, and there is no emancipation in what one does not own, by the text of the hadith. In the case of purchase, he becomes free because sale and ownership exist. Thus in al-'Inaya and others. [3] His saying: 'with option' – i.e., option of stipulation for three days or less. Its ruling is that it prevents the exit of the sold item from the seller's ownership if the option is for the seller, by agreement. If the option is for the buyer, it does not prevent its exit from the seller's ownership, by agreement. But whether it prevents its entry into the buyer's ownership, there is disagreement: according to him (Abu Hanifa), it prevents; according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it does not. You will see the detail of this research in its place from the Book of Sales, God willing. [4] His saying: 'has not left his ownership' – based on the fact that the option of the seller prevents its exit from his ownership. [0] His saying: 'because he suspended emancipation' etc. – its summary: he suspended emancipation upon purchase where he said: ... (1) If he sold it with a binding sale or an invalid sale, he does not become free. In the case of a binding sale, because once the sale is completed, ownership is removed, and the consequence (emancipation) does not occur in non-ownership. See: Fath Bab al-'Inaya (2:287).
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses the case of conditional emancipation in sales with khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation). It clarifies that if someone says 'If I sell him, he is free' or 'If I buy him, he is free' and then sells or buys with khiyar, the slave is emancipated upon the occurrence of the sale or purchase. For sale with khiyar: according to Abu Hanifa, the slave does not leave the seller's ownership during the option period; hence, the condition of sale has occurred while still owned, so the slave is freed. For purchase with khiyar: according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the slave enters the buyer's ownership immediately (even with khiyar), so upon purchase the condition is fulfilled and the slave is freed. Abu Hanifa argues that the emancipation is suspended upon the purchase itself, not upon ownership; thus, the purchase condition is met and the slave is freed. The commentary also reiterates the principle that a description (like 'with option') is irrelevant if not the motivator. The footnote adds that if the sale is binding (without option) or invalid, emancipation does not occur in the case of sale because ownership is lost; in purchase, it occurs because both purchase and ownership exist.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyār al-sharṭ): Option of stipulation: the right to cancel a sale within three days (or specified period).
تعليق العتق (ta‘līq al-‘itq): Suspending emancipation on a condition, e.g., 'if I sell him, he is free'.
أصل (aṣl): Principle or basis of a ruling; here referring to the differing principles of Abu Hanifa and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave is freed immediately because the sale condition is met and the slave hasn't left ownership.
B) The slave is not freed until the option period ends.
C) The slave is freed only if the buyer exercises the option.
D) The slave is not freed because the sale is incomplete.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a conditional emancipation on sale or purchase takes effect even when the contract includes khiyar al-shart, because the condition is the contract itself, not ownership transfer.

Swearing Not to Speak: Religious Utterances Do Not Break the Oath
Stating that reciting Quran or dhikr is not considered 'speaking' in custom and shariah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between linguistic speech (kalam) and customary speech (mutakallim urfan) to determine when an oath not to speak is broken.

Core Idea
Custom (urf) exempts religious utterances from breaking an oath not to speak.

When someone swears not to speak and then recites Quran, glorifies God, or makes dhikr, does the oath break? Linguistically these are speech, but Hanafi law looks to custom. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains why such acts are not considered 'speaking' and therefore do not break the oath, whether performed in prayer or outside it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The swearer recites Quran, tasbih, tahlil, takbir, etc. The ruling: the oath is not broken (la yahanth).
The passage lists: qira'a, tasbih, tahlil, takbir, hawqala, hamdala.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The proof is from shariah: the Prophet (PBUH) forbade speaking (kalam) in prayer but permitted Quran recitation and dhikr. This shows that in the legal view, these are not 'kalam'.
Hadiths: 'We were forbidden to speak in prayer...' while recitation is allowed.
3
Analyse the custom (urf) argument
Linguistically, these acts are kalam, but custom defines 'mutakallim' as one who addresses people in conversational manner. Religious utterances are not seen as addressing people, so custom exempts them.
The text: 'al-mutakallim urfan man yukhatibu al-nas wa yatakallamu bima yukhatibu bihi al-nas.'
4
Apply the ruling to context
The ruling applies whether the utterances are in prayer or outside prayer. The same custom-based logic holds in both settings.
The text explicitly says: 'fi al-salat aw kharijaha'.
5
Note cross-madhhab difference
Other schools may consider any utterance as speech breaking the oath. Hanafi relies on custom (urf) to narrow the meaning of 'speaking' in oaths.
This is mukhtalaf fih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في : لا يتكلم فقرأ القرآن، أو سبح، أو هلل ، أو كبر في الصلاة، أو خارجها
(ولا في : لا يتكلم فقرأ القرآن أو سبح، أو هلل ، أو كبر في الصلاة) ، أو خارجها) : هذا عندنا فإنه لا يسمى متكلماً عرفاً وشرعاً.
(۱) قوله : ولا في لا يتكلّم... الخ ؛ يعني إذا حلف لا يتكلّم فقرأ القرآن أو الأذكار المأثورة كالأدعية وغيرها، أو صلى على النبي الله أو سبح - أي قال سبحان الله ونحوه - ، أو هلل - أي قال : لا اله إلا الله ونحوه - ، أو كبر - أي قال الله أكبر ونحوه - ، أو حوقل - أي قال : لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله - ، أو حمـدل – أي قال : الحمد لله ونحوه - ، لا يحنث ؛ لأن هذه وإن كانت من الكلام لغة، لكن لا يسمى بمثل ذلك متكلّماً عرفاً، فان المتكلّم عُرفاً من يخاطب الناس ، ويتكلم بما يخاطب به الناس. [٢] قوله : في الصلاة ؛ متعلق بكل من الأفعال المذكورة : أي سواء كانت هذه الأفعال – أي قراءة القرآن والتسبيح وغيرهما في الصلاة أو خارج الصلاة - [٣]قوله : وشرعاً ؛ بدليل أنه نهى في الأحاديث عن التكلم في الصلاة، وأبيحت هذه الأمور فيها ، وأبيحت هذه الأمور في مواقع كره فيها الكلام كحالة الوضوء ونحوها.
And not in the case of: 'He swears not to speak, then recites Quran, or glorifies God, or says 'there is no god but Allah', or says 'God is greater' in prayer or outside it. (And not in the case of: 'He swears not to speak, then recites Quran or glorifies God, or says 'there is no god but Allah', or says 'God is greater' in prayer) or outside it: According to us, he is not called 'speaker' by custom and by law. (1) His saying: 'And not in the case of 'He swears not to speak'... etc.' means if he swears not to speak and then recites Quran or the prescribed remembrances like supplications and others, or sends blessings upon the Prophet, or glorifies God (i.e., says 'Glory be to God' and similar), or says 'there is no god but Allah' (i.e., 'La ilaha illallah' and similar), or says 'God is greater' (i.e., 'Allahu Akbar' and similar), or says 'There is no power and no strength except with God' (i.e., 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah'), or says 'Praise be to God' (i.e., 'Alhamdulillah' and similar), he does not break his oath, because although these are speech linguistically, one is not called a 'speaker' by custom for such things. For the 'speaker' by custom is one who addresses people and speaks with what people address each other. [2] His saying: 'in prayer' is connected to each of the mentioned actions, meaning whether these actions (reciting Quran, glorifying, etc.) are in prayer or outside prayer. [3] His saying: 'and by law' as evidence that he forbade speaking in prayer in hadiths, and permitted these matters in it, and these matters are permitted in places where speaking is disliked, like during wudu and similar.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling that if someone swears not to speak, and then recites Quran, glorifies God, or performs other religious utterances, the oath is not broken. The key reasoning is based on custom (urf) and shariah. Linguistically, these are forms of speech (kalam), but in common custom, 'speaking' (takallum) is understood as addressing people with ordinary conversation, not reciting divine words or remembrance. Therefore, the swearer is not considered a 'speaker' in the customary sense. The passage also clarifies that this ruling applies whether these utterances are made inside prayer or outside it. The shariah proof is that the Prophet Muhammad (PBUH) forbade speaking in prayer (ordinary talk) but permitted reciting Quran, tasbih, tahmid, etc., in prayer. Similarly, in other contexts where speech is discouraged (e.g., during wudu), these religious utterances are allowed. Thus, the oath is not broken.

Key Terms
كلام (kalam): Speech; linguistically includes any utterance, but customarily refers to conversational speech.
عرف (urf): Customary usage; defines what is considered 'speaking' in the context of oaths.
متكلم (mutakallim): A speaker; in this context, one who engages in conversational speech addressing people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, why does reciting Quran not break an oath not to speak?
A) Because reciting Quran is not considered speech linguistically.
B) Because custom (urf) defines 'speaker' as one who addresses people, not one who recites divine words.
C) Because the oath only applies to speaking in prayer.
D) Because reciting Quran is obligatory and overrides the oath.

Key Takeaway
Reciting Quran and dhikr does not break an oath not to speak because custom and shariah do not classify these as 'speaking'.

The Cause vs. Condition: Intention in Suspended Emancipation
Ruling with proof from usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that intention must accompany the cause (illa) rather than the condition (shart) in suspended acts, and distinguish between the rulings of Abu Hanifa and the two companions on the cause of emancipation.

Core Idea
Intention must accompany the cause, not the condition. The oath is the cause; purchase is only a condition.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: a man swears 'If I buy this slave, he is free,' then buys him intending to fulfill a kaffarah oath. Does the emancipation count for kaffarah? The passage from Al-Hidayah and its commentary in 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explain that it does not, because the cause of emancipation is the oath, not the purchase. The intention accompanied the purchase (the condition), not the oath (the cause). We will unpack the usul principles behind this ruling, including the effect of ta'liq on causality, and note the intra-madhhab difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Read the matn: a man says 'If I buy this slave, he is free.' He later buys him with the intention of fulfilling a kaffarah oath. Determine whether the emancipation counts for kaffarah according to the Hanafi school.
The passage clearly states it does not count.
2
Distinguish cause from condition
The commentator explains that the cause (illa) of emancipation is the oath (al-yamin), not the purchase. The purchase is a condition (shart) for the emancipation to occur. Intention must accompany the cause, not the condition.
Hanafi usul: shart is external to the cause; intention at the condition does not suffice.
3
Analyze the objection from ta'liq
An objector argues that according to Hanafi usul, ta'liq (suspending) prevents causality until the condition occurs. When the condition occurs, the suspended statement becomes the cause. So when the purchase happens, the suspended 'he is free' becomes the cause, and intention at purchase should accompany it. Understand this objection.
This is based on the ta'liq rule: 'if you enter, you are divorced' – the divorce occurs at entry, and the statement becomes the cause then.
4
Resolve the objection: essence vs. description of causality
The response from Ibn Kamal Pasha distinguishes between the essence of the cause (dhat al-illa) and the description of causality (wasf al-illiyyah). What matters is that intention accompanies the essence, not the descriptive causality. Here the essence is the oath, which is prior to the purchase. Therefore intention at purchase does not accompany the essence.
The example of capacity (ahliyyah) at the time of suspension (ta'liq) vs. condition supports this.
5
Note the intra-madhhab difference
Abu Hanifa holds the cause is the oath. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if the purchased slave is a relative, the cause is kinship; but if not, they agree the cause is the oath. In all cases, the ruling is the same: kaffarah not fulfilled.
The difference is theoretical; no practical consequence in this scenario.

عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
لا بشراء عبد حلف بعتقه
وعندهما لا حيث جعلا القرابة علة.
(1)
(لا بشراء" عبد حلف بعتقه) : أي قال : إن اشتريت هذا العبد فهو حرّ، فشراه بنية الكفارة لا تسقط الكفارة ؛ لأن علة العتق اليمين، والشراء شرط له، فلا تكون النية مقارنة للعلة. يرد عليه " : أنه قد ذُكِرَ في أصول الفقة أنّ التعليق عندنا يمنعُ العِليَّة، فإذا وُجِدَ الشَّرطُ يصيرُ المعلّق علّة حينئذ، فتكونُ النِيَّةُ مقارنةً لعلّة العتق ) ١ ] قوله : علّة ؛ فإنّ الملك عندهما شرط ، والنيّة ليست مقارنة إلا بالشرط لا بالعلة، فلا تفيد في الإجزاء عن الكفّارة. [٢]قوله : لا بشراء ؛ أي لا تسقط الكفّارة بشراء عبد عَلَّقَ عتقه بالشراء قبله، فإنّ علة العتق في هذه الصورة هي الحلف السابق، والشرط هو الشراء، والنية إنما قارنت الشراء، فلا تفيد في إجزاء الكفّارة ؛ لأنّ الشرط فيه تقارن النية مع : (۳) قوله : يرد عليه...الخ ؛ حاصلُ الإيرادِ إنه قد ثبت في أصول الفقه أن التعليق عندنا يمنع ! العلية، يعني كون الجزاء علّة لحكمه مثلاً في قولنا : إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ، يمنع التعليق كون قوله : أنت طالق علّةً لوقوع الطلاق، فلولا التعليق لكان : أنت طالق علّة له، ووقع الطلاق، فالتعليقُ بالشرط منعه من كونه علّة، وعند وجود الشرط يصير الجزاء علة، فيكون قوله : أنت طالق علّة لوقوع الطلاق عند دخول الدار. إذا تقرر هذا فنقول : لَمّا علّق العتق بالشراء وقال : إن اشتريته فهو حر لم يكن اليمين علة، بل منع هو كون هو حرّ علّة لعتقه، وإنّما ينعقد علة عند وجود الشرط ، وهو الشراء ونيّة الكفّارة مقارنةً للشراء، فتكون مقارنة للعلة من حيث علّته، نعم لو
علة العتق.
ثبت أنه يجب أن تكون النية مقارنةً لذات العلة أيضاً اندفع الإشكال.
(1) أجاب عن هذا الإيراد ابن كمال باشا في «الإيضاح» (ق١/٧٥) : بأنه ذكر في الأصول أيضاً أن المعتبر مقارنة النية لذات العلة لا لوصف العلية ولذلك شرطوا الأهلية حال التعليق لا حال وجود الشرط الذي هو زمان حدوث العلية ، واللازم من منع التعليق العلية قبل وجود الشرط مقارنة النية للعلية لا مقارنتها لذات العلة.
He said: 'By the purchase of a slave he swore to free him' means: he said, 'If I buy this slave, he is free,' so he bought him with the intention of kaffarah; the kaffarah is not fulfilled because the cause of emancipation is the oath, and the purchase is a condition for it, so the intention does not accompany the cause. It is objected: It has been mentioned in usul al-fiqh that suspending prevents causality according to us; when the condition is found, the suspended becomes a cause at that time, so the intention accompanies the cause of emancipation. [1] He said: 'Cause' — because ownership is a condition according to the two, and the intention accompanies only the condition, not the cause, so it does not avail for fulfilling the kaffarah. [2] He said: 'Not by purchase' means the kaffarah is not fulfilled by purchasing a slave whose emancipation he suspended on purchase beforehand; indeed the cause of emancipation in this case is the prior oath, and the condition is the purchase, and the intention only accompanied the purchase, so it does not avail for fulfilling the kaffarah because the condition requires the accompaniment of intention with... (3) He said: 'It is objected...' the gist of the objection: it is established in usul al-fiqh that suspending prevents causality with us, meaning that the consequence being a cause for its ruling, for example in our saying 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' suspending prevents his saying 'you are divorced' from being a cause for the occurrence of divorce; were it not for the suspending, his saying 'you are divorced' would be a cause for it, and divorce would occur; so suspending with a condition prevented it from being a cause, and at the occurrence of the condition the consequence becomes a cause, so his saying 'you are divorced' becomes a cause for the occurrence of divorce upon entering the house. If this is settled, we say: When he suspended emancipation on purchase and said 'If I buy him, he is free,' the oath was not a cause; rather, it prevented 'he is free' from being a cause for his emancipation; it only becomes a cause upon the occurrence of the condition, which is the purchase, and the intention of kaffarah accompanies the purchase, so it accompanies the cause in terms of its causality. Indeed, if it were established that the intention must accompany the essence of the cause, the difficulty would be resolved. (1) Ibn Kamal Pasha responded to this objection in al-Idah by stating that it is also mentioned in usul that what is considered is the accompaniment of the intention to the essence of the cause, not to the description of causality; therefore they required capacity at the time of suspension, not at the time of the occurrence of the condition which is the time of the occurrence of causality; and what follows from the prevention of causality before the occurrence of the condition is the accompaniment of the intention to causality, not its accompaniment to the essence of the cause.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from usul al-fiqh. The matn states that buying a slave after swearing to free him upon purchase does not fulfill the kaffarah oath. The commentator explains that the cause (illa) of emancipation is the oath, and the purchase is only a condition (shart). Since the intention (niyyah) is required to accompany the cause, and it only accompanies the purchase (the condition), the kaffarah is not discharged. An objection is raised based on the principle that suspending (ta'liq) prevents causality; when the condition occurs, the suspended part becomes the cause. So the intention at the time of purchase should accompany the cause. The response distinguishes between the essence of the cause (dhat al-illa) and the description of causality (wasf al-illiyyah). The intention must accompany the essence, not the causality. The example of divorce illustrates this. Ibn Kamal Pasha further clarifies that capacity (ahliyyah) is required at the time of the oath, not at the occurrence of the condition. The first ruling (Abu Hanifa's) is that it does not fulfill kaffarah; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) also agree on the ruling but differ in the cause: they consider kinship as the cause. However, the passage focuses on the general case of a non-relative slave.

Key Terms
علة ('illa): The effective cause that brings about the ruling; here, the oath is the cause of emancipation.
شرط (shart): A condition that is necessary for the existence of the ruling but is not the cause; here, purchase is the condition for the emancipation to occur.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending a ruling upon the occurrence of a future condition; here, the emancipation is suspended on purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the cause (illa) of emancipation in the scenario where a man says 'If I buy this slave, he is free' and then buys him?
A) The purchase
B) The oath
C) The ownership
D) The intention

Key Takeaway
In suspended acts, intention must accompany the cause (the original oath) and not merely the condition (the purchase); otherwise, the act does not fulfill the obligation.

Freeing an Um Walad for Kaffarah
Ruling on freeing um walad for kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a man swears to free his um walad (by marriage) upon purchase and explain why it does not count for kaffarah.

Core Idea
Freeing an um walad by purchase does not fulfill kaffarah because her freedom was already due.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a man makes an oath that if he buys his um walad (a slave woman who has borne him a child through marriage), she will be free for his kaffarah. Upon purchase, she indeed becomes free, but the kaffarah remains unfulfilled. Why? Because her right to freedom already existed through istilad. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that an act assigned to an oath must be a fresh act of emancipation, not a mere confirmation of an already existing right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the characters and oath
The man has an um walad by marriage. He says, 'If I buy you, you are free for my kaffarah.' Note that she is not his property, but he has a right to her through marriage.
Um walad by marriage: she is owned by another, but has borne him a child.
2
Determine the legal consequence when condition met
He buys her. She becomes free. This is because the condition (buying) is fulfilled, and the oath triggers freedom.
3
Analyze why it does not count for kaffarah
The freeing does not count for the kaffarah because she already had a right to freedom through istilad (bearing a child). The Hanafi principle: the act must be a new emancipation, not a confirmation of an existing right.
The freeing is effective but not sufficient for expiation.
4
Compare with the case of a close relative
Contrast with buying a close relative (e.g., a son) who is fully enslaved: freeing him would count for kaffarah because there is no pre-existing right to freedom.
The difference is the completeness of the slave's status.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان اليمين في العتق والطلاق
ومستولدة بنكاح عُلّق عتقها عن كفارته بشرائها، وتعتق بإن تسريت أمةً ومستولدةٍ بنكاح عُلّق عتقها عن كفارته بشرائها : قوله : ومستولدة بالجر عطف على عبد: أي ولا بشراء مستولدة.
وصورتها : أن يقول لأمة استولدَها بالنكاح إن اشتريتك فأنت حرة عن كفارة يميني، فاشتراها تعتق لوجود الشرط، ولا يجزئه عن الكفارة؛ لأن حريتها مستحقة بالاستيلاد، ولا يضاف إلى اليمين من كل وجه.
وتعتق بإن تسريت أمة
والجواب عنه على ما أشار إليه في «الكافي»: إن ذات العلة إنما هي: هو حر، لكن لا تظهر عليه عند التعليق، بل عند وجود الشرط، فعند وجود الشرط يضاف الحكم إلى العلة السابقة، فصار كأنه علة من قبل ولايته عند ذلك، ومن هاهنا صرحوا بأنه يضمن عند الرجوع شهود التعليق دون شهود وجود الشرط، وصرحوا أيضاً باشتراط الأهلية عند اليمين لا عند الشرط.
[1] قوله : ومستولدة; على صيغة اسم المفعول من الاستيلاد; أي التي جعلت أم ولد بنكاح، بأن نكح أمة غيره وولدت منه ولداً فصارت أم ولده بالنكاح، وإنما لا يكون عتقها مجزئاً عن الكفارة لنقصان رقها، فإنها استحقت العتق بالاستيلاد. ولهذا لا يجزئ إعتاقها عن الكفارة منجزاً أيضاً، فشراؤه بعدما علق عتقها بشرائها يكون إعتاقاً من وجه دون وجه، فلا يجزئ عن الكفارة بخلاف شراء القريب، فإن الرق فيه كامل، وهو إعتاق من كل وجه؛ لأنه لم يثبت له قبل الشراء إعتاق من وجه. كذا في «الفتح». [2] قوله : بإن تسريت أمة; معنى التسري - بتشديد الراء المهملة – على وزن التفعل: اتخاذ الأمة سرية، وهو - بضم السين المهملة، وتشديد الراء المهملة – على وزن فعلية، منسوب إلى السر - بكسر السين المهملة وتشديد الراء – بمعنى الإخفاء، وسمى الجماع سراً للزوم الإخفاء به.
وإنما ضمت سين السرية; لأن الأبنية قد تتغير في النسبة، كما يقال للمعمر دهري - بضم الدال المهملة -، نسبة إلى الدهر - بفتح الدال – بمعنى الزمان. كذا في «فتح القدير»(٥ : ١٧٢).
فهي حرة من تسراها وهي ملكه يوم حلف، لا من شراها فتسراها، وبكل مملوك لي حر أمهات أولاده، ومدبروه، وعبيده لا مكاتبوه إلا بنيتهم فهي حرة من تسراها وهي ملكه يوم حلف، لا من شراها فتسراها); لأن هذه الأمة لم تكن في ملكه زمان الحلف، ولم يضف عنقها إلى الملك أو سببه، وفيه خلاف زفر.
وبكل مملوك لي حر أمهات أولاده، ومدبروه، وعبيده لا مكاتبوه إلا بنيتهم); لأنه لا يملكهم يدا.
«العناية».
وحاصل المسألة: إنه إذا قال: إن تسريت أمةً فهي حرة، تعتق منها من اتخذها سرية، وكانت مملوكة له عند الحلف لا من اشتراها بعد الحلف فتسراها. [1] قوله : من تسراها; فاعل لقوله: «تعتق»، ولما كان من عبارة: أمة، عن أنث الفعل المسند إليه، ووجه عتقها: إن الحلف انعقد في حقها لمصادفته الملك; وذلك لأن الأمة منكرة في الشرط، فيتناول كل جارية على الانفراد. [2] قوله : شراها; ذكر الشراء اتفاقي; فإن الحكم كذلك فيمن ملكها بعد الحلف بغير الشراء من أسباب الملك. [3] قوله : خلاف زفره; فإنه يقول: التسري لا يصح إلا في الملك، فكأن ذكره ذكر الملك، فصار كما إذا قال لأجنبية: إن طلقتك فعبدي حر، يصير التزوج مذكوراً. ولنا: إن الملك يصير مذكوراً ضرورة صحة التسري، وهو شرط فيتقدر بقدره، فلا يظهر في حق صحة الجزاء، وهو الحرية، وفي مسألة الطلاق إنما يظهر في حق الشرط دون الجزاء، حتى لو قال: إن طلقتك فأنت طالق ثلاثاً، فتزوجها أو طلقها لا تطلق ثلاثاً، فهذه وزان مسألتنا. كذا في «الهداية».
(4) قوله : أمهات أولاده; فإن الملك في أم الولد والمدبر والعبد والأمة كامل، وإن كان الرق في أم الولد والمدبر ناقصاً لاستحقاقهما العتق بخلاف المكاتب; فإن الملك فيه ناقص; لأنه مملوك رقبة لا يداً لاستقلاله بالتصرف فلا يدخل في إطلاق قوله: مملوك
Book of Oaths: The oath about freeing slaves and divorce. And an um walad by marriage whose freedom is suspended for his expiation upon buying her, and she is freed by 'if I take a concubine' ... (full translation not shown for brevity)
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on rulings concerning oaths involving freeing slaves. It first discusses the case of an um walad (a female slave who has borne her master a child) who is owned by another but the man has married her. If he says, 'If I buy you, you are free for the expiation of my oath,' and then buys her, she becomes free but it does not count for the expiation because her freedom was already due through istilad (bearing a child). The commentary explains that her status is incomplete, unlike buying a relative who is fully enslaved. Then it discusses the case of taking a concubine (tasarrī): if one swears, 'If I take a concubine, she is free,' the woman who becomes free is the one he already owned at the time of the oath, not one he buys later. This is because the oath only applies to those in his possession. The text also covers that saying 'every slave of mine is free' includes ummahat awlad, mudabbarun, and slaves, but not mukatabun unless intended, since mukatabun are not fully owned (they are owned in body but not in hand due to their autonomy). The commentary adds a disagreement with Zufar, who analogizes to a divorce case. The Hanafi response is that ownership is implied only to the extent necessary for the validity of tasarrī, and does not extend to the consequence (freedom).

Key Terms
مستولدة (mustaulada): A female slave who has become an um walad (mother of her master's child) through marriage (not by ownership).
استيلاد (istilad): The act of bearing a child to the master, which gives the slave mother a right to freedom upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his um walad by marriage, 'If I buy you, you are free for my kaffarah.' He buys her. Which is correct?
A) She becomes free and it counts for his kaffarah.
B) She becomes free but it does not count for his kaffarah.
C) She does not become free because the oath is invalid.
D) She becomes free only if he also frees her after purchase.

Key Takeaway
An um walad by marriage freed by purchase after an oath does not count for kaffarah because her freedom was already due through istilad.

Oaths Involving Concubines (Tasarrī) and the Dispute with Zufar
Ruling on concubine oaths and dispute with Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath to free a concubine only applies to women owned at the time of the oath, and evaluate Zufar's opposing analogy.

Core Idea
An oath conditional on taking a concubine does not extend to future acquisitions; ownership implied only for the act, not the consequence.

This lesson focuses on a disagreement within the Hanafi school (with Zufar) regarding the scope of an oath tied to tasarrī. The Hanafi position is that the oath only covers women already in possession at the time of swearing. Zufar analogizes to divorce, arguing that since tasarrī requires ownership, mentioning tasarrī implies ownership for all future acquisitions. The Hanafi response draws a careful distinction: ownership is implied only for the validity of the act, not for the consequence (freedom).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
If a man says 'If I take a concubine (tasarrī), she is free,' the woman freed is the one he owned at the time of the oath, not one acquired later.
The condition 'if I take a concubine' refers to an existing slave, not to the act of buying.
2
Identify the opponent and his view
Zufar holds that the oath includes future acquisitions because taking a concubine is only valid with ownership, so mentioning tasarrī implicitly includes ownership.
Zufar uses a qiyas from the case of divorce.
3
Follow Zufar's proof
Zufar argues: Just as saying 'If I marry you' to a foreign woman implies the marriage, and if it occurs, the divorce condition triggers, similarly mentioning tasarrī implies ownership and the condition triggers.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis reply: In the divorce analogy, ownership is implied only to the extent necessary for the validity of the act (marriage), not for the consequence (divorce). Here, ownership is implied only for tasarrī to be valid, not for the freedom. So future acquisitions are not covered.
This is a subtle but critical distinction.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: According to Zufar, if a man swears this and later buys a slave and takes her as concubine, she goes free. According to the Hanafis, she does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان اليمين في العتق والطلاق
ومستولدة بنكاح عُلّق عتقها عن كفارته بشرائها، وتعتق بإن تسريت أمةً ومستولدةٍ بنكاح عُلّق عتقها عن كفارته بشرائها : قوله : ومستولدة بالجر عطف على عبد: أي ولا بشراء مستولدة.
وصورتها : أن يقول لأمة استولدَها بالنكاح إن اشتريتك فأنت حرة عن كفارة يميني، فاشتراها تعتق لوجود الشرط، ولا يجزئه عن الكفارة؛ لأن حريتها مستحقة بالاستيلاد، ولا يضاف إلى اليمين من كل وجه.
وتعتق بإن تسريت أمة
والجواب عنه على ما أشار إليه في «الكافي»: إن ذات العلة إنما هي: هو حر، لكن لا تظهر عليه عند التعليق، بل عند وجود الشرط، فعند وجود الشرط يضاف الحكم إلى العلة السابقة، فصار كأنه علة من قبل ولايته عند ذلك، ومن هاهنا صرحوا بأنه يضمن عند الرجوع شهود التعليق دون شهود وجود الشرط، وصرحوا أيضاً باشتراط الأهلية عند اليمين لا عند الشرط.
[1] قوله : ومستولدة; على صيغة اسم المفعول من الاستيلاد; أي التي جعلت أم ولد بنكاح، بأن نكح أمة غيره وولدت منه ولداً فصارت أم ولده بالنكاح، وإنما لا يكون عتقها مجزئاً عن الكفارة لنقصان رقها، فإنها استحقت العتق بالاستيلاد. ولهذا لا يجزئ إعتاقها عن الكفارة منجزاً أيضاً، فشراؤه بعدما علق عتقها بشرائها يكون إعتاقاً من وجه دون وجه، فلا يجزئ عن الكفارة بخلاف شراء القريب، فإن الرق فيه كامل، وهو إعتاق من كل وجه؛ لأنه لم يثبت له قبل الشراء إعتاق من وجه. كذا في «الفتح». [2] قوله : بإن تسريت أمة; معنى التسري - بتشديد الراء المهملة – على وزن التفعل: اتخاذ الأمة سرية، وهو - بضم السين المهملة، وتشديد الراء المهملة – على وزن فعلية، منسوب إلى السر - بكسر السين المهملة وتشديد الراء – بمعنى الإخفاء، وسمى الجماع سراً للزوم الإخفاء به.
وإنما ضمت سين السرية; لأن الأبنية قد تتغير في النسبة، كما يقال للمعمر دهري - بضم الدال المهملة -، نسبة إلى الدهر - بفتح الدال – بمعنى الزمان. كذا في «فتح القدير»(٥ : ١٧٢).
فهي حرة من تسراها وهي ملكه يوم حلف، لا من شراها فتسراها، وبكل مملوك لي حر أمهات أولاده، ومدبروه، وعبيده لا مكاتبوه إلا بنيتهم فهي حرة من تسراها وهي ملكه يوم حلف، لا من شراها فتسراها); لأن هذه الأمة لم تكن في ملكه زمان الحلف، ولم يضف عنقها إلى الملك أو سببه، وفيه خلاف زفر.
وبكل مملوك لي حر أمهات أولاده، ومدبروه، وعبيده لا مكاتبوه إلا بنيتهم); لأنه لا يملكهم يدا.
«العناية».
وحاصل المسألة: إنه إذا قال: إن تسريت أمةً فهي حرة، تعتق منها من اتخذها سرية، وكانت مملوكة له عند الحلف لا من اشتراها بعد الحلف فتسراها. [1] قوله : من تسراها; فاعل لقوله: «تعتق»، ولما كان من عبارة: أمة، عن أنث الفعل المسند إليه، ووجه عتقها: إن الحلف انعقد في حقها لمصادفته الملك; وذلك لأن الأمة منكرة في الشرط، فيتناول كل جارية على الانفراد. [2] قوله : شراها; ذكر الشراء اتفاقي; فإن الحكم كذلك فيمن ملكها بعد الحلف بغير الشراء من أسباب الملك. [3] قوله : خلاف زفره; فإنه يقول: التسري لا يصح إلا في الملك، فكأن ذكره ذكر الملك، فصار كما إذا قال لأجنبية: إن طلقتك فعبدي حر، يصير التزوج مذكوراً. ولنا: إن الملك يصير مذكوراً ضرورة صحة التسري، وهو شرط فيتقدر بقدره، فلا يظهر في حق صحة الجزاء، وهو الحرية، وفي مسألة الطلاق إنما يظهر في حق الشرط دون الجزاء، حتى لو قال: إن طلقتك فأنت طالق ثلاثاً، فتزوجها أو طلقها لا تطلق ثلاثاً، فهذه وزان مسألتنا. كذا في «الهداية».
(4) قوله : أمهات أولاده; فإن الملك في أم الولد والمدبر والعبد والأمة كامل، وإن كان الرق في أم الولد والمدبر ناقصاً لاستحقاقهما العتق بخلاف المكاتب; فإن الملك فيه ناقص; لأنه مملوك رقبة لا يداً لاستقلاله بالتصرف فلا يدخل في إطلاق قوله: مملوك
Book of Oaths: The oath about freeing slaves and divorce. And an um walad by marriage whose freedom is suspended for his expiation upon buying her, and she is freed by 'if I take a concubine' ... (full translation not shown for brevity)

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on rulings concerning oaths involving freeing slaves. It first discusses the case of an um walad (a female slave who has borne her master a child) who is owned by another but the man has married her. If he says, 'If I buy you, you are free for the expiation of my oath,' and then buys her, she becomes free but it does not count for the expiation because her freedom was already due through istilad (bearing a child). The commentary explains that her status is incomplete, unlike buying a relative who is fully enslaved. Then it discusses the case of taking a concubine (tasarrī): if one swears, 'If I take a concubine, she is free,' the woman who becomes free is the one he already owned at the time of the oath, not one he buys later. This is because the oath only applies to those in his possession. The text also covers that saying 'every slave of mine is free' includes ummahat awlad, mudabbarun, and slaves, but not mukatabun unless intended, since mukatabun are not fully owned (they are owned in body but not in hand due to their autonomy). The commentary adds a disagreement with Zufar, who analogizes to a divorce case. The Hanafi response is that ownership is implied only to the extent necessary for the validity of tasarrī, and does not extend to the consequence (freedom).

Key Terms
تسري (tasarrī): Taking a female slave as a concubine for sexual relations.
زفر (Zufar): Abu al-Qasim Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a prominent early Hanafi jurist and student of Abu Hanifa.

A) Only the slave owned at the time of the oath.
B) Only the slave bought later.
C) Both slaves.

Key Takeaway
An oath conditional on taking a concubine only frees the slave owned at the time of swearing, not one acquired later.

Inclusion of Slave Categories in General Oaths of Manumission
Ruling on inclusion of slave categories in general oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which slaves are included in a general oath of freeing 'every slave of mine' and explain the exception for mukatabun.

Core Idea
Mukatabun are owned in body but not in hand, so they are excluded from general oaths unless specifically intended.

This lesson clarifies the scope of a general oath of emancipation. When a man swears 'Every slave of mine is free,' the ruling includes ummahat awlad, mudabbarun, and regular slaves, but not mukatabun. The reason is that mukatabun are owned only in body (raqaba) and not in hand (yad), meaning they have significant autonomy. The exception is if the man specifically intends to include them.

Guided Path
1
List the categories mentioned
The passage mentions: ummahat awlad (slave mothers of his children), mudabbarun (slaves whose freedom is deferred after his death), normal slaves (abeed), and then excludes mukatabun (slaves under contract of manumission).
2
Understand the basis of inclusion
Ummahat awlad, mudabbarun, and regular slaves are fully owned in both body and hand (or have a complete right to freedom eventually). Mukatabun are owned in body but not in hand because they have the right to work and manage their own affairs.
The distinction is 'milk al-raqaba wa al-yad' vs. 'milk al-raqaba faqat'.
3
Apply the rule to the oath
When the man says 'every slave of mine,' it applies to those he fully owns. Mukatabun are not included unless he explicitly intends them, because they are not fully under his control.
The exception is noted: 'illa biniyyatihim'.
4
Identify the practical consequence
If a man has a mukatab and says 'all my slaves are free,' the mukatab is not freed unless he specifically intended it. If he did not intend, the mukatab remains under contract.

Source Text

The Legal Implication of Lam in Oath Formulas
Explaining the legal implication of the letter lam in oath formulas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules to determine whether the lam in an oath indicates specification of the action to the addressee or ownership of the object, and state the condition for the oath to be broken.

Core Idea
When lam follows a delegable verb, it specifies the action; when attached to a non-delegable verb or an object, it indicates ownership.

This lesson explores a cornerstone of Hanafi oath jurisprudence: the interpretation of the letter lam (ل) in formulas like 'if I sell a garment for you (laka)...' The ruling determines whether the swearer's oath is broken and thus whether emancipation or divorce takes effect. We will dissect the two possibilities—specification of the action versus ownership of the object—and learn how the nature of the verb determines which applies, resolving an intra-Hanafi difference on the role of command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verb type
Determine if the verb in the oath admits proxy (niyabah) — i.e., can be delegated to another person. Verbs like selling, buying, hitting a slave admit proxy; eating, drinking, hitting a child do not.
This is the primary determinant.
2
Locate the lam's attachment
Is the lam attached to the verb (e.g., 'laka' as indirect object) or directly to a noun (object of the verb)?
If attached to an object directly, ownership rule applies.
3
Apply the rule
If lam is attached to a verb that admits proxy, it indicates ikhtisas (specification of the action to the addressee). If attached to a non-delegable verb or an object, it indicates milk (ownership of the object by the addressee).
This rule saves the lam from being meaningless.
4
Determine the breaking condition
For ikhtisas, the oath is broken only if the action is done by the swearer's command or with his knowledge/intention that it is for the addressee. For milk, the oath is broken whenever the swearer performs the action on the object owned by the addressee, regardless of command.
The mufta bihi position: specific command not required; intention suffices.
5
Check the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Zahiriyyah required a direct command; al-Nahr and al-Bahr hold that intention or knowledge is enough. The relied-upon view is that intention suffices, as stated in al-Bahr: 'This is something that must be memorized.'
Know both views but follow the mufta bihi.
