Book of Oaths: The Oath concerning Emancipation and Divorce. His ownership or not. [2] 145 His ownership or not: He meant by its entering upon an action that is connected to it, so in his statement: 'If I sell a garment for you' [4] then his slave is free. So the lam is connected to the sale, and it necessitates the specification of the sale to the addressee, and the action is not specified to other than the doer except by command: i.e., agency. And for this reason the command necessitated it. The author of al-Zahiriyyah: If he ordered him to buy a garment for his young son, he does not break the oath. And in al-Nahr: The implication of the directive meaning that it is for specification, he breaks the oath if the purchase is for his sake. Do you not see that if he ordered him to sell the property of another, it necessitates his breaking of the oath, not restricted to it being for him. [1] His statement: 'his ownership or not' i.e., whether that garment is owned by the addressee or not owned by him. The reason for that is that the one swearing did not make the garment specific to the addressee, since he did not say 'a garment for you' or 'your garment', but rather made the sale specific to him, so it necessitates his specification to him, not the specification of the garment to him. [2] His statement: 'He meant by its entering upon an action that is connected to it'; it indicates that the intended meaning is not the apparent one of its entering upon an action, for it is inconceivable that the lam enters upon it, nor is it for connection to it; the intended meaning is its connection to it. [3] His statement: 'so the lam'; thus in the copies, and better, rather the correct thing is to delete the fa. [4] His statement: 'specification of the sale' to the addressee; for the lam is for specification, for it connects what it is attached to, which is the action, to what it enters upon, which is the second-person pronoun, so it conveys that the addressee is specified with the action, and his being specified with it conveys that no advantage is taken of his performance of the action except from his direction, and that is by his command. And if he sold by his command, his sale was for his sake, and it is the lam of purpose, so the thing sworn against becomes that he does not sell it for his sake. So if the addressee smuggled his garment without his knowledge and sold it, he did not sell it for his sake, because that is only conceivable with knowledge of his command. And it follows from this that it only applies to actions that admit proxy. Thus in al-Fath. [0] His statement: 'except by command'; he said in Sharh Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir: If he said to Zayd: 'If I sell a garment for you, my slave is free', and he has no intention, and Zayd gave a garment to a man to hand it to the swearer to sell it, and he handed it and said: 'Sell it for me', and the swearer did not know that it was Zayd's garment, he does not break the oath, because the lam in 'I sold for you' indicates specification of the action to Zayd, and that is only by the command of the swearer, or by his knowledge that he sold it for his sake, whether the garment belonged to Zayd or to someone else. End. And this conveys that specific command is not a condition, rather it is sufficient for his breaking the oath that he intended the sale for his sake, whether by his command or not. He said in al-Bahr: This is something that must be memorized, for the apparent meaning of their statements here contradicts that, but together it is the ruling. [1] His statement: 'upon an entity'; the intended meaning is the essence by the context of its comparison with action. The gist of the intention: The action is either that which admits proxy or not, and in either case, the entry of the lam is either upon the action or upon its object, which is the entity. If it enters upon an action that admits proxy for another, it necessitates specification of the action to the addressee as mentioned before. And if it enters upon an action that does not admit proxy, such that agency does not apply to it, like eating and drinking and the like, or it enters upon the entity absolutely, whether the action is from the first category or the second, like 'If I sell a garment for you' and 'If I eat food for you', it necessitates ownership of the entity for the addressee, whether the action is by his command or not. [2] His statement: 'and hitting the child'; as for hitting a slave, it admits proxy, so it is from the first category; hence if he swore not to hit his slave and ordered another to hit him, he breaks the oath, because the benefit returns to him. Thus in al-'Inayah. [3] His statement: 'it necessitates his ownership'; i.e., the entity being owned by the addressee, so he breaks the oath by his action whether by his command or without his command, because if the action does not admit proxy, it cannot be transferred to other than the doer, so command and lack thereof are equal; so it is determined that the lam is for specification of the entity to save the speech from being meaningless. Thus in al-'Inayah.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on the Kitab al-Ayman (Book of Oaths) in the Hanafi school, specifically dealing with oaths concerning emancipation ('itq) and divorce (talaq). The author explains the legal implication of the letter lam (ل) when it appears in oath formulas like 'If I sell a garment for you (laka), then my slave is free.' The central question is whether the lam indicates that the action (e.g., selling) is done for the benefit of the addressee (ikhtisas al-fi'l) or that the object (e.g., the garment) belongs to the addressee (milk al-'ayn). The ruling depends on the nature of the verb. If the verb is one that can be performed by proxy (niyabah) – that is, it can be delegated to another person – then the lam specifies the action to the addressee. This means the oath is broken only if the action is done by the command of the swearer or with his knowledge and intention that it be for the addressee. For example, if someone swears 'If I sell a garment for Zayd...' and Zayd sells his own garment without the swearer's knowledge, the oath is not broken because the sale was not done by the swearer's command. The author cites al-Zahiriyyah and al-Nahr to illustrate differences: some say the oath is broken only if the action is done by the swearer's command; others say it is broken if the action is done for the addressee's sake, even without a direct command. The preferred view is that specific command is not required; it suffices that the swearer intends the action for the addressee. If the verb is one that does not admit proxy, such as eating, drinking, or hitting a child, or if the lam enters upon an object (ayn) directly, then the lam indicates ownership by the addressee, regardless of who performs the action. For instance, if someone swears 'If I eat food for you (laka)' or 'If I sell a garment for you (laka),' and the action is done by the swearer himself, the oath is broken even without any command, because the ownership of the food or garment is attributed to the addressee. Hitting a child is contrasted with hitting a slave: hitting a child is not delegable (since the benefit is direct), while hitting a slave is delegable because the benefit returns to the owner. The commentary emphasizes that this distinction is based on avoiding meaningless speech (ilgha') – the lam must have a legal effect, so if it cannot specify the action (due to non-delegability), it must specify ownership of the object. The final ruling, as stated in al-Bahr, is that the apparent contradiction in the earlier statements is resolved by understanding that the lam always has either an effect on the action or on the object, as explained.

Key Terms
اللام (al-lam): The letter lam prefixed to a noun or pronoun in oath formulas, indicating either specification (ikhtisas) of the action to the addressee or ownership (milk) of the object by the addressee.
اختصاص (ikhtisas): Specification of the action to the addressee, meaning the action is done for his benefit or by his command.
نيابة (niyabah): Proxy or delegation; the ability to have an action performed by another person on one's behalf.
ملك (milk): Ownership; the right of possession over an object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears, 'By Allah, if I eat food for you (laka), my slave is free,' and then eats food owned by the addressee. According to the Hanafi rule explained in the passage, is the oath broken?
A) No, because eating is delegable, so the lam indicates specification of the action, and the eating was not by command.
B) No, because the lam always indicates ownership, but the food was not specified to the addressee.
C) Yes, because eating is not delegable, so the lam indicates ownership of the food, and the food belonged to the addressee.
D) Yes, because eating is delegable, so the lam indicates specification of the action, and the swearer ate for the addressee's benefit.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath jurisprudence, the letter lam indicates specification of the action if the verb admits proxy, otherwise it indicates ownership of the object, and for delegable verbs, the oath is broken if the swearer intends the action for the addressee.

Interpretation of 'Lam' in Oaths: Ownership vs Benefit
Explains legal meaning of possessive lam in oath phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between delegable and non-delegable acts in Hanafi oath law and apply the rule that the lam (ل) in oath phrases indicates ownership of the object for non-delegable acts and special right for delegable acts, based on the apparent meaning.

Core Idea
In oaths, the lam indicates ownership of the object, not benefit of the action, for non-delegable acts.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya clarifies a subtle grammatical point that has significant legal consequences in Hanafi oath law. When someone swears an oath like 'If I ate food for you', the lam (for) does not mean the action is for the addressee's benefit; rather, it indicates that the food belongs to the addressee. The distinction depends on whether the action can be performed by a proxy (delegable) or not. Understanding this rule prevents misapplication in fatwa and litigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of actions
Distinguish between actions that can be deputized (e.g., striking a child) and those that cannot (e.g., eating, drinking). Non-delegable acts are personal and cannot be performed by a substitute.
Non-delegable acts: eating, drinking, sexual intercourse. Delegable acts: striking, buying, selling.
2
Analyze the grammatical role of the lam
For non-delegable acts, the lam attaches to the object noun and indicates ownership (مِلك). For delegable acts, the lam may indicate special right (اختصاص) over the object, such as striking the addressee's child.
In ‘إن أكلتُ لك طعاماً’, the lam is attached to the noun ‘طعاماً’ (food), indicating that the food is owned by the addressee.
3
Apply the qiyas (analogy)
Compare with the phrase ‘طعاماً لك’ (food of yours). Both indicate that the food is described as being owned by the addressee. The action (eating) is not performed for the addressee's benefit.
The qiyas is based on the garment example: 'a garment of yours' means a garment owned by you.
4
Note the exception for inward intention
If the oath-taker intended a different meaning (e.g., benefit), it is valid between him and Allah (diyanatan) but not in court (qada). This is from Fath al-Qadir.
The apparent meaning prevails in judicial rulings unless a clear contextual indication exists.
5
Summarize the ruling
In judicial rulings, take the apparent meaning of the lam: for non-delegable acts it implies ownership of the object; for delegable acts it implies special right (ikhtisas). The oath is violated only if the specific object owned by or specially related to the addressee is involved.
For example, if the oath is ‘If I eat food for you’, it is only broken if you eat food owned by the addressee, not just any food.

As for the analogy of its entry upon an action that does not occur from another, his saying: 'If I ate food for you, or drank drink for you', requires that the food or drink be the property of the addressee, just as in his saying: 'If I ate food of yours', for although it is related to eating in form, in meaning it is related to the food. Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya explaining the interpretation of the lam (ل) in oath phrases. The author distinguishes between two types of analogies: entry upon a substance (like a garment) and entry upon an action that cannot be performed by proxy (like eating or drinking). In the phrase 'If I ate food for you' (إن أكلتُ لك طعاماً), the lam indicates ownership of the food by the addressee, not that the action is performed for his benefit, because eating is not delegable. This is analogous to saying 'food of yours' (طعاماً لك). In contrast, striking a child can be delegated, so the lam may indicate the addressee's right over the child rather than ownership. The commentary clarifies that 'nazir' here means 'example' and discusses the grammatical proximity that determines attachment. If the oath-taker intends a different meaning, it is valid inwardly (diyanatan) as per Fath al-Qadir, but outwardly the apparent meaning prevails.

Key Terms
النظير (al-nazir): Here means 'example' or 'analogy', not 'counterpart' in the conventional sense.
الدخول على العين (al-dukhul 'ala al-'ayn): Entry upon a substance, meaning the pronoun or lam attaches to a specific object (like a garment).
لا يقع عن غيره (la yaqa'u 'an ghayrihi): An action that cannot be performed by a substitute (i.e., not delegable), such as eating and drinking.
اختصاص (ikhtisas): Special right or entitlement over a person or property, less than full ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the phrase 'إن أكلتُ لك طعاماً', what does the lam (ل) indicate according to the Hanafi interpretation?
A) The action of eating is performed for the benefit of the addressee.
B) The food is owned by the addressee.
C) The oath is about the act of eating anyway.
D) The lam is redundant and has no effect.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath law, the lam (ل) indicates ownership of the object for non-delegable acts and special right for delegable acts, with the apparent meaning prevailing in judicial rulings.

Conditions for Amputation in Theft: Nisab, Mukallaf, Hiraz, and Shubha
Defining the ruling and conditions of amputation for theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the four Hanafi conditions for amputation in theft and explain how shubha repels the hadd.

Core Idea
Amputation for theft requires perfect conditions: nisab, mukallaf, hiraz, and absence of shubha.

The passage opens the chapter on theft by establishing the hadd penalty and immediately listing the conditions that must be met. Understanding these conditions is crucial because if any one is missing, the severe penalty of amputation is dropped. This lesson breaks down each condition and their legal rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its preconditions
The passage states the ruling (amputation) and then lists conditions: nisab, mukallaf, hiraz, and no shubha. Note that these are shurut (conditions) for the hadd, not arkan.
The absence of any condition makes the hadd inapplicable.
2
Examine the nisab condition
The nisab is ten dirhams of minted silver. The passage contrasts this with Shafi'i (1/4 dinar) and Maliki (3 dirhams). Understand that the Hanafi nisab is specific to silver dirhams, not gold or value equivalents.
Also quality matters: dirhams must be good (jā'ida).
3
Analyze the condition of mukallaf
Only a legally responsible person (mukallaf) is subject to the hadd. This excludes minors and the insane. The passage also clarifies that free and slave are equal in liability, citing al-Hidayah's rationale: the text makes no distinction, and half-measure is impossible.
This is a Hanafi position; other schools may differ on slave liability.
4
Understand hiraz and its interaction with shubha
The property must be taken from a secure place (hiraz). However, if there is ambiguity (shubha) about the security—e.g., theft from a mahram relative's house—the hadd is repelled. The shubha arises from implied permission to enter.
Shubha overrides the hiraz condition, protecting the accused from the hadd.
5
Synthesize all conditions in a scenario
Given a scenario, check all four conditions. If any condition is missing or shubha exists, the hadd is not applied. This step prepares you to apply the rules to real cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة ركنها ومحلها ونصابها وحكمها وحكمها : القطع ، فإن سرق مكلّف حرّ أو عبد قدر النصاب محرزاً بلا شُبهة اعلم أن المال المذكور مقدَّر بالنّصاب ، وهو مقدار عشرة دراهم مضروبة من فضة. وعند الشَّافِعِي ربع دينار ذهب. وعند مالك ثلاثة دراهم. (وحكمها : القطع). فإن سرق مكلف حر أو عبد قدر النصاب محرزاً بلا شبهة)، احتراز عما يكون في الحرز شبهة ، كما إذا سرق من بيتِ ذي رحم - ويؤيده أن شروط العقوباتِ تراعى على صفة الكمال رعاية لكمال الجناية، فلو سرق عشرة تبراً قيمتها أنقص من عشرة مضروبة، لا يجب القطع ؛ ولهذا شرطوا في الدراهم الجودة كما مرّ. قوله : مكلّف؛ احترز به عن الصبي والمجنون، فإنّ العقوبة لا تقام عليهما لعدم الجناية منهما. قوله : حر أو عبد ؛ قال في «الهداية»: «العبد» والحر في القطع سواء ؛ لأنّ النّصّ لم يفصل، ولأن التنصيف متعذر فيتكامل ؛ صيانة لأموال الناس. قوله : كما إذا سرق من بيت ذي رحم محرم ؛ سواء سرق مال ذي رحم محرم أو مال غيره من بيته فلا قطع فيه ؛ لوجودِ الشَّبهةِ الدّارئة للحد في الحرز ؛ لوجود الإذنِ بالدخول في بيته من ذي رحم محرم منه، لا سيما إذا كانت بينهما قرابة الولاد.
The Book of Theft: Its essential element, its location, its nisab, and its ruling. And its ruling is: amputation. So if a legally responsible person, free or slave, steals the amount of nisab from a secure place without any ambiguity... Know that the mentioned property is measured by the nisab, which is the amount of ten minted dirhams of silver. According to al-Shafi'i, it is one-quarter dinar of gold. According to Malik, three dirhams. "And its ruling is: amputation." "For if a legally responsible person, free or slave, steals the amount of nisab from a secure place without any ambiguity" — this excludes what involves ambiguity in the secure place, such as if he steals from the house of a relative... This is supported by the fact that the conditions of penalties are observed in a perfect manner to ensure the completeness of the crime. So if someone steals ten nuggets whose value is less than ten minted [dirhams], amputation is not obligatory; for this reason they stipulated good quality in dirhams as mentioned. "His saying: 'legally responsible' excludes the minor and the insane, for the penalty is not imposed on them due to the absence of criminal act from them." "His saying: 'free or slave' — Al-Hidayah states: 'The slave and the free are equal in amputation, because the [sacred] text did not differentiate, and because half-measure is impossible, so it is completed fully; to protect people's wealth.'" "His saying: 'as when he steals from the house of a mahram relative' — whether he steals the wealth of the mahram relative or someone else from his house, there is no amputation in it; due to the presence of ambiguity that averts the hadd in the secure place; because of the existence of permission to enter his house from the mahram relative, especially if there is a blood relationship between them.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on theft by stating the core ruling: amputation (qat') is the penalty. It then outlines the essential conditions for this penalty to apply. The teacher explains that the nisab is ten dirhams of minted silver, noting differences with other schools. The condition of 'mukallaf' excludes minors and the insane, as they lack liability. Both free and slave are equal in the penalty. The condition of 'hiraz' (secure place) is linked to the absence of shubha: if there is ambiguity, such as when the theft occurs from a relative's house where entry is normally permitted, the hadd penalty is dropped. This is because penalties require perfect conditions to ensure the crime is complete and unambiguous.

Key Terms
نِصَاب (nisab): The minimum value for theft to warrant amputation: ten dirhams of minted silver.
مكلّف (mukallaf): A legally responsible person, excluding minors and the insane.
محرز (muhraz): A secure place where property is stored, such as a house or chest.
شُبهة (shubha): Ambiguity that repels the hadd, such as stealing from a relative's house with implied permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum nisab for amputation to apply in theft?
A) One-quarter dinar of gold
B) Ten dirhams of minted silver
C) Three dirhams
D) One dinar

Key Takeaway
Amputation for theft requires perfect conditions: ten dirhams, a legally responsible person, secure place, and no ambiguity – the absence of any condition drops the hadd.

Witness Testimony and Judge's Investigation in Theft Hadd
Ruling on witness testimony and judge's questions in theft hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the admissibility of witness testimony in theft hadd cases and explain why women's testimony is accepted for property but not for the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
Two male witnesses plus judge's investigation confirm theft for hadd; women's testimony excluded from hudud.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the procedural requirements for proving theft (sariqa) to apply the hadd of amputation. The judge must interrogate witnesses with six precise questions to ensure the act is indeed theft warranting hadd, not highway robbery or other misuse of the term. Understanding these rules is essential for correct implementation of Islamic criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core evidentiary rule
The passage states that two male witnesses must testify, and the judge asks about the how, what, when, where, how much, and from whom. Then amputation is carried out.
This is the standard for theft hadd; contrast with zina which requires four witnesses.
2
Recognise the exclusion of women's testimony
Women's testimony is not accepted for the hadd itself, but it may be accepted for the property aspect. This is a key limitation.
This is consistent with the Hanafi rule that women's testimony is not accepted in hudud but is in financial matters.
3
Understand the purpose of the judge's questions
The questions ensure the act is legally defined theft (sariqa) – secret taking from a protected place – and not other acts like eavesdropping, deficient prayer, plagiarism, or highway robbery.
This prevents misapplication of the severe hadd punishment.
4
Note the nuance on concealment (khafiyyah)
For ordinary theft, the taking must be hidden from the owner's eye; for highway robbery (sariqa kubra), this condition is not required. The passage reconciles apparent contradictions.
This illustrates the precision of legal definition in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو شهد رجلان، وسألهما الأمام كيف هي؟ وما هي؟ ومتى؟ وأين هي؟ وكم هي؟ وممن سرق؟ فإن بيناها قطع. قوله : أو شهد رجلان؛ عطف على قوله: «وأقرَّ بها»، وأشار بذكر الرجلين إلى أن لا يشترط فيه أن يشهد أربعة، وإنما ذلك في حد الزنا فحسب، وفيما سواه من الحقوق يكتفي بشهادة اثنين. وإلى أنه لا تقبل في هذا الباب شهادة النساء على ما سيأتي ذكره إن شاء الله في كتاب الشهادات. وذكر في كافي الحاكم وغيره: إنه لو شهد بالسرقة رجل وامرأتان لم تقبل ذلك للقطع، وتقبل في حق المال، وكذا الشهادة على الشهادة. قوله : يُسأل؛ بصيغة المعروف والضمير إلى الإمام أو بصيغة المجهول. قوله : لأنه ربما... الخ؛ ذكر للسؤال عن الماهية أموراً، منها: دفع أن ما يراد غير الذي يوجب الحد، فقد تطلق السرقة على استراق السمع، وقد تطلق على السرقة في الصلاة، وهي أن يؤديها ناقصة، وقد تطلق على الانتحال في التصانيف، وقد تطلق على قطع الطريق. قوله : كما في السرقة الكبرى؛ ظاهره أنه لا يعتبر قيد الخفية في حد قطع الطريق، وقد ذكرنا سابقاً عن فتح القدير ما يخالفه، والأولى أن يراد بالخفية هاهنا الخفية عن عين المالك، فلا يخالف ما مرّ.
Or if two men testify, and the imam asks them: How was it? What was it? When was it? Where was it? How much was it? And from whom was it stolen? Then if they explain, he amputates. He said: 'Or if two men testify' is conjuncted to his statement 'and he confessed to it', and by mentioning 'two men' he indicates that it is not required that four testify, rather that is only in the hadd of zina, and in other than it among rights, two witnesses suffice. And it indicates that in this chapter, women's testimony is not accepted, as will come in the book of testimonies. And it is mentioned in Kafi al-Hakim and others: If a man and two women testify to theft, it is not accepted for amputation, but it is accepted regarding property, and likewise testimony upon testimony. He said: 'is asked' in the active voice with the pronoun referring to the imam, or in the passive voice. He said: 'because perhaps...' etc.; he mentions matters regarding the question about the nature: among them, to reject that what is meant is other than what obliges the hadd, for theft is sometimes applied to eavesdropping, sometimes applied to theft in prayer (performing it deficiently), sometimes applied to plagiarism in compositions, and sometimes applied to highway robbery. He said: 'as in grand theft' (highway robbery); its apparent meaning is that the condition of concealment is not considered in the hadd of highway robbery, and we have previously mentioned from Fath al-Qadir what contradicts it, and the better view is that by concealment here is meant concealment from the eye of the owner, so it does not contradict what has passed.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the evidence required for the hadd of theft and the investigative role of the judge. The base ruling states that if two male witnesses testify and answer the judge's questions about the how, what, when, where, how much, and from whom, then amputation is carried out. The commentary explains that the requirement of two witnesses (as opposed to four in zina) shows that theft is like other legal rights where two male witnesses suffice. Additionally, women's testimony is not accepted for the hadd of theft, but it may be accepted regarding the property aspect. The judge's questioning is crucial to ensure that the crime is indeed theft (sariqa) that warrants hadd, as opposed to other meanings of 'theft' like eavesdropping, deficient prayer, plagiarism, or highway robbery (which has its own ruling). The final note addresses the issue of concealment (khafiyyah) in the definition of sariqa: for ordinary theft, the act must be hidden from the owner's view, but for highway robbery, this is not a condition. The passage refines the legal definition and ensures specificity in evidence.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahada): Testimony of witnesses in court; for theft hadd, it must be from two male witnesses who provide detailed answers to the judge's questions.
سرقة كبرى (sariqa kubra): Grand theft, meaning highway robbery (qat' al-tariq); it has different rulings (hadd for brigandage rather than ordinary theft).
خفية (khafiyyah): Concealment; in the definition of theft (sariqa), the taking must be hidden from the owner's awareness (not necessarily from all eyes).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is accepted like that of two men
B) It is accepted only if there is no male witness
C) It is not accepted for the hadd, but may be accepted for the property aspect
D) It is accepted for both hadd and property

Key Takeaway
For the hadd of theft, two male witnesses who answer the judge's detailed questions are required; women's testimony is insufficient for the punishment but can establish the financial claim.

Rulings on Theft of Chess, Backgammon, Mosque Door, Mus-haf, Minors, Slaves, and Notebooks: When Hadd Is Averted
States legal rulings and proofs for when theft triggers hand-cutting and when not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on theft of specific items and explain the legal reasoning (shubha, iḥrāz, māliyyah) that determines whether the hadd is applied.

Core Idea
Hadd is averted by shubha: a plausible claim of permissible intent or lack of secure storage can prevent hand-cutting.

In this passage, the Hanafi school specifies which stolen items trigger the ḥadd of hand-cutting and which do not. The rulings hinge on three key factors: the existence of a plausible shubha (doubt) that can avert the punishment, the requirement of secure storage (iḥrāz), and whether the item is considered property (māl). The text presents the positions of Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, along with a disagreement with al-Shafi'i on certain points.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item and the default ruling
For each item listed (chess/backgammon, mosque door, mus-haf, free minor, adult slave, notebook), note whether the Hanafi school applies hand-cutting (hadd) or not.
The default ruling is no cutting for most items listed, except small slave and account book.
2
Identify the legal reason (ʿillah) for averting the hadd
For each item where hadd is averted, find the specific reason: plausible claim of permissible intent (shubha), lack of secure storage (ʿadam al-iḥrāz), or the item not being property (laysa bi-māl).
The reasons are explicitly stated: chess/backgammon – claim of breaking; mosque door – no ḥirz; mus-haf – claim of reading; free minor – not property; adult slave – not theft but usurpation; ordinary notebook – content not property.
3
Check for intra-Hanafi disagreement (Sahibayn vs. Abu Yusuf)
Note where Abu Yusuf differs: on ornamented (muḥallā) items, he holds that if the ornament alone reaches nisab, the hand is cut.
The text follows the majority position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad that the ornament is a follower (tabiʿ) of the original and does not trigger cutting.
4
Check for inter-madhhab disagreement (Shafiʿi)
Identify where al-Shafiʿi disagrees: on the mosque door and the mus-haf. Under Shafiʿi law, the hand is cut for theft of these items because they are considered property and no valid shubha exists.
The Hanafi position relies on the lack of ḥirz for the door and the permissibility of taking the mus-haf for recitation.
5
Derive the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf)
For each disagreement, state how the ruling would differ in practice between Hanafi and Shafiʿi, or between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
Example: stealing a Quran from a mosque: Hanafi – no cutting; Shafiʿi – cutting. The distinction affects actual criminal proceedings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة باب ما يقطع به وما لا يقطع أو شطرنج ، ونرد ، وباب مسجد ، ومصحف (أو شطرنج، ونرد) ؛ لأنه يقول أخذتُه للإراقة والكسر. (وباب مسجد) ؛ لعدم الإحراز خلافا للشافعي (1) له (و مصحف (0) و [١] قوله : وشطرنج ونرد ؛ هو وإن كان داخلاً في آلات اللهو لكن ذكرهما توضيحاً وتصريحاً ؛ ولأن أكثر ما يتبادر من آلات اللهو هو المزامير، قال صلاح الدين خليل الصفدي في «غيث الأدب المسجم شرح لامية العجم» عند شرح : إن العلى حدثتني وهي صادقة فيما تحدث أنَّ العــــ في النقل لك في لفظ الشطرنج : لغتان بالشين المعجمة ، وهو الأفصح ؛ لأنه مأخوذ من الشطر؛ لأن كل لاعب له شطر من القِطَع، وبالسين المهملة ؛ لأنه مأخوذ من تسطير الرقعة بيوتاً، وإن ألحقته بأوزان العربية كسرت أوله ، والصحيح أن هذه اللفظة عجمية، وأصله : شش رنك: معناه ستة ألوان. وكثير من الناس يغلطون في الصولي، وهو أبو بكر محمد بن يحيى بن عبد الله بن عباس بن محمد بن صول، ويزعم أنه واضع الشطرنج لِمَا ضُرب به المثلُ فيه والصحيح أن واضعه صصه بن داهر الهندي وكان أردشير بن بابك أول ملوك الفرس الأخيرة قد وضع النرد ؛ ولذلك قيل له : نردشير، وجعله مثالاً للدنيا وأهلها، افتخرت به الفرس، وكان ملك الهند يومئذ بلهيت، فوضع له صصه الشطرنج. انتهى ملخصاً. [٢]قوله : يقول ؛ أي يحتمل أن يقول ذلك، فإن وجود قوله ليس بشرط للدرء. (۲) [٣] قوله : والكسر ؛ هذا مرتبط بآلات اللهو والصليب والشطرنج والنرد. [٤]قوله : وباب مسجد ؛ وكذا سارقُ باب كلِّ دار لعدم الحرز، وكذا سارق نعال المصلين من المسجد ، وكذا ستار الكعبة. كذا في «البحر» [0] قوله : ومصحف ؛ - بفتح الحاء المهملة، وتثليث الميم والضم أشهر – ، كما في «المصباح»، وهو ما كتب فيه القرآن. والوجه في عدم القطع، هو وجود الشبهة الدارئة للحد ؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يقول لأنه يقولُ أخذتُهُ للقراءة خلافا لأبي يوسف والشَّافِعِي ، وصبي . لأنه ليس بمال، (ولو محلّيين ) ، يرجع إلى المصحف والصبي، فإن الحلية تبع". وعند أبي يوسف له إن بلغت الحلية النصاب يقطع. وعبد ودفتر ت (٤) (۲) إلا الصغير، ودفتر الحساب ؛ لأنَّ أخذ العبد الكبير يكونُ غصباً أو خداعاً لا سرقة، والمقصود من الدفتر ما فيه، وهو ليس بمال أخذته لأن اقرأ منه القرآن، وأمّا الكتب العلمية، فإن كانت شرعيّة فأخذها كأخذ آلات اللهو. [1]قوله : وصبي : أي لا قطع بسرقة صبي حر لعدم كونه مالاً ، وكذا البالغ الحر بالطريق الأولى، وأما سرقة العبد فتوجب القطع إذا كان صغيراً، ولا توجب إذا كان كبيراً ؛ لأن العبد مال غير أنّ أخذ الكبير ليس بسرقة ، بل غصب أو خداع على ما سيأتي ، والمراد بالكبير هناك المميز الذي يعبر عن نفسه بالغاً كان أو غير بالغ، وبالصغير خلافه. كذا في «البحر». [٢] قوله : ولو محليين ؛ هذا اللفظ في بعض النسخ باليائين، والصواب ما في بعض النسخ محلين بالياء الواحدة : أي لا قطع بسرقة المصحف والصبي، ولو كان عليهما حلية ذهب، أو فضة. [٣]قوله : تبع ; فحيث لم يجب القطعُ في سرقة الأصل لم يجب بالتبع. [٤]قوله : ودفتر؛ هو - بالفتح - ، وقد يكسر : جماعة الصحف المضمومة، والحاصل أنه لا قطع بسرقة عبد إلا إذا كان صغيراً ، فإنّ أخذ العبد الكبير إن كان أخذه قهراً فهو غصب، وإن أخذه بحلية، فهو خداع، وكل منهما ليس بسرقة. وكذا : لا قطع بسرقة دفتر ؛ لأن المقصود من أخذها هو معرفة ما كتب فيه، لا نفس أوراق القرطاس، وهو ليس بمال إلا إذا كان دفتر الحساب، فإنّ المقصود من أخذه المالُ الذي كَتَبَ حاسبه فيه.
The book of theft, chapter of what the hand is cut for and what not: Or chess and backgammon, and the door of a mosque, and a mus-haf. (Or chess and backgammon) because he says: I took it for pouring and breaking. (And the door of a mosque) due to lack of secure storage, contrary to al-Shafi'i. And mus-haf... [Footnote 1: saying: and chess and backgammon...] [Footnote 2: saying: he says; that is, he may say that, for his saying is not a condition for aversion.] [Footnote 3: saying: and breaking; this is connected to instruments of play, cross, chess, and backgammon.] [Footnote 4: saying: and door of a mosque; and likewise the thief of the door of every house due to lack of secure storage, and likewise the thief of the slippers of worshippers from the mosque, and likewise the covering of the Kaaba. Thus in al-Bahr.] [Footnote 0: saying: and mus-haf; with fatha on ha, and three vowels on mim, damma being most famous, as in al-Misbah, and it is what the Quran is written in. And the reason for no cutting is the existence of shubha that averts the hadd, because he may say he took it for recitation, contrary to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i. And a free minor boy: because he is not property. (And if ornamented) it refers to the mus-haf and the boy, for the ornament is a follower. And according to Abu Yusuf, if the ornament reaches nisab, his hand is cut. And a slave and a notebook, except the small one and the account book, because taking an adult slave is usurpation or deception, not theft, and the purpose of the notebook is what is in it, and it is not property. He says: I took it to recite Quran from it. As for scholarly books, if they are religious, taking them is like taking instruments of play. [Commentary: Saying: and a free minor boy: i.e., no cutting for stealing a free minor boy because he is not property, and likewise a free adult by priority. As for stealing a slave, it requires cutting if the slave is small, and does not require if adult, because a slave is property but taking an adult is not theft, rather usurpation or deception as will come. The meaning of adult there is the discerning one who can express himself, whether adult or not, and the small is other than that. Thus in al-Bahr. Saying: and if ornamented; this wording in some copies with two ya's, the correct in some copies is with one ya: i.e., no cutting for stealing the mus-haf and the boy even if they have ornaments of gold or silver. Saying: follower; so when cutting is not required in stealing the original, it is not required by following. Saying: and notebook; it is with fatha, and may be with kasra: a collection of bound pages. The gist is that there is no cutting for stealing a slave except if small. For taking an adult slave, if taken by force it is usurpation, and if taken by trickery it is deception, and neither is theft. Likewise, no cutting for stealing a notebook because the purpose of taking it is to know what is written in it, not the paper leaves themselves, and that is not property, except if it is an account book, for the purpose of taking it is the money that his accountant wrote in it.]
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on theft, specifically the section on what items trigger the hadd (hand-cutting) punishment and what items do not. The text begins by listing items whose theft does not incur the hadd: chess and backgammon (instruments of play), the door of a mosque, and a mus-haf (copy of the Quran). For each, the author provides a rationale. For chess and backgammon, the thief could claim he took them to break or destroy them, creating a shubha (doubt) that averts the hadd. For a mosque door, since it is not secured (حرز), the condition of secure storage is missing, and therefore no cutting, though al-Shafi'i differs on this. For the mus-haf, the thief might claim he took it to read it, creating a shubha. This is contrary to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, who hold that the hand should be cut. The commentary then delves into a lengthy linguistic and historical note on the words 'chess' and 'backgammon' by al-Safadi, explaining the etymology and the origins of the games. Returning to legal points, the commentator clarifies that the thief's actual statement is not required; it is sufficient that he could plausibly say such a thing. The ruling on mosque doors is extended to other similar items like doors of houses, slippers in the mosque, and the covering of the Kaaba, citing al-Bahr. For the mus-haf, the commentator defines the term and confirms the shubha argument. The passage then discusses a free minor boy, who is not considered property, so no cutting applies. When items are ornamented (محليين), the ornament follows the ruled item; hence, no cutting unless the ornament itself reaches nisab, per Imam Abu Yusuf. Next, the text covers adult slaves and notebooks: stealing an adult slave is either usurpation (if by force) or deception (if by trickery), not theft, so no cutting; but stealing a small slave does require cutting. Similarly, notebooks are not cut for because the intended value is the content, which is not property (except account books where the writing records money). The commentator further elaborates on the definitions of 'small' and 'adult' slaves and the wording of 'محليين'. This shows the Hanafi method of using shubha and the condition of proper storage (حرز) to restrict the application of hadd.

Key Terms
شبهة دارئة (shubha dāri'a): A doubt or ambiguity that averts the application of the hadd punishment.
إحراز (iḥrāz): Secure storage or location of property; a condition for theft to be subject to hadd.
آلات اللهو (ālāt al-lahw): Instruments of play or amusement, such as chess and backgammon, whose theft does not incur hadd due to the possibility of claiming intent to break them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the hand not cut for stealing a mosque door?
A) Because the door is not considered property.
B) Because the door is not in a secure storage (حرز).
C) Because the thief may claim he took it for reading.
D) Because the door is attached to the mosque and thus deemed ownerless.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school averts the ḥadd for theft when there is a plausible shubha or when the item lacks secure storage, even if the item is valuable, prioritizing the principle of doubt over punishment.

No Liability for Damages After Hadd for Theft
Ruling and proof that thief is not liable for damages after hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the thief is not liable for damages after hadd, explain the proofs from Quran and hadith, and evaluate the rational argument based on the retroactive ownership contradiction.

Core Idea
After hadd, the thief owes nothing — property's inviolability transfers to Allah.

This passage establishes a key Hanafi principle: after the hand is cut, the thief is not required to compensate the owner. The author cites a hadith (though weak) and rational arguments, including the Quran's silence on damages and the contradiction that would arise if damages were required. This doctrine sharply distinguishes the Hanafi school from the Shafi'i position, which combines both hadd and financial liability.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and core principle
Identify that the ruling is non-liability after hadd. The core principle is the transfer of inviolability (ismah) from the owner to Allah, which extinguishes the owner's claim.
This transfer is unique to theft under Hanafi law.
2
Examine the hadith evidence
The hadith 'لا يغرم صاحب السرقة إذا أقيم عليه الحد' directly states the rule. Note that the author acknowledges its weak chains but supports it with other evidence.
Weakness does not prevent reliance when corroborated by other proofs.
3
Analyze the Quranic argument from silence
Verse 5:38 commands cutting hands but does not mention financial penalty. If it were obligatory, Allah would have stated it. Silence implies non-obligation.
This is an argument from absence of mention.
4
Understand the rational argument about retroactive ownership
If the thief paid damages, he would retroactively own the stolen item at the time of theft, creating doubt (shubha) that would prevent the hadd. Since hadd is definitive, damages must be negated.
This shows internal consistency in Hanafi legal reasoning.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the thief must both suffer hadd and return the item or its value. This difference arises from different views on whether hadd replaces civil liability.
Both positions are valid ikhtilaf.

Book of Theft / Section: How to Cut and Its Proof. [1]. Transfer of inviolability to Allah Almighty. An-Nasa'i. One of them: Hadith: 'The owner of the theft is not made liable when the hadd is established against him' narrated by and in a narration of ad-Daraqutni: 'No liability on the thief after cutting his right hand' and in a narration of al-Bazzar and at-Tabarani: 'The thief does not guarantee his theft after the establishment of the hadd'. This hadith repels disturbance and cuts off dispute if it is established by a probative chain, but it is not established, for all its chains contain weakness due to discontinuity, ignorance, and other things, as az-Zayla'i expanded in Takhrij Ahadith al-Hidayah. And al-'Ayni mentioned in al-Binaya that Ibn Jarir at-Tabari narrated it in Tahdhib al-Athar with a connected probative chain, then non-liability was narrated from Ibn Sirin, an-Nakha'i, ash-Sha'bi, al-'Ata', al-Hasan, and Qatada, and he said: This is correct because of His statement: 'As for the thief, male and female, cut off their hands as recompense for what they have earned' – He did not order imposing a fine; if it were obligatory on them He would have mentioned it. 2. Among them: The obligation of damages contradicts the obligation of cutting, because the thief becomes owner of it, retroactively to the time of taking after paying damages, as established in the chapter of liability of usurpers and others, so it would entail the theft occurring on his property, and if it is retroactive ownership, cutting is negated due to ambiguity, and it is known that cutting is definitively established, so liability is negated. (6) And among them: The fall of inviolability as a right of the slave at the time of theft, and its explanation has passed. [1] His statement: 'Transfer of inviolability' – because if it remained, it would be permissible in itself, for it is known by stability that what is forbidden as a right of the slave is permissible in itself, so the property is forbidden to the thief from one aspect but not another. /: (1) In Sunan an-Nasa'i al-Kubra (4): (349), and al-Mujtaba (8): (93), and al-Mu'jam al-Awsat (9): 110, and others. (2) In Sunan ad-Daraqutni (3): 182, and others. (3) In Musnad al-Bazzar (3): 267, and others. (4) Nasb ar-Raya (3): 379. (5) In al-Binaya (5): 613-614. (6) Al-Ma'idah: from verse 38.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling that a thief is not liable for damages (gharam) after the hadd punishment (amputation of the hand) is executed. The author begins by citing a hadith that states the owner of the stolen property cannot claim compensation after the hadd is applied. He notes that the hadith is weak in transmission but is supported by other evidence. The author then gives three rational arguments. First, if damages were obligatory, they would have been mentioned in the Quranic verse on cutting hands (Quran 5:38), but they are not. Second, requiring damages would create a contradiction: if the thief pays damages, he retroactively becomes owner of the stolen item, so the theft would be on his own property, creating doubt (shubha) that would prevent the hadd. Since the hadd is definitively established, the obligation of damages must be negated. Third, at the moment of theft, the inviolability of the property transfers from being a right of the owner to a right of Allah, so the thief incurs no liability to the owner. The commentary explains this 'transfer of inviolability' in more detail, stating that what is forbidden as a right of the slave (owner) becomes permissible in itself, so the property becomes like carrion (which is forbidden as a right of Allah) and incurs no damages. The passage thus establishes that the hadd punishment replaces the obligation to compensate the owner.

Key Terms
عصمة (ismah): Legal inviolability; protection of property from being taken without liability.
تضمين (tadmin): Imposition of financial liability/damages for loss or destruction of property.
شبهة (shubha): Ambiguity or doubt that can avert the hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the Quran does not mention compensation, and requiring it would create a contradiction with the hadd.
C) Because the owner should forgive the thief.
D) Because the thief has already paid the fine.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the hadd punishment for theft extinguishes the thief's financial liability to the owner, based on the transfer of inviolability to Allah.

Multiple Thefts and Liability After Hadd Execution
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability after multiple thefts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on liability after multiple thefts and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding absent victims.

Core Idea
Multiple thefts merge into a single hadd; once the hand is cut, the thief owes no compensation to any victim.

In this lesson, we examine a subtle case: a thief steals multiple times from one or more persons, each time reaching the nisab. If his hand is cut, does he still owe compensation to his victims? The Hanafi school holds a unique position based on the principle of tadaakhul (overlap of hadd punishments). We will explore the ruling of Abu Hanifa, the disagreement of his two disciples, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage considers a thief who commits multiple thefts, each meeting the nisab threshold. The victims may be one person (multiple thefts from the same) or multiple persons (one theft from each). The hand is cut for all or only for some victims who litigate.
Note: The thefts must each be a complete, independent act of stealing subject to hadd.
2
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa holds that once the hand is cut, whether for all victims or only for those who litigated, the thief is not liable for damages (gharam) to any victim. The single hadd execution fulfills the right of Allah for all thefts.
3
State the ruling of Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)
The two disciples hold that if the hand is cut only due to litigation of some victims, the thief remains liable to the absent victims. They argue that litigation is required for the theft to be 'manifested'; for absent victims, the theft was not proven, so their property remains inviolable.
4
Understand the underlying principle: tadaakhul
Abu Hanifa's reasoning is based on tadaakhul: the hadd obligation for multiple thefts overlaps into a single execution. Since the cutting is wajib as a right of Allah, fulfilling it once satisfies all causes. Once the hand is cut, the right of the Law has been served, and the victim's right to compensation (beyond the return of the specific item) lapses.
This is contrasted with Sahibayn's view that each theft remains independent until litigation manifests it.
5
Apply to a specific example
Example: Thief A steals from B, C, and D (each nisab). Only B litigates; A's hand is cut. According to Abu Hanifa: A owes nothing to B, C, or D (the cut covers all). According to Sahibayn: A owes damages to C and D (since they didn't litigate, their property remained inviolable).
Note: In all cases, the specific stolen items, if found, are returned to their owners because ownership remains.

Umdat ar-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah: And he does not guarantee from one who steals multiple times, and his hand is cut for all of them or for some of them. Meaning: Indeed the property was inviolable as a right of the slave, so when theft occurred upon it, the Lawgiver obligated the hadd, which is a right of the Law, so the injury occurred upon the right of the Law, so in the state of theft the property became inviolable as a right of the Law, so it did not remain inviolable as a right of the slave, so compensation is not obligatory. (And he does not guarantee from one who steals multiple times), and his hand is cut for all of them or for some of them) from the victims: if they are all present such that the cutting is for all, he does not guarantee anything to anyone at all; and if only some are present such that the cutting is for them, then likewise according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the liability of the one for whom the hand was cut is dropped. [2] An aspect: So cutting is negated due to the ambiguity of it being permissible in itself; and when it is no longer inviolable as a right of the slave, it becomes forbidden as a right of the Law like carrion, and there is no liability for something forbidden as a right of the Law, as in al-Binaya. And it is answered: It is returned due to formal similarity and the survival of the owner's ownership in it even though inviolability has transferred to Allah. [2] His statement: 'multiple times' – whether the usurpation is from one person, in that he stole from a single man multiple times, each time to the extent of the nisab, or from multiple persons. [3] His statement: 'from the victims...etc.': The summary is that if he stole from men, from each to the extent of the nisab, and they all come and litigate, his hand is cut due to their litigation, he does not guarantee anything to any one by agreement. And if some of them come and litigate and his hand is cut, then it is likewise according to him (Abu Hanifa), contrary to the two (disciple); based on the fact that the one present is not a representative of the absent, and litigation is necessary for the manifestation of the theft, so the theft is not manifested with respect to the absent ones, and the cutting did not occur for them, so their properties remain inviolable, so compensation is obligatory for them except for the one for whose litigation the hand was cut. And his (Abu Hanifa) argument: The basis of hadd punishments is overlapping (tadaakhul), so what is obligatory due to all thefts is one cutting as a right of Allah, so when it is executed, even by the litigation of some, what is executed is all that is obligatory, so it occurs for all. And upon this difference is the case if the thefts are from one person and he litigated concerning some of the stolen items, even if some. This is from al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage discusses a nuanced case: a thief steals multiple times, reaching the nisab each time, from one or multiple persons. If he is caught and his hand is cut, is he liable for damages to the victims? The text explains that according to Abu Hanifa, once the hand is cut—whether for all victims or only for some who litigate—the thief is not liable to any victim. His reasoning is that hadd punishments 'overlap' (tadaakhul): the single obligation of cutting covers all thefts, so the execution of the hadd fulfills the right of Allah for all. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if only some victims litigate and the hand is cut for them, the thief remains liable to the absent victims because their theft was not 'manifested' through litigation. The author then addresses an objection: if liability is dropped, why is the stolen item itself still returned if found intact? The answer is that ownership remains with the original owner, so the item is returned due to formal similarity and continuing ownership, even though the right of compensation (damages) has lapsed. The passage concludes by cross-referencing al-Hidayah for the same ruling in single-victim cases.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadaakhul): Overlap or merging of multiple obligations into one; specifically, multiple hadd punishments for repeated thefts merge into a single cutting.
خصومة (khusoomah): Litigation; formal legal claim by the victim to establish the theft and demand the hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The thief must pay compensation to all three victims.
B) The thief must pay compensation only to the two who did not litigate.
C) The thief owes no compensation to any of the three victims.
D) The thief owes compensation only to the one who litigated.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, multiple thefts cause a single hadd obligation; once the hand is cut, the thief is not liable for damages to any victim, though stolen items must be returned if found.

No Liability for Multiple Thefts: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Explains that multiple thefts entail one cutting with no financial liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a thief who steals multiple times is liable after hadd, and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When a thief's hand is cut for one theft, all thefts merge into a single divine right; no financial liability remains.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: if a thief steals from multiple victims and his hand is cut due to only some of them, does he still owe compensation to the others? The answer hinges on two key principles: tadakhul (overlap) of hadd punishments and the transfer of 'ismah (inviolability) from private right to divine right. We will unpack Abu Hanifa's reasoning and see why the Sahibayn disagree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is multiple thefts by the same thief, each reaching the nisab. The ruling is that the hand is cut once for all thefts. The dispute is over financial liability (daman) for the stolen property after the cutting.
The passage assumes the cutting is valid (hadd has been applied).
2
Understand the shift in 'ismah
Abu Hanifa argues that theft transforms the property's 'ismah from a servant's right (haqq al-'abd) to a right of the Shari'a (haqq al-shar'). Once the hand is cut, the property loses its inviolability as a private claim, so no daman is due.
The analogy: stolen property becomes like carrion (maytah) – prohibited but not financially compensable.
3
Apply tadakhul of hadd
The hadd cutting is one single divine obligation that covers all thefts due to tadakhul (overlap). Once executed, even for only some thefts, it fulfills the entire obligation.
Tadakhul is a Hanafi principle: multiple identical causes trigger a single punishment.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's objection
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the theft is only manifest through litigation. Absent victims have not had their theft proven, so their property remains inviolable. Therefore, the thief must compensate them.
For the Sahibayn, the present litigant is not a deputy for the absent ones.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence (thamarah) is clear: under Abu Hanifa the thief pays nothing; under the Sahibayn he pays the absent victims. This difference directly affects how a qadi would rule.
The passage does not settle tarjih; both views are recorded in zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يضمن من سرق مرَّات، فقطع بكلها ، أو بعضها شيئاً منها معناه : إن المال كان معصوماً حقاً للعبد ، فإذا ورد عليه السرقة، أوجب الشارع الحد، وهو حق الشرع، فالجناية وردت على حق الشرع، ففي حالة السرقة صار المال معصوماً حقاً للشرع، فلم يبق معصوماً لحق العبد، فلا يجب الضمان. ( ولا يضمن من سرق مرات ) ، فقطعَ بكلها ، أو بعضها شيئاً منها) المسروق منهم " إن حضروا حتى كان القطع للكل لا يضمن لأحد أصلاً، وإن حضر البعض حتى قطعَ لأجلهم ، فكذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما يسقط ضمانُ مَن قُطِعَ لأجله. وجه، فينتفي القطع لشبهة كونه مباحاً في نفسه وإذا لم يبق معصوماً حقاً للعبد، فيصير محرماً حقاً للشرع كالميتة، ولا ضمان في المحرم حقاً للشرع، كذا في «البناية). 11 ] قوله : فالجناية وردت على حق الشرع ...الخ ؛ يرد عليه : أنه لو صح هذا التقرير لزم أن لا يردّ المسروق ؛ لذا كان قائماً بعينه وليس كذالك. ويجاب عنه : بأنه يردّ للتشابه الصوري ولبقاء ملك المالك فيه وإن انتقلت العصمة إلى الله ع وإن شئت زيادة تفصيل في هذا المقام فارجع إلى كتب الأصول. [٣] قوله : المسروق منهم... الخ : الحاصل أنه إذا سرق من رجال من كل بقدر النصاب، فحضروا جميعاً وخاصموا ، قطعت يده لخصومتهم لا يضمن شيئاً لواحد اتفاقا ، وإن حضر بعضُهم فخاصم وقُطعت يده فكذا عنده، خلافاً لهما ؛ بناءً على أن الحاضر ليس بنائب عن الغائب، ولا بُدَّ من الخصومة لظهور السرقة، فلم تظهر السرقة في حق الغائبين، ولم يقع القطع لهم، فبقيت أموالهم معصومةً فوجب الضمان لهم دون من قطعت يده بخصومة. وله : إن مبنى الحدود على التداخل ، فالواجب بكل السرقات قطع واحد حقاً لله ، فإذا استوفى ولو بخصومة البعض، فالمستوفى كل الواجب فيقع عن الكل، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا كانت السرقات من واحدٍ فخاصم في بعض المسروقات وإن البعض، كذا في «الهداية».
And he is not liable who steals multiple times, and his hand is cut for all of them or some of them. Meaning: The property was inviolable as a right of the servant. When theft occurred, the Lawgiver imposed the hadd, which is a right of the Shari'a. So the felony occurred on the right of the Shari'a, and in the case of theft, the property became inviolable as a right of the Shari'a, so it no longer remained inviolable as a right of the servant, thus liability is not obligatory. (And he is not liable who steals multiple times, and his hand is cut for all or some of them) from the victims: if they all are present so that the cutting is for all, he is not liable to anyone at all. If some are present and the cutting is for them, then similarly according to Abu Hanifa. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the liability of the one for whom the cutting was done is lifted. An argument: the cutting is negated due to the semblance of it (the hand) being permissible in itself. And when it is no longer inviolable as a right of the servant, it becomes prohibited as a right of the Shari'a, like carrion, and there is no liability for what is prohibited as a right of the Shari'a. Thus in al-Binaya. He said: 'So the felony occurred on the right of the Shari'a...' It is objected: if this reasoning were correct, it would necessitate that the stolen property not be returned even if it still exists in kind, but that is not the case. The response: it is returned due to formal similarity and because the owner's ownership remains in it, even though the inviolability has transferred to Allah. If you wish further detail on this topic, refer to books of usul. He said: 'multiple times' — whether the misappropriation is from one person, i.e., he stole from one man multiple times each time to the extent of the nisab of theft, or from multiple persons. He said: 'the victims...' The summary: if he stole from men, from each to the extent of the nisab, and they all come and litigate, his hand is cut due to their litigation, and he is not liable for anything to anyone by agreement. If some of them are present and litigate and his hand is cut, then similarly according to him (Abu Hanifa), contrary to them (the two companions); based on the fact that the present one is not a deputy for the absent one, and litigation is necessary for the manifestation of the theft, so the theft is not manifested in regard to the absent ones, and the cutting did not occur for them, so their property remains inviolable, thus liability is obligatory for them, not for the one for whose litigation the hand was cut. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that the basis of hadd punishments is on overlapping (tadakhul), so what is obligatory by all thefts is one cut as a right of Allah. So if it is executed, even by litigation of some, what is executed is the entire obligatory, so it covers all. And upon this difference is the case if the thefts are from one person and he litigates over some of the stolen items even if some. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the ruling on a thief who steals multiple times, whether from one or multiple victims, and his hand is cut for all or some of the thefts. The central issue is whether the thief remains liable (daman) to compensate the victims for the stolen property after the hadd punishment is executed. The text explains that according to Abu Hanifa, once the theft occasions the hadd, the property's inviolability (ismah) shifts from being a right of the servant (haqq al-‘abd) to a right of the Shari‘a (haqq al-shar‘). Consequently, the hand becomes lawful to cut, and the property is no longer protected as a private right, so no financial liability remains. This is supported by the principle of tadakhul (overlapping) of hadd punishments: the single cutting fulfills the entire obligation for all thefts. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) differ: they hold that if only some victims are present and litigate, the cutting only affects the liability towards those present; the absent victims retain their right to compensation because the theft was not established against them. Abu Hanifa argues that the presence of some litigants suffices because the cutting is one divine right that covers all. The commentary also addresses an objection: if liability drops, why is the stolen property still returned? The answer is that the owner's ownership remains, but the inviolability (ismah) for the purpose of daman is transferred; the return is due to the formal resemblance (tashabbuh suri) and to uphold ownership. The passage is taken from al-Hidayah and al-Binaya, and reflects a key principle of Hanafi fiqh regarding the interplay between divine rights and private rights in hadd punishments.

Key Terms
عصمة (‘ismah): Inviolability; legal protection of property or life, entitling the holder to claims (like daman) if violated.
تداخل (tadakhul): Overlapping; principle that multiple identical causes merge into a single obligation when the punishment is the same.
ضمان (daman): Financial liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does a thief who steals from multiple individuals not have to compensate the victims after his hand is cut, even if only some victims were present in court?
A) Because the victims forfeited their right by not attending court.
B) Because the hadd cutting is a single right of Allah that covers all thefts due to tadakhul, and the property's inviolability shifts away from private claims.
C) Because the property is considered legally dead (murd) after theft.
D) Because the thief's hand is a substitute for the monetary value.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the hadd punishment for theft (cutting) can override private financial claims through the principles of tadakhul and shift of ‘ismah, but the Sahibayn limit this to cases where all thefts were litigated.

Tearing a Cloth Inside and Taking It Out: Dispute with Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on a theft scenario

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a thief who tears a cloth from inside the house and then takes it out, explain Abu Yusuf's objection based on ownership via extreme tearing, and apply the rules to the scenario of a third party cutting the thief's hand.

Core Idea
Extreme tearing of stolen property transfers ownership to the thief via guarantee, creating a doubt that may prevent the hadd amputation.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in theft law: a thief tears a cloth from inside the owner's house and then takes it out. The matn states he is cut if the torn piece reaches nisab. However, Imam Abu Yusuf raises a powerful objection: extreme tearing transfers ownership to the thief, so when he takes it out, he is taking his own property, which does not constitute theft. The commentary analyses this dispute and also explores a case where a third party cuts off the thief's hand before the amputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the thief doing? He tears a cloth from inside the house and then takes it out. According to the matn, he is subject to hadd amputation (cut) if the torn piece reaches the nisab of theft (10 dirhams or equivalent).
The nisab condition is shared by all positions.
2
State the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the thief is cut. Imam Abu Yusuf (and Muhammad ibn al-Hasan in some reports) holds that he is not cut because the extreme tearing (kharq fahish) makes the cloth the property of the thief via the guarantee for the damage, so when he takes it out he is removing his own property, not stealing.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyse the reasoning (talil) behind Abu Yusuf's position
The extreme tearing causes loss of some substance and benefit, obligating the thief to pay compensation. In Hanafi law, this compensation gives the destroyer ownership of the damaged item. Thus, the cloth becomes the thief's property before removal, creating a shubha (doubt) that blocks the hadd punishment.
This ownership is conditional on the tearing being extreme (fahish); minor tearing does not transfer ownership.
4
Examine the third-party scenario
If someone cuts off the thief's right hand before the official amputation, the ruling depends on timing: (1) Before litigation: the cutter faces qisas (retaliation) or blood money, and the thief's left leg is cut for the theft. (2) After litigation but before judgment: same except no leg cut because the obligation on the hand fell due to its loss. (3) After judgment: no liability on the cutter, as it counts as execution of the punishment.
This scenario illustrates the interaction between private vengeance and state punishment.
5
Understand the commentary's elaboration
The disagreement applies only when the tearing is extreme. If the owner chooses to claim compensation for the decrease and takes the torn cloth, then according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad the thief is cut (contrary to Abu Hanifa's view). If the owner claims full value and leaves the cloth, no cutting by agreement. For minor tearing (only defaces), cutting occurs by agreement. The combination of cutting and guarantee is explained as two separate acts: guarantee for the part lost (due to destruction) and cutting for taking out the remainder.
This resolves the apparent conflict between the two obligations.

Source Text

And he is cut who tears what he stole inside the house, then takes it out; and he is only cut if the torn piece reaches the nisab of theft. And according to Abu Yusuf, he is not cut, because the garment became the property of the thief due to the extreme tearing. Then (as for the) theft: he was not cut until a cutter cut his right hand; this is not free from either being before litigation or after it, before judgment or after it. If it was before litigation, then upon the cutter is retaliation in case of intentionality and compensation in case of error, and his left leg is cut for the theft. And if it was after litigation before judgment, the answer is the same except that his leg is not cut, because when he litigated, the obligation was on the right hand, and it fell due to its loss. And if it was after judgment, there is no liability on the cutter, and his cut was (part) of the theft. (Commentator's words): "al-mashqooq" means the stolen object after tearing and split that reaches the nisab of theft. (Commentator's words): "He is not cut"; he said in al-Nahr: 'The place of disagreement is when he tore it extremely, which is that by which some of the substance and some of the benefit is lost, according to the sounder view, and the owner chose to claim compensation for the decrease and take the garment; cutting occurs according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to him (Abu Hanifa). But if he chose to claim compensation for the value and leave the garment, there is no cutting by agreement. As for minor tearing: that which only defaces it, then cutting occurs by agreement.' Because the guarantee for the decrease was obligated by another injury before the removal, which is what was lost of the substance, and cutting is for taking out the remainder.' (Commentator's words): "Because the garment... etc."; its summary: when he tore it extremely, he owned it because of the existence of its cause, for it necessitates the value and ownership of the guaranteed item as established in the Book of Usurpation, so it is found.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage from the commentary discusses the ruling on a thief who tears a piece of cloth from inside the owner's house and then takes it out. The matn states that the thief is cut (hand amputated) only if the torn piece reaches the nisab (minimum value for amputation). Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that because the thief tore the cloth extremely (making it his own according to the principle that one who destroys property must compensate and thereby owns it), the cloth becomes his property, creating a doubt (shubha) that prevents the amputation. The text then examines a scenario where a third party cuts the thief's right hand before the official amputation. The ruling depends on timing: if before litigation, the cutter faces retaliation or blood money and the thief's left leg is cut instead; if after litigation but before judgment, the same applies except no leg cut because the obligation on the hand fell due to its loss; if after judgment, the cutter has no liability since it counts as part of the punishment. The commentary then explains that 'mashqooq' refers to the stolen item after tearing, and elaborates on the disagreement: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if the owner demands compensation for the decrease and takes the torn cloth, the thief is cut, but Abu Hanifa disagrees; if the owner demands full value and leaves the cloth, no amputation by agreement. The objection about the combination of cutting and guarantee is answered by noting that the guarantee for the decrease is due to a different injury (the destruction of part of the cloth), while cutting is for taking out the remainder. The rationale for ownership after extreme tearing is that the thief must pay the value and thus owns the guaranteed item, as established in the book of usurpation.

Key Terms
المشقوق (al-mashqooq): The torn piece of cloth that was stolen after being torn (by the thief) from inside the house.
خرق فاحش (kharq fahish): An extreme tearing that causes loss of some material and some benefit of the cloth; it leads to ownership by the tearer in Hanafi law.
نصاب السرقة (nisab al-sariqah): The minimum value of stolen property that necessitates hand amputation; according to Hanafi, it is 10 dirhams or 1 dinar or equivalent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, in which scenario is the thief NOT subject to amputation by agreement of all (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad)?
A) When the tearing is extreme and the owner takes the torn piece and claims compensation for the decrease.
B) When the tearing is minor (only defaces) and the torn piece reaches nisab.
C) When the tearing is extreme and the owner claims full value of the cloth and leaves the torn piece with the thief.
D) When the tearing is extreme and a third party cuts the thief's hand before litigation.

Key Takeaway
The hadd amputation for theft can be precluded by a shubha (doubt) created when the thief's extreme destruction of the stolen property gives him ownership of it through the guarantee he owes.

Stealing a Sheep, Slaughtering It Inside: Theft of Meat Exempts Amputation
Exception: no amputation for theft of meat via slaughter before removal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi exception to hand amputation when a sheep is slaughtered before removal and apply the timing test to determine amputation.

Core Idea
Slaughter inside transforms theft to meat; meat never triggers hand amputation.

This passage presents a critical distinction in the Hanafi law of theft (sariqa). The general rule is that theft of property of nisab from a guarded place triggers hand amputation. However, certain categories of property are excluded, including fresh meat. The matn gives a concrete example: a sheep stolen, slaughtered inside, then taken out — no cutting. The commentary clarifies the timing: if taken out alive first, then slaughtered outside, the theft is of the live animal, so amputation applies. Understanding this exception helps grasp the principle that the character of the stolen item at the moment the theft is completed determines the hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is stealing a sheep, slaughtering it inside the house, then taking the meat out. The ruling is: no hand amputation (la qat'a fih).
This is an exception to the general rule of amputation for theft of nisab from hirz.
2
Determine the object of theft
At the moment the theft is completed (taking out of hirz), the object is meat, not the live animal. Meat is among the categories for which amputation is not prescribed, as detailed earlier in the chapter.
The hadd depends on the type of property at the time of taking from hirz.
3
Apply the counterfactual
If the thief took the sheep out alive (reaching nisab), then slaughtered it outside, the theft is of the live animal at the moment of removal, so amputation is wajib, even if the value later drops due to slaughter.
Timing is decisive: slaughter before removal changes the object to meat.
4
Connect to the principle
The hadd is tied to the type of property at the time of taking from hirz. Meat, being perishable and not stored like valuable movable property, is excluded from the hadd. This exception is specific to items that are not considered protected property for hadd purposes.
The principle is based on the concept of hirz and the nature of the property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الا من سرق شاة ، فذبحها ، فأخرج ; لأنَّ السرقة تمت على اللحم ولا قطع فيه. قوله : فذبحها ; أي ذبحها قبل الإخراج ثمّ أخرجه من الدار، فلو أخرجها حية وهي بالغة مبلغ النصاب ثمّ ذبحها قطع ، وإن انتقصت قيمتها بالذبح. قوله : ولا قطع فيه ; فإنّ اللحم من الأشياء التي لا يجب القطع بسرقتها على ما مر تفصيله.
Except he who steals a sheep, then slaughters it, then takes it out; because the theft was completed on the meat, and there is no cutting in it. (Commentator's words): "Then slaughters it" meaning he slaughters it before taking it out, then takes it out of the house; if he took it out alive while it reaches the nisab, then slaughtered it, he is cut, even if its value decreased by slaughtering. (Commentator's words): "And there is no cutting in it" because meat is among the things for which amputation is not obligatory for stealing it, as previously detailed.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage provides an exception to the general rule of amputation for theft. The matn states that if someone steals a live sheep that reaches nisab, but slaughters it inside the house before taking it out, then takes the meat out, the thief is not subject to hand amputation. The reason is that the theft is now of meat, and meat is a type of property for which the hadd (amputation) is not prescribed. The commentary clarifies that the slaughter must occur before taking the sheep out; if the thief takes the sheep out alive (its value at nisab) and then slaughters it outside, he is cut because the theft was of a live animal of sufficient value. The decrease in value after slaughter does not affect the obligation. The rationale is that the hadd is for theft of property that is not subject to common ownership or such, and meat from slaughtered animals is considered less protected in terms of hadd, as detailed earlier in the chapter (e.g., it is perishable or not stored in the same manner).

Key Terms
اللحم (al-lahm): The meat of the slaughtered sheep; as a stolen item, it does not trigger hand amputation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The thief takes the sheep out alive, then slaughters it outside.
C) The thief takes the meat of a previously slaughtered sheep.
D) The thief slaughters it inside but the meat does not reach nisab.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, amputation for theft is not applied when the stolen item at the time of removal is meat, as exemplified by the case of stealing a sheep and slaughtering it before taking it out.
