Stealing Raw Gold/Silver and Making It into Dirhams/Dinars: Amputation and Return
Intra-Hanafi dispute on manufacturing stolen property and its effect on hadd and ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a thief who transforms stolen gold/silver into coins, explain the underlying principle of guarantee (daman) and ownership, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding copper utensils.

Core Idea
Transforming stolen gold into coins does not negate the hadd: substance remains the same, so thief is cut and coins returned.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents a nuanced scenario: what if a thief steals raw gold or silver and then mints it into coins? The act of manufacturing changes the form but not the essence. Abu Hanifa rules that amputation is wajib and the coins must be returned to the original owner. The passage then extends the analogy to other metals like copper and highlights a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) when the transformed item is sold by weight versus by count. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the Hanafi principle of 'al-badlan' (the two compensations) and how ownership transfers only upon payment of guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario and ruling
The thief steals raw gold/silver and makes it into coins. Abu Hanifa holds that the thief is cut (hadd) and the coins are returned to the owner. Note the underlying principle: the taking (akhdh) imposes guarantee (daman), not ownership.
Ownership for the thief arises only if he pays compensation, to avoid combining original and compensation in one hand.
2
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
The disagreement concerns other metals like copper. If stolen copper is made into utensils sold by weight, Abu Hanifa says the same ruling (cut and return). The Sahibayn disagree. If sold by count, all agree the thief owns the utensils because manufacturing changed the substance and name.
The distinction hinges on whether the manufactured item remains weight-based (like gold/silver) or becomes count-based, which alters its legal character (e.g., riba rulings).
3
Understand the principle of al-badlan
The objection was: after manufacturing, the thief owns the item, so he would be stealing his own property. The answer: the thief does not acquire ownership by the act of taking; he only becomes owner upon paying compensation. Otherwise, both the original (mubdal minhu) and the compensation (badal) would combine in the owner – which is impossible.
This principle prevents the original owner from having both the goods and their value simultaneously.
4
Apply the weight vs. count test
Gold and silver remain weight-based goods (mawzunat) even after being minted. Copper, when made into utensils, loses its weight-based status if sold by count, signifying a complete transformation of substance and name. Thus, for count-based copper utensils, the thief owns them and no hadd.
The change in riba ruling (from weight-based to count-based) is evidence of substantial transformation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
For gold/silver, Abu Hanifa's view is relied upon (mufta bihi). For copper utensils sold by weight, there is a legitimate ikhtilaf; Abu Hanifa's position may be preferred, but the Sahibayn's view is also considered. For count-based copper, all agree the thief owns it.
The tarjih authority is Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam and others.

ومن جعل ما سرق دراهم ، أو دنانير قطع وردت : هذا عند ابي حنيفة إخراجه بعد ملكه ولا قطع بسرقة ماله نفسه بل بسرقة مال غيره بخلاف ما إذا أخرج ثم شق ، فإنه وجد هناك التصرف بعد تمام السرقة. والجواب عنه : إن الأخذَ وُضِعَ سبباً للضمان لا للملك، وإنما الملك يثبتُ للسارق لأداء الضمان، فإذا اختار المالك تضمين نقصان الثوب وأخذه لم يلزم اجتماعُ البدلين في ملك واحد. قوله : لئلا يجتمع البدلان ; هذا على سبيل التغليب، وإلا فالشيء مبدل منه ، والضمان بدل، وحاصله : إنه لو لم يثبت الملك للسارق بعد أداء الضمان ، يلزمُ اجتماع المبدل منه : أي الثوب والبدل : أي ضمانه في ملك شخص واحد، وهو المالك القديم، وهو غير معقول عقلاً ونقلاً. قوله : ومن جعل ...الخ ; يعني إن سرق ذهباً أو فضة، ثم صنعه دراهم أو دنانير قطع فيه، وردّت تلك الدراهم والدنانير إلى المالك عنده ، وأما نحو النحاس لو جعله أواني بعد السرقة، فإن كان ذلك المصنوع يباع وزناً فكذلك عنده خلافاً لهما. وإن كان يباع عدداً فهي للسارق اتَّفاقاً ; لأنّ هذه الصنعة بدلت العين والاسم بدليل أنه تغيّر بها حكم الربا، حيث خرجت عن كونها موزونة، بخلاف الذهب والفضة لبقاء الاسم مع بقاء العين حكماً، كذا في «الفتح»)، وغيره.
And he who makes what he stole into dirhams or dinars is cut and (they) are returned: this according to Abu Hanifa is (considered) taking it out after ownership, and there is no cutting for stealing his own property but for stealing another's property, contrary to if he took it out then tore, for there the disposal occurred after the completion of the theft. And the answer to it: taking was made a cause for guarantee, not for ownership; ownership is established for the thief only for the sake of discharging the guarantee. So if the owner chooses to claim compensation for the decrease of the garment and takes it, there is no combination of two compensations in one ownership. (Commentator's words): "So that the two compensations do not combine" this is on the basis of overwhelming, otherwise the thing is the original and the guarantee is compensation; its summary: if ownership were not established for the thief after discharging the guarantee, there would result the combination of the original (the garment) and the compensation (its guarantee) in one ownership, which is the old owner, and that is not reasonable legally or rationally. (Commentator's words): "And he who makes... etc." meaning if he stole gold or silver, then made them into dirhams or dinars, he is cut for it, and those dirhams and dinars are returned to the owner according to him (Abu Hanifa). As for something like copper, if he made it into utensils after theft: if that manufactured item is sold by weight, then the same ruling applies according to him, contrary to the two companions. And if it is sold by count, it belongs to the thief by agreement, because this manufacturing changed the substance and the name, as evidenced by the change in the ruling of riba, since it leaves the category of weighed items, unlike gold and silver because the name remains with the substance in effect. As in al-Fath and others.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a thief steals raw gold or silver and then transforms it into minted coins (dirhams or dinars). According to Abu Hanifa, the thief is still subject to amputation (hand cut) and the newly made coins must be returned to the original owner. The reasoning is that the act of making coins does not change the substance's identity regarding the hadd; the thief is cutting someone else's property. The objection arises: if the thief now owns the transformed item (since he changed it), then he would be stealing his own property. The answer, as explained, is that the taking (of the raw material) imposes a guarantee (daman) on the thief, not ownership. Ownership is only established for the thief if he pays compensation, to avoid combining the original and compensation in one hand. The commentary then extends the analogy to other metals like copper: if stolen copper is made into utensils that are sold by weight, the same ruling applies (amputation and return) according to Abu Hanifa, but the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree. If the utensils are sold by count (not weight), then by agreement the thief owns them because the transformation completely changes the substance and its legal characteristics (e.g., riba rulings change). Gold and silver, however, remain weight-based even after minting, so their intrinsic like-kind nature persists.

Key Terms
البدلان (al-badlan): The two compensations: the original stolen item (mubdal minhu) and its guarantee (badal). The principle is that they should not combine in one ownership.
التصنيع (al-tasni'): Manufacturing or transformation of stolen property into a new form, affecting ownership and hadd rulings depending on whether the substance changes in the view of the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is correct regarding stolen copper made into utensils that are sold by weight?
A) Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree the thief is cut.
B) Abu Hanifa holds the thief is cut; the Sahibayn hold he is not.
C) By agreement, the thief owns the utensils.
D) The thief is not cut but the utensils are returned.

Key Takeaway
Transforming stolen gold/silver into coins does not prevent hadd or ownership transfer to the thief because the substance remains weight-based; the principle of al-badlan ensures that ownership only passes upon payment of guarantee.

Stealing a Sheep and Slaughtering It: No Amputation
Ruling on theft of sheep slaughtered after taking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft of a sheep slaughtered before exiting, explain the rational proof (istihsan) behind the thief's ownership for guarantee, and distinguish the Sahibayn's view.

Core Idea
Theft of meat does not trigger amputation; the thief's ownership is only to facilitate guarantee, not a true ownership that creates doubt.

This lesson examines a specific case in Hanafi hudud law: a thief steals a sheep, slaughters it, and then carries it out. The ruling is that no hadd cut applies. Why? Because the theft is considered complete on the meat, and meat is not subject to the cut. The commentators elaborate a key Hanafi principle: the thief's ownership is not real but a legal fiction to avoid combining two compensations in one ownership. We will unpack this proof and the intra-Hanafi difference with the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the exact scenario? A thief steals a sheep, slaughters it inside the owner's premises, then exits. The ruling: no amputation (cut).
This is the main ruling of the passage.
2
Note the exception
If the sheep is taken out alive and then slaughtered outside, amputation applies. The difference is the state of the item at the moment of exiting the custody.
The text contrasts: slaughter before exit vs after exit.
3
Understand the rational proof (istihsan)
The Hanafi school holds that taking (akhdh) is a cause for guarantee, not ownership. However, to avoid combining the original item and its substitute (badal) in one ownership, the thief is considered owner after paying the guarantee—but only for that purpose, not real ownership.
This prevents shubha that would otherwise block the cut.
4
Contrast with Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider taking as a cause of ownership, which could create shubha. But the Hanafi response shows even if ownership existed, it would be only for guarantee, so no real shubha.
The relied-upon position remains no cut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ومن جعل ما سرق دراهم، أو دنانير قطع وردت): هذا عند ابي حنيفة إخراجه بعد ملكه ولا قطع بسرقة ماله نفسه بل بسرقة مال غيره بخلاف ما إذا أخرج ثم شق، فإنه وجد هناك التصرف بعد تمام السرقة. والجواب عنه: إن الأخذَ وُضِعَ سبباً للضمان لا للملك، وإنما الملك يثبتُ للسارق لأداء الضمان، فإذا اختار المالك تضمين نقصان الثوب وأخذه لم يلزم اجتماعُ البدلين في ملك واحد. [11] قوله: لئلا يجتمع البدلان؛ هذا على سبيل التغليب، وإلا فالشيء مبدل منه، والضمان بدل، وحاصله: إنه لو لم يثبت الملك للسارق بعد أداء الضمان، يلزمُ اجتماع المبدل منه: أي الثوب والبدل: أي ضمانه في ملك شخص واحد، وهو المالك القديم، وهو غير معقول عقلاً ونقلاً. [3] قوله: ولا قطع فيه؛ فإنّ اللحم من الأشياء التي لا يجب القطع بسرقتها على ما مر تفصيله.
Not: he who steals a sheep, slaughters it, and brings it out, and he who makes what he stole into dirhams or dinars, he is cut and returned to them: because taking is not a cause for ownership, but we say ownership out of necessity for fulfilling the guarantee, so that two compensations are not combined in the ownership of one person, and the like does not create doubt. Except he who steals a sheep, slaughters it, and brings it out (then there is no cut), because the theft was completed on the meat, and there is no cut in it. (And he who makes what he stole into dirhams or dinars, he is cut and returned): This according to Abu Hanifa, after his ownership, and there is no cut for stealing his own property, but for stealing the property of others, contrary to if he brought it out and then cut, because there the disposal occurred after completion of the theft. The answer to it: Because taking was made a cause for guarantee, not for ownership, and ownership is established for the thief only to fulfill the guarantee. So if the owner chooses to hold him liable for the decrease of the garment and takes it, combining two compensations in one ownership does not occur. [11] His statement: 'so that two compensations are not combined'; this is by way of predominance, otherwise the thing is the replaced and the guarantee is the replacement. The summary is: if ownership were not established for the thief after fulfilling the guarantee, combining the replaced (i.e., the garment) and the replacement (i.e., its guarantee) in the ownership of one person would occur, namely the former owner, and this is inconceivable by reason and transmission. [2] His statement: 'slaughters it'; meaning he slaughters it before taking it out, then brings it out of the house. If he brings it out alive while it reaches the amount of nisab, then slaughters it, he is cut, even if its value decreases by slaughter. [3] His statement: 'and there is no cut in it'; because meat is among the things for which cutting is not obligatory for stealing it, as per the detailed explanation earlier.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding theft of a sheep slaughtered after taking. The matn states that if someone steals a sheep, slaughters it, and then brings it out, there is no amputation (cut) because the theft is considered complete on the meat, and meat is not a type of property that triggers the cut. The commentators explain that the taking is not a cause of ownership in itself; rather, ownership is established only to facilitate the guarantee (damān) owed by the thief to the owner. If the thief were not considered the owner after paying the guarantee, the past owner would combine both the original item and its compensation in his ownership, which is irrational and invalid. The sheep case is an exception because the theft is on the meat, which does not have a cut according to the school. The proof is rational necessity (darūra) to avoid combining two compensations. The dispute with the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is that they consider taking as a cause of ownership, leading to possible shubha (doubt) that prevents the cut. The Hanafi response shows that no shubha arises because the ownership is only for the guarantee's sake.

Key Terms
أخذ (akhdh): Taking physical possession of the stolen item from the owner's custody.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt arising from a semblance of ownership that may prevent the application of the ḥadd penalty.
بدلان (badalān): Two compensations: the original item (mubaddal) and its substitute (badal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the theft is considered complete on meat, which is not subject to the cut.
C) Because the thief owned the sheep after slaughter.
D) Because the owner forgave the thief.

Key Takeaway
Amputation for theft does not apply when the stolen item is transformed into meat before exiting the owner's custody, because meat is not subject to the hadd.

Rulings on Making Peace with Non-Muslims
Explaining the permissibility of peace treaties and the ruling on money received.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissibility of peace treaties with non-Muslims and explain the conditions and disposition of money taken.

Core Idea
Peace with non-Muslims is permissible when beneficial; money taken is booty or tax depending on timing.

This lesson explains the foundational ruling of the Hanafi school on making peace (sulh) with non-Muslims. Based on the Quranic command and the Prophet's example at Hudaybiyyah, the Imam may enter into a truce if it serves the Muslim interest. The lesson also covers how money received or given in such treaties is categorized, impacting its distribution.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on peace treaties
Identify that peace is permissible (mubah) and can become necessary if it prevents destruction.
The ruling is conditional on benefit.
2
Analyze the condition 'if it is better'
The Imam must judge that the peace brings overall benefit and does not lead to greater evil.
This is a condition for permissibility, not an obligation.
3
Identify the dalil
Quran 8:61 commands inclining to peace if the enemy inclines; the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah is the sunnah evidence.
The imperative indicates permission.
4
Distinguish the two scenarios for taking money
If money is taken before descending on enemy territory, it is like kharaj/jizyah; if after descending, it is ghanimah subject to khums.
The distinction affects distribution: booty is divided after khums, tax goes to public treasury.
5
Note the madhhab dispute
Some schools treat all such money as booty; the Hanafi distinction is relied upon.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is different allocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الموادعة ومن يجوز أمانه
وصولحوا إن خيراً، ويؤخذ منهم مال إن لنا به حاجة، ونُبذ
قوله: وصولحوا؛ أي يجوز الصلحُ مع الكفّار بلا مال، ومع مال يؤخذ منهم أو مال يعطيه الإمام لهم إن خاف الإمام الهلاك على نفسه وعلى المسلمين، كذا في «النهر»
قوله: إن خيراً؛ أي إن كان الصلح خيراً في رأي الإمام، لا إذا كان مفضياً إلى شرور، والأصل فيه قوله: وَإِن جَنَحُوا لِلسَّلْمِ فَأَجْنَحْ لَمَا وَتَوَكَّلْ عَلَى اللَّهِ، وقوله: وَلَا تَهِنُوا وَلَا تَحْزَنُوا وَأَنتُمُ الْأَعْلَوْنَ، وقد صالح النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بأهل مكة عام الحديبية سنة ست على أن يوضع الحرب بينه وبينهم عشر سنين، أخرجه أبو داود وأحمد وغيرهما.
قوله: ويؤخذ منهم مال؛ قال في «النهر»: ويصرف مصارف الخراج والجزية إن كان قبل النزول بساحتهم بلا رسول، أما إذا نزلنا بهم فهو غنيمة، نخمسها ونقسم الباقي.
قوله: ونبذ؛ بصيغة المجهول من النبذ - بالفتح -، وهو لغة: الطرح، والمراد به نقض العهد، قال الله: وَإِمَّا تَخَافَنَ مِن قَوْمٍ خِيَانَةً فَأَنْبِذْ إِلَيْهِمْ عَلَى سَوَاءٍ، وقد ثبت أنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نقض الصلح الذي كان بينه وبين كفار مكة حين غدروا وخانوا وقاتلهم سنة ثمان من الهجرة، فتح الله له على يديه مكة وما حولها، أخرجه البيهقي في دلائل النبوة، والطحاوي في «شرح معاني الآثار»
قوله: إن هو؛ أي إن كان النبذ أنفع؛ لأنّ المصلحة لما تبدلت كان النبذ جهاداً وإيفاء العهد ترك الجهاد صورة ومعنى، ولا بُدّ من إخبارهم بذلك تحرزاً عن الغدر، ولا بد من اعتبار مدة تبلغ فيها خبر النبذ إلى جميعهم، ويكتفي في ذلك بمضي مدة يتمكن فيها ملكهم بعد علمه بالنبذ من إيصال الخبر إلى أطراف مملكته.
وهذا بخلاف ما إذا كان نقض الصلح بناءً على خيانتهم وغدرهم لا ابتداء، فإنّه حينئذ لا يحتاج إلى إخبارهم بذلك؛ لأنهم صاروا ناقضين للعهد، فلا حاجة لنا إلى نقضه، حتى يحتاج إلى إخبارهم بذلك، كذا في «الهداية» وحواشيها.
قوله: مع إخبارهم بذلك؛ ظاهره: إنّ هذا القيد داخل في مفهوم النبذ، وإليه يشير قول المصنف: وقبل نبذ... الخ؛ لكن الذي يفهم من كثير من الكتب هو أن النبذ هو نقض الصلح فحسب، وإذا تعدّى بإلى تضمن معنى إيصال الخبر إليهم، كما في قوله تعالى: فَأَنْبِذْ إِلَيْهِمْ، وقد صرَّحَ بعضُهم بأنّ إخبارهم بذلك شرط لجواز النبذ في صورة عدم خيانتهم.
قوله: لو خانوا؛ بأن بدأ ملكهم القتال معنا أو بعض أتباعه بإذنه ورضاه، ولو قاتل بعضُهم بدون إذنه انتقض العهد في حق المقاتلين فقط، لا في حق غيرهم؛ لأنّ فعلهم لا يلزم غيرهم، هذا إذا كانت للمقاتلين منعة، فإن لم تكن لهم منعة لم يكن نقضاً للعهد مطلقاً، كذا قال الزيلعي في «شرح الكنز».
Chapter of Truce and Whose Security is Permissible
And make peace with them if it is better, and take money from them if we have need of it, and repudiate.
His saying: 'And make peace with them' means: it is permissible to make peace with the disbelievers without money, and with money taken from them or money given by the Imam to them if the Imam fears destruction for himself and the Muslims, as in 'al-Nahr'.
His saying: 'if it is better' means: if the peace is better in the Imam's opinion, not if it leads to evils. The basis for this is His saying: 'And if they incline to peace, then incline to it and rely upon Allah' (Qur'an 8:61) and His saying: 'And do not weaken and do not grieve, and you are the superior' (Qur'an 3:139). And the Prophet, peace be upon him, made peace with the people of Mecca in the year of Hudaybiyyah in the sixth year [AH] on the condition that war be suspended between him and them for ten years. Reported by Abu Dawud, Ahmad, and others.
His saying: 'And take money from them' – it is said in 'al-Nahr': 'It is spent on the expenditures of kharaj and jizyah if it is before descending upon their quarter without a messenger. But if we have descended upon them, it is booty, we take a fifth of it and divide the remainder.'
His saying: 'and repudiate' – in the passive form from 'al-nabd' (with fatha), which linguistically means casting away, and what is meant here is breaking the covenant. Allah said: 'And if you fear from a people betrayal, throw [their treaty] back to them with equality' (Qur'an 8:58). And it is established that the Prophet, peace be upon him, broke the peace that was between him and the disbelievers of Mecca when they betrayed and fought him in the year eight of the Hijrah, and Allah opened Mecca and its surroundings for him. Reported by al-Bayhaqi in 'Dala'il al-Nubuwwah' and al-Tahawi in 'Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar'.
His saying: 'if it is' means: if repudiation is more beneficial, because when the interest has changed, repudiation becomes jihad, and fulfilling the covenant would leave jihad in form and meaning. And it is necessary to inform them of that to avoid treachery, and it is necessary to consider a period in which the news of repudiation reaches all of them, and it is sufficient that a period passes in which their king, after knowing of the repudiation, can deliver the news to the extremities of his kingdom.
And this is contrary to the case where the breaking of the peace is due to their betrayal and treachery, not initiated by us. In that case, there is no need to inform them of that, because they have become breakers of the covenant, so we have no need to break it such that we need to inform them. As in 'al-Hidayah' and its glosses.
His saying: 'with informing them of that' – its apparent meaning is that this restriction is included in the concept of 'nabd', and the author's statement 'and repudiate...' indicates that. But what is understood from many books is that 'nabd' is simply the breaking of the peace, and when it is transitive with 'ila' it includes the meaning of conveying the news to them, as in His saying: 'fa-anbidh ilayhim' (so throw back to them). And some have explicitly stated that informing them is a condition for the permissibility of repudiation in the case where they have not betrayed.
His saying: 'if they betray' – meaning if their king initiates fighting with us, or some of his followers with his permission and approval. And if some of them fight without his permission, the treaty is broken only with respect to the fighters, not with respect to others, because their action does not bind others. This if the fighters have strength; if they do not have strength, it is not a breach of the covenant at all. As stated by al-Zayla'i in 'Sharh al-Kanz'.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah, explaining the chapter on truce (al-muwada'ah). It addresses three main issues: the permissibility of making peace with non-Muslims, the ruling on taking money from them, and the procedure for repudiating the treaty.

First, peace treaties are permissible (ja'iz) with or without exchange of money. The Imam may agree to give money to the enemy if he fears destruction for the Muslims. The Quranic basis is verse 8:61, which commands inclining to peace if the enemy does. However, the condition is that peace must be 'better' in the Imam's judgment—i.e., beneficial overall, not leading to greater harm. The precedent of the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah (6 AH) is cited, where the Prophet ﷺ agreed to a ten-year truce.

Second, if money is taken from the enemy in a peace agreement, its disposition depends on timing: if taken before descending upon their territory (i.e., before actual combat), it is treated like kharaj and jizyah (taxes from non-Muslims under Muslim rule) and spent on public needs. If taken after descending (i.e., after entering their land with force), it is booty (ghanimah) subject to khums (one-fifth for the state) and distribution among the fighters.

Third, the concept of 'nabd' (repudiation) is explained. Linguistically meaning 'to throw away,' it refers to breaking the treaty. The Quranic basis is verse 8:58. If the Muslims fear betrayal from the other party, they may repudiate the treaty, but they must first inform the enemy (by giving them notice) to avoid treachery. A reasonable time must be allowed for the news to reach all of them. However, if the enemy breaks the treaty first (by attacking), no notification is needed—they have already nullified it. The commentary also discusses who counts as breaking the treaty: if the king or his authorized followers attack, the entire treaty is broken; if unauthorized individuals attack, only they lose immunity, and the treaty remains binding on others (provided the fighters have significant strength; otherwise, even that is not a breach).

The discussion is purely Hanafi, drawing on the major references: al-Nahr al-Fa'iq, al-Hidayah, and Sharh al-Kanz. The authority weight is from the later commentary tradition, but the core rulings are based on primary texts.

Key Terms
موادعة (muwada'ah): Truce or peace treaty with non-Muslims for a specified period.
صلح (sulh): Peace agreement, including truce with non-Muslims, may involve exchange of money.
غنيمة (ghanimah): Booty taken from the enemy after a battle or descent on their territory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Imam must receive money
B) The peace must be beneficial and not lead to evil
C) The enemy must be weak
D) The treaty must be for ten years

Key Takeaway
Peace with non-Muslims is permissible (mubah) if it serves the Muslims' interest; money taken is classified based on the stage of conflict.

Repudiation of a Peace Treaty (Nabd)
Explaining the rulings on repudiating a peace treaty with non-Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on repudiating a peace treaty, including the requirement of notification and exceptions.

Core Idea
Repudiation requires prior notification unless the enemy already broke the treaty.

This lesson addresses the procedure for breaking a peace treaty with non-Muslims. Based on Quran 8:58 and the Prophet's example, the Hanafi school distinguishes between repudiation initiated by Muslims (which requires notice) and repudiation following the enemy's betrayal (which does not). The lesson also covers the extent of the breach when unauthorized individuals act.

Guided Path
1
Define 'nabd'
Linguistically meaning 'to throw away', it refers to repudiation of a treaty.
The Quran uses 'anbidh ilayhim' to mean 'throw back to them'.
2
Identify the dalil
Quran 8:58 and the Prophet's repudiation of the Hudaybiyyah treaty after Quraysh's betrayal.
The precedent shows both notification and the exception.
3
Distinguish two scenarios
Scenario 1: Muslims initiate repudiation due to fear of betrayal – notification is required. Scenario 2: Enemy breaks the treaty first – no notification needed.
The rationale: they already nullified it by their action.
4
Explain the notification procedure
A reasonable period must be allowed for the news to reach all enemy parties to avoid treachery.
The period is gauged by the king's ability to inform his realm.
5
Analyze the case of unauthorized fighters
If unauthorized individuals fight, only they lose immunity; the treaty remains binding on others unless they have strength.
This prevents the entire treaty from collapsing due to a few.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الموادعة ومن يجوز أمانه
وصولحوا إن خيراً، ويؤخذ منهم مال إن لنا به حاجة، ونُبذ
قوله: وصولحوا؛ أي يجوز الصلحُ مع الكفّار بلا مال، ومع مال يؤخذ منهم أو مال يعطيه الإمام لهم إن خاف الإمام الهلاك على نفسه وعلى المسلمين، كذا في «النهر»
قوله: إن خيراً؛ أي إن كان الصلح خيراً في رأي الإمام، لا إذا كان مفضياً إلى شرور، والأصل فيه قوله: وَإِن جَنَحُوا لِلسَّلْمِ فَأَجْنَحْ لَمَا وَتَوَكَّلْ عَلَى اللَّهِ، وقوله: وَلَا تَهِنُوا وَلَا تَحْزَنُوا وَأَنتُمُ الْأَعْلَوْنَ، وقد صالح النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بأهل مكة عام الحديبية سنة ست على أن يوضع الحرب بينه وبينهم عشر سنين، أخرجه أبو داود وأحمد وغيرهما.
قوله: ويؤخذ منهم مال؛ قال في «النهر»: ويصرف مصارف الخراج والجزية إن كان قبل النزول بساحتهم بلا رسول، أما إذا نزلنا بهم فهو غنيمة، نخمسها ونقسم الباقي.
قوله: ونبذ؛ بصيغة المجهول من النبذ - بالفتح -، وهو لغة: الطرح، والمراد به نقض العهد، قال الله: وَإِمَّا تَخَافَنَ مِن قَوْمٍ خِيَانَةً فَأَنْبِذْ إِلَيْهِمْ عَلَى سَوَاءٍ، وقد ثبت أنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نقض الصلح الذي كان بينه وبين كفار مكة حين غدروا وخانوا وقاتلهم سنة ثمان من الهجرة، فتح الله له على يديه مكة وما حولها، أخرجه البيهقي في دلائل النبوة، والطحاوي في «شرح معاني الآثار»
قوله: إن هو؛ أي إن كان النبذ أنفع؛ لأنّ المصلحة لما تبدلت كان النبذ جهاداً وإيفاء العهد ترك الجهاد صورة ومعنى، ولا بُدّ من إخبارهم بذلك تحرزاً عن الغدر، ولا بد من اعتبار مدة تبلغ فيها خبر النبذ إلى جميعهم، ويكتفي في ذلك بمضي مدة يتمكن فيها ملكهم بعد علمه بالنبذ من إيصال الخبر إلى أطراف مملكته.
وهذا بخلاف ما إذا كان نقض الصلح بناءً على خيانتهم وغدرهم لا ابتداء، فإنّه حينئذ لا يحتاج إلى إخبارهم بذلك؛ لأنهم صاروا ناقضين للعهد، فلا حاجة لنا إلى نقضه، حتى يحتاج إلى إخبارهم بذلك، كذا في «الهداية» وحواشيها.
قوله: مع إخبارهم بذلك؛ ظاهره: إنّ هذا القيد داخل في مفهوم النبذ، وإليه يشير قول المصنف: وقبل نبذ... الخ؛ لكن الذي يفهم من كثير من الكتب هو أن النبذ هو نقض الصلح فحسب، وإذا تعدّى بإلى تضمن معنى إيصال الخبر إليهم، كما في قوله تعالى: فَأَنْبِذْ إِلَيْهِمْ، وقد صرَّحَ بعضُهم بأنّ إخبارهم بذلك شرط لجواز النبذ في صورة عدم خيانتهم.

Chapter of Truce and Whose Security is Permissible
And make peace with them if it is better, and take money from them if we have need of it, and repudiate.
His saying: 'And make peace with them' means: it is permissible to make peace with the disbelievers without money, and with money taken from them or money given by the Imam to them if the Imam fears destruction for himself and the Muslims, as in 'al-Nahr'.
His saying: 'if it is better' means: if the peace is better in the Imam's opinion, not if it leads to evils. The basis for this is His saying: 'And if they incline to peace, then incline to it and rely upon Allah' (Qur'an 8:61) and His saying: 'And do not weaken and do not grieve, and you are the superior' (Qur'an 3:139). And the Prophet, peace be upon him, made peace with the people of Mecca in the year of Hudaybiyyah in the sixth year [AH] on the condition that war be suspended between him and them for ten years. Reported by Abu Dawud, Ahmad, and others.
His saying: 'And take money from them' – it is said in 'al-Nahr': 'It is spent on the expenditures of kharaj and jizyah if it is before descending upon their quarter without a messenger. But if we have descended upon them, it is booty, we take a fifth of it and divide the remainder.'
His saying: 'and repudiate' – in the passive form from 'al-nabd' (with fatha), which linguistically means casting away, and what is meant here is breaking the covenant. Allah said: 'And if you fear from a people betrayal, throw [their treaty] back to them with equality' (Qur'an 8:58). And it is established that the Prophet, peace be upon him, broke the peace that was between him and the disbelievers of Mecca when they betrayed and fought him in the year eight of the Hijrah, and Allah opened Mecca and its surroundings for him. Reported by al-Bayhaqi in 'Dala'il al-Nubuwwah' and al-Tahawi in 'Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar'.
His saying: 'if it is' means: if repudiation is more beneficial, because when the interest has changed, repudiation becomes jihad, and fulfilling the covenant would leave jihad in form and meaning. And it is necessary to inform them of that to avoid treachery, and it is necessary to consider a period in which the news of repudiation reaches all of them, and it is sufficient that a period passes in which their king, after knowing of the repudiation, can deliver the news to the extremities of his kingdom.
And this is contrary to the case where the breaking of the peace is due to their betrayal and treachery, not initiated by us. In that case, there is no need to inform them of that, because they have become breakers of the covenant, so we have no need to break it such that we need to inform them. As in 'al-Hidayah' and its glosses.
His saying: 'with informing them of that' – its apparent meaning is that this restriction is included in the concept of 'nabd', and the author's statement 'and repudiate...' indicates that. But what is understood from many books is that 'nabd' is simply the breaking of the peace, and when it is transitive with 'ila' it includes the meaning of conveying the news to them, as in His saying: 'fa-anbidh ilayhim' (so throw back to them). And some have explicitly stated that informing them is a condition for the permissibility of repudiation in the case where they have not betrayed.
His saying: 'if they betray' – meaning if their king initiates fighting with us, or some of his followers with his permission and approval. And if some of them fight without his permission, the treaty is broken only with respect to the fighters, not with respect to others, because their action does not bind others. This if the fighters have strength; if they do not have strength, it is not a breach of the covenant at all. As stated by al-Zayla'i in 'Sharh al-Kanz'.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah, explaining the chapter on truce (al-muwada'ah). It addresses three main issues: the permissibility of making peace with non-Muslims, the ruling on taking money from them, and the procedure for repudiating the treaty.

First, peace treaties are permissible (ja'iz) with or without exchange of money. The Imam may agree to give money to the enemy if he fears destruction for the Muslims. The Quranic basis is verse 8:61, which commands inclining to peace if the enemy does. However, the condition is that peace must be 'better' in the Imam's judgment—i.e., beneficial overall, not leading to greater harm. The precedent of the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah (6 AH) is cited, where the Prophet ﷺ agreed to a ten-year truce.

Second, if money is taken from the enemy in a peace agreement, its disposition depends on timing: if taken before descending upon their territory (i.e., before actual combat), it is treated like kharaj and jizyah (taxes from non-Muslims under Muslim rule) and spent on public needs. If taken after descending (i.e., after entering their land with force), it is booty (ghanimah) subject to khums (one-fifth for the state) and distribution among the fighters.

Third, the concept of 'nabd' (repudiation) is explained. Linguistically meaning 'to throw away,' it refers to breaking the treaty. The Quranic basis is verse 8:58. If the Muslims fear betrayal from the other party, they may repudiate the treaty, but they must first inform the enemy (by giving them notice) to avoid treachery. A reasonable time must be allowed for the news to reach all of them. However, if the enemy breaks the treaty first (by attacking), no notification is needed—they have already nullified it. The commentary also discusses who counts as breaking the treaty: if the king or his authorized followers attack, the entire treaty is broken; if unauthorized individuals attack, only they lose immunity, and the treaty remains binding on others (provided the fighters have significant strength; otherwise, even that is not a breach).

The discussion is purely Hanafi, drawing on the major references: al-Nahr al-Fa'iq, al-Hidayah, and Sharh al-Kanz. The authority weight is from the later commentary tradition, but the core rulings are based on primary texts.

Key Terms
نبذ (nabd): Repudiation of a treaty, with notification to the other party.
نقض العهد (naqd al-'ahd): Breaking or violation of a covenant/treaty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the Hanafi school NOT require notification before repudiating a treaty?
A) When the Imam fears betrayal
B) When the enemy has already broken the treaty by attacking
C) When the treaty has no fixed term
D) When the enemy is weak

Key Takeaway
Repudiation of a treaty requires prior notice if initiated by Muslims but not if the enemy already betrayed it.

Protection of the Convert's Person and Minor Child
Explains property and family protections for a convert in Dar al-Harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which persons are protected for a convert in Dar al-Harb and identify the condition of actual possession for movable property.

Core Idea
A convert protects himself and his minor child; his property with him or a trustee is also protected.

When a person accepts Islam in enemy territory (Dar al-Harb), his new faith grants him protection from being killed or enslaved. However, that protection does not automatically extend to all his family and property. This lesson examines the categories that are protected, focusing on the principles of followership (taba'iyyah) and actual possession (yad haqiqa).

Guided Path
1
Identify the location of conversion
The rule applies specifically to a person who accepts Islam in Dar al-Harb (thamma). If he converts in Dar al-Islam, different rules apply due to the difference of abodes.
Thamma is a key technical term in this passage.
2
Determine who is protected: self and minor child
The convert himself is protected (ma'sum) because Islam precludes initial enslavement. His minor child, whether discerning or not, follows him in protection (taba'iyyah).
The text says 'child' means non-adult (ghayr ba'i') irrespective of tamayyuz.
3
Determine the protected property
Only movable property that is physically with the convert or entrusted to a protected person (Muslim or dhimmi) is protected. Property in the hands of a hostile (harbi) is not protected.
The condition is actual custody or trusted custody (yad amin ma'sum).
4
Recognize the limiting principle
Protection is based on the idea that the convert has real possession. Land and property under enemy control are not in his true possession and thus become fay'.
This principle leads to the intra-madhhab dispute on land.

[5] His statement: 'and his child' – i.e., the non-adult child, even if discerning. If he takes his child and property before his Islam, he only protects himself. Likewise his wife, and likewise her pregnancy because it is part of her, so it is enslaved with her. Likewise his fighting slave and his slave woman and her pregnancy; because when he rebelled against his master, he left his hand and became a follower of the people of his abode, so his ruling is their ruling. Likewise his property with a hostile by usurpation or deposit, because the hostile is not protected. Likewise his land, because it is in the hands of the people of Dar al-Harb, as stated in al-Hidayah. In the view of Muhammad, and it is the first view of Abu Yusuf, it is like other property, based on the fact that (true) possession of land is not established according to them (Abu Hanifa and later Abu Yusuf), while according to Muhammad it is established, as stated in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Al-Wiqayah regarding the rules of spoils (ghanimah). It addresses a specific scenario: a person who embraces Islam while still in Dar al-Harb (enemy territory). The discussion distinguishes between what is protected (ma'sum) for such a convert and what becomes fay' (spoils for the Muslim army) if the Muslims later conquer that territory. The passage begins by explaining two general principles: first, that certain items (like food and drink) may be used out of necessity, but not with the intention of acquiring wealth (tamawwul); second, any surplus must be returned to the general spoils after the need ceases. The core of the passage then details the convert's protection: he himself is protected from killing and enslavement because Islam precludes initial enslavement (though if captured before Islam, he becomes fay'). His minor child (non-adult) also follows him in protection. His property that is physically with him or entrusted to a protected person (Muslim or dhimmi) is also protected, but property in the hands of a hostile (harbi) is fay'. On the other hand, certain categories are not protected: his adult child (who remains a harbi), his wife and her pregnancy (she is a harbiyya and the pregnancy follows her), his fighting slave (who has joined the enemy), his land (because land is considered part of Dar al-Harb and is in enemy control), and his property that is with a hostile as a usurpation (ghasb) or deposit (wadi'a). The passage then mentions a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding land: al-Shafi'i considers the convert's land like movable property because it is in his hand, but the Hanafi position (as stated) is that land remains in the hand of the enemy rulers, so it is not truly in the convert's possession. Within the Hanafi school, there is ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and the later view of Abu Yusuf hold that land is not protected (it is fay'), while Muhammad and the early view of Abu Yusuf consider it protected like other movable property. This dispute hinges on whether 'hand' (yad) over land is considered true possession. Additionally, the passage notes an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding property that was usurped (ghasb) and is now in the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi: according to Abu Hanifa, such property is still fay' because the original usurpation by a harbi made it fay', and the Muslim or dhimmi's hand does not restore protection; but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, it is protected because the current holder is protected.

Key Terms
ثمة (thamma): In Dar al-Harb (enemy territory), as opposed to accepting Islam in Dar al-Islam.
عصم (asama): To secure protection from killing or enslavement, and to safeguard property from being taken as spoils.
تمول (tamawwul): The intention to acquire and store something as personal wealth, beyond immediate necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is protected for a convert in Dar al-Harb?
A) His adult son
B) His wife
C) His minor child
D) His land

Key Takeaway
A convert's own person and his minor child are automatically protected; movable property in his hand or with a trustee is also protected.

Non-Protected Categories and the Land Dispute
Explains property and family protections for a convert in Dar al-Harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the categories that are not protected for a convert in Dar al-Harb and explain the Hanafi intra-madhhab reasoning regarding land.

Core Idea
Adult children, wives, land, fighting slaves, and property in enemy hands are not protected; land's status is disputed within the Hanafi school.

While the convert himself and his minor child are protected, several other relationships and assets are not. This lesson categorizes the exceptions and analyzes the reasoning behind each, with a special focus on the land dispute between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute illuminates the Hanafi method of analogical reasoning (qiyas) and the role of 'yad' (possession) in determining ownership.

Guided Path
1
List the non-protected categories
From the text, identify: adult child, wife and her pregnancy, land (real estate), fighting slave, and property usurped or deposited with a harbi.
Note that each category has a specific rationale.
2
Analyze the rationale for each
Adult child: is a harbi and does not follow his parent. Wife: remains a harbiyya. Land: is in enemy hands. Fighting slave: has rebelled and left his master's hand. Property with harbi: because the harbi is not protected.
The unifying theme is that all these elements are tied to Dar al-Harb or have no protected possession.
3
Focus on land: the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that land is protected because it is in the convert's hand. The Hanafi response: land is in the hands of the enemy rulers and the convert does not have true possession (yad haqiqa) over it.
This dispute has a clear practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on land
Abu Hanifa and the later view of Abu Yusuf: land is fay'. Muhammad and the early Abu Yusuf: land is protected like movable property. The difference turns on whether 'yad haqiqa' is possible over land.
This shows how a difference in usul (definition of 'hand') leads to different fiqh rulings.
5
Understand the practical outcome
When Muslim army conquers, under the majority Hanafi view, land becomes fay' (part of conquered land). Under the minority view, it remains the convert's property. In practice, the majority view prevailed in later Hanafi law.
The text does not give a tarjih, but later authorities may adopt one.

Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Al-Wiqayah regarding the rules of spoils (ghanimah). It addresses a specific scenario: a person who embraces Islam while still in Dar al-Harb (enemy territory). The discussion distinguishes between what is protected (ma'sum) for such a convert and what becomes fay' (spoils for the Muslim army) if the Muslims later conquer that territory. The passage begins by explaining two general principles: first, that certain items (like food and drink) may be used out of necessity, but not with the intention of acquiring wealth (tamawwul); second, any surplus must be returned to the general spoils after the need ceases. The core of the passage then details the convert's protection: he himself is protected from killing and enslavement because Islam precludes initial enslavement (though if captured before Islam, he becomes fay'). His minor child (non-adult) also follows him in protection. His property that is physically with him or entrusted to a protected person (Muslim or dhimmi) is also protected, but property in the hands of a hostile (harbi) is fay'. On the other hand, certain categories are not protected: his adult child (who remains a harbi), his wife and her pregnancy (she is a harbiyya and the pregnancy follows her), his fighting slave (who has joined the enemy), his land (because land is considered part of Dar al-Harb and is in enemy control), and his property that is with a hostile as a usurpation (ghasb) or deposit (wadi'a). The passage then mentions a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding land: al-Shafi'i considers the convert's land like movable property because it is in his hand, but the Hanafi position (as stated) is that land remains in the hand of the enemy rulers, so it is not truly in the convert's possession. Within the Hanafi school, there is ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and the later view of Abu Yusuf hold that land is not protected (it is fay'), while Muhammad and the early view of Abu Yusuf consider it protected like other movable property. This dispute hinges on whether 'hand' (yad) over land is considered true possession. Additionally, the passage notes an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding property that was usurped (ghasb) and is now in the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi: according to Abu Hanifa, such property is still fay' because the original usurpation by a harbi made it fay', and the Muslim or dhimmi's hand does not restore protection; but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, it is protected because the current holder is protected.

Key Terms
فَيْء (fay'): Spoils taken from non-Muslims without a military campaign, or property that becomes state property upon conquest, as opposed to ghanimah which requires battle. Here, it refers to property that is not protected and becomes part of the conquered land.
يد حقيقة (yad haqiqa): True or actual possession, characterized by physical control and custody, as opposed to mere legal ownership (milk).
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; taking someone's property wrongfully. In this context, property that was usurped by a harbi and remains in a harbi's hand is not protected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The land is protected and remains the convert's property.
B) The land becomes fay' (spoils) because it is in the hands of the enemy.
C) The land is distributed only among the horsemen.
D) The land is given to the convert's adult son.

Key Takeaway
Land of a convert in Dar al-Harb is not protected and becomes fay', though the issue is disputed within the Hanafi school; other non-protected categories are settled.

Share of Dhawi al-Qurba in Khums After the Prophet's Death
Dispute on continuation of Dhawi al-Qurba's share in khums after Prophet's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the continuation of the share of Dhawi al-Qurba in khums after the Prophet's death and explain the evidence from the practice of the Caliphs.

Core Idea
The share of Dhawi al-Qurba in khums ended with the Prophet's death; it is not an ongoing obligation.

This lesson examines a significant dispute in Islamic law regarding the distribution of khums (one-fifth of spoils). After the Prophet's death, the question arose whether the share allocated to his near relatives (Dhawi al-Qurba) continued as an obligation. We will explore the Hanafi position—supported by the practice of the first Caliphs—and the opposing view, analyzing the proofs from the Quran, Sunnah, and the consensus of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What is the ruling of the Hanafi school regarding the share of Dhawi al-Qurba after the Prophet's death? Is it still wajib or has it ceased?
The Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifah, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree it has ceased.
2
Identify the opposing view
Which scholars hold that the share continues? What is their basis?
Some early and later scholars argue based on the general wording of Quran 8:41.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the evidence they present: the Prophet gave to rich relatives like al-Abbas, so poverty is not the condition. Also the verse is general.
This suggests the entitlement is not conditional on poverty.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the opponent's argument? They cite the practice of Abu Bakr and Umar who restricted the share to the poor, and the linguistic argument that the share was tied to the Prophet's messengership.
The Hanafi response uses the principle that the description 'Messenger' is a derived term that ceases with the person.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? In the Hanafi view, the khums share for Dhawi al-Qurba is not set aside but goes to the general treasury. In the opposing view, it must be given to descendants of Hashim and Muttalib.
This affects the distribution of spoils in contemporary contexts.

وثانيها : إنه لو كان استحقاقهم للفقر لكان حرم عليهم كما حرمت عليهم الصدقة. وجوابه: إن خمس الخمس ليس في حكم الصدقة من كل وجه ; ولذا جعل ي أيضاً خمس الخمس مع حرمة الصدقة عليه، فلا يلزم من حرمة الصدقة عليهم كون خمس الخمس حراما عليهم.
وثالثها : إنه لو كان استحقاقهم للفقرِ لحرمَ إعطاؤه للغني منهم ، وهو خلاف ما ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عند الطحاوي وغيره من أنه كان يعطي العباس له ، وكان من مياسير الصحابة . وأجيب عنه : بأن النبي الله وإن كان يقسم خمس الخمس لأغنياء ذوي القربي أيضاً، لكن أبا بكر وعمر لم يعطيا إلا للفقراء منهم، كما أخرجه الطحاوي وغيره ، وكان ذلك بمحضر من ذوي القربى وغيرهم من الصحابة ، فصار ذلك بمنزلة الإجماع على أن استحقاقهم بعد النبي بالفقر. وفيه : إنّ هذا كان اجتهاداً ورأياً رأياه ، وقد خالفه علي الله وغيره كما أخرجه الطحاوي وغيره، فلا إجماع هاهنا على أن المذكورين في الآية إنما هم مصارف ، فيكفي الصرفُ إلى واحد منها، فلا يلزمُ من صرفِ الخلفاء الخمس إلى غيرهم مـن المصارف وعدم صرفهم إلى أغنيائهم لمصلحة فقراء المسلمين بطلان استحقاقهم ، وكونهم إنما يستحقون بالفقر.
وفي «شرح معاني الآثار» بعد حكاية الأقوال المختلفة مع ما لها وما عليها : «كان سهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جارياً في حياته وانقطع بموته، وكان سهم اليتامى وابن السبيل بعد وفاة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كما كان قبل ذلك.
ثم اختلفوا في سهم ذوي القربى، فقال قوم: هو لهم كما كان لهم في حياته ، وقال قوم: قد انقطع عنهم بموته، وكان الله قد جمع كل قرابة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في قوله : ( وَلِذِي الْقُرْبَى ) ، فلم يخص أحداً منهم دون أحد.

Book of Jihad / Chapter of Spoils and its Division
Second: If their entitlement was due to poverty, then it would be prohibited for them just as charity is prohibited. The answer: Indeed, the fifth of the fifth is not entirely in the ruling of charity; therefore, the fifth of the fifth was also made permissible despite the prohibition of charity upon him, so the prohibition of charity upon them does not necessitate that the fifth of the fifth is prohibited for them.
Third: If their entitlement was due to poverty, then giving it to the rich among them would be prohibited, and that contradicts what is established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) according to al-Tahawi and others, that he used to give to al-Abbas, and he was among the wealthy companions. It was answered: The Prophet (peace be upon him) used to distribute the fifth of the fifth to the rich of Dhawi al-Qurba as well, but Abu Bakr and Umar did not give except to the poor among them, as narrated by al-Tahawi and others, and that was in the presence of Dhawi al-Qurba and other companions, so that became analogous to consensus that their entitlement after the Prophet is based on poverty. Objection: This was an ijtihad and an opinion they held, and Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and others opposed them as narrated by al-Tahawi and others, so there is no consensus here. That those mentioned in the verse are only categories of recipients (masarif), so it suffices to give to one of them, and the caliphs' giving the fifth to other categories and not giving to their rich for the benefit of the poor Muslims does not necessitate the invalidity of their entitlement and that they are only entitled due to poverty.
In 'Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar', after narrating the different opinions with their pros and cons: 'The share of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was current during his lifetime and ceased with his death, and the share of orphans and the wayfarer remained after the death of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) as it was before. Then they differed regarding the share of Dhawi al-Qurba. Some said: It is for them as it was in his lifetime. Others said: It has ceased with his death. And Allah had gathered all the relatives of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in His statement: 'And for Dhawi al-Qurba' (Anfal 41), and He did not single out anyone over another. And nothing for their rich, and the mention of Allah is for blessing. The share of the Prophet (peace be upon him) ceased with his death. Then the Prophet (peace be upon him) distributed that share specifically to Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, and prohibited Banu Umayyah and Banu Nawfal. Thus it is established that that share was for the Prophet (peace be upon him), so he placed it among whichever relatives he wished. Therefore, its ruling became the ruling of his share that he used to select for himself. Just as that share ended with his death and was not obligatory for anyone after him, this also ended with his death and is not obligatory for anyone after him. This is the saying of Abu Hanifah, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them). End.'
His statement: 'for their rich' is supported by the saying of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz (may Allah be pleased with him), one of the rightly guided caliphs, in a long statement as narrated by al-Tahawi. Whoever wishes to see it, let him refer to it.
His statement: 'for blessing' is supported by the saying of al-Hasan ibn Ali ibn Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah: 'For Allah is its fifth' (Anfal 41), this is the key to the speech: to Allah belongs the dominion of the heavens and the earth, and to the Messenger and to Dhawi al-Qurba. They differed after the death of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him). Some said: The share of Dhawi al-Qurba is for the relatives of the caliph. Others said: The share of the Prophet is for the caliph after him. The companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) agreed to place these two shares in horses and weaponry in the path of Allah, and so it was during the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar (may Allah be pleased with them). Narrated by Abd al-Razzaq, Ibn Abi Shaybah, al-Tahawi, Ibn Abi Hatim, Ibn al-Mundhir, al-Hakim, and others. Ibn Abbas said: His statement: 'For Allah is its fifth' is the key to the speech: to Allah belongs what is in the heavens and what is on earth. The share of Allah and the Messenger are one. Narrated by Ibn Jarir, al-Tabarani, Ibn Mardawayh, Abu al-Shaykh, and others.
His statement: 'ceased with his death' because it is attached to a derived form, so the source of derivation is the cause for it. Thus the meaning is: for the Messenger due to his messengership, and no one after him is described with this description, so no one else deserves it. And those who argued that his messengership ended with his death are mistaken, for messengership does not end with death, and likewise wilayah and all religious honors. How so? The prophets are alive in their graves.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah deals with the dispute regarding the share of Dhawi al-Qurba (the near relatives of the Prophet) from the khums (one-fifth of spoils) after the death of the Prophet (peace be upon him). The passage is a commentary expanding on a previous matn, analyzing arguments for and against the continuation of this share. The author presents two objections against the claim that their entitlement is based on poverty, and then gives the Hanafi position. The first objection is that if the entitlement was due to poverty, then khums would be prohibited for them like charity. The answer distinguishes khums from charity. The second objection cites the Prophet giving to rich relatives, implying entitlement is not based on poverty. The answer cites the practice of Abu Bakr and Umar who restricted it to the poor, suggesting a consensus. But the author notes this was ijtihad, not consensus. The text then quotes from 'Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar' to summarize the dispute: some say the share continues, others say it ceased. The Hanafi position is that the share ceased with the Prophet's death, as it was tied to his prophethood. The passage also includes footnotes supporting this view with statements from early authorities. The final authority is the Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifah, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The commentary emphasizes that the share was specific to the Prophet; after his death, it is no longer wajib for anyone.

Key Terms
خمس (khums): One-fifth of the spoils of war that is to be divided according to Quran 8:41 among Allah, the Messenger, Dhawi al-Qurba, orphans, and wayfarers.
ذوو القربى (Dhawi al-Qurba): The near relatives of the Prophet (peace be upon him), specifically Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib according to the Prophet's own distribution.
سهم (sahm): A share or portion of the khums allocated to a specific category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happened to the share of Dhawi al-Qurba from khums after the death of the Prophet (peace be upon him)?
A) It continued to be wajib for them as during his lifetime.
B) It ceased to be wajib and became part of the general treasury.
C) It was given to the caliphs.
D) It was distributed among all Muslims equally.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that the share of Dhawi al-Qurba in khums ceased with the Prophet's death, based on the practice of the first Caliphs and the linguistic link to his prophethood.

Ruling on a Slave Who Converts and Joins Muslims
Explaining the ruling of a slave who escapes from Dar al-Harb to Dar al-Islam as a Muslim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave who converts to Islam and escapes to Muslim territory, and distinguish the scenarios where the slave becomes free versus when he does not.

Core Idea
A slave who escapes to Muslims in defiance of his master becomes free; one who leaves with permission does not.

This lesson examines a classical Hanafi ruling regarding a wartime slave who converts to Islam and joins the Muslim community. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the conditions under which such a slave becomes free, based on the hadith of the Prophet ﷺ. It distinguishes between coming out in defiance of the master (muraghaman) and coming out with permission, producing different legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Recognize the scenario: a slave from Dar al-Harb converts and joins Muslims by coming to Dar al-Islam or joining the Muslim army.
The slave must act in defiance of his master.
2
Determine the ruling
The slave becomes free (عَتِقَ) if he comes out in defiance (muraghaman).
Freedom is immediate upon joining Muslims.
3
Examine the dalil
The Prophet ﷺ said of the slaves from Ta'if: 'They are the freed ones of Allah.' This hadith is the primary evidence.
Reported by al-Bayhaqi and others.
4
Understand the legal reasoning
The slave takes possession of himself (ahraza nafsahu) by coming out, thus he becomes free.
Ihraz is a means of acquiring ownership over movable property.
5
Note the exception
If the slave left with his master's permission (e.g., for trade), he does not become free; the Imam sells him and returns the price to the master.
This distinguishes defiance from permitted travel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(كعبد لهم أسلم ثمة فجاءنا ، أو ظهرنا عليهم الإزالة عليهم، وإنما يملكونه بالإحراز بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإنهم ملكوه بالشراء، فاستحق الإزالة عليهم بإقامة شرط الزوال مقام السبب.
[ ١ ] قوله : كعبد ؛ أي عُتِق عبد أسلم فجاءنا في دار الإسلام أو لحق عسكرنا بدار الحرب، أو غلبنا على دارهم ؛ وذلك لأنه أحرز نفسه بالخروج إلينا مراغماً لمولاه في الصورة الأولى والثانية ، أو بالالتحاق بمنعة المسلمين في الثالثة.
والأصل فيه : أنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لَمَّا حاصر الطائف خرج إليهم من عبيدهم أبو بكرة الثقفي له وغيره مسلمين ، فقال النبي : «هم عتقاء الله )، أخرجه البيهقي وغيره.
[٢] قوله : ثمة ؛ أي في دار الحرب، وهذا قيد اتفاقي ، فإنه لو خرج مراغماً لمولاه فأسلم في دارنا، فالحكم كذلك بخلاف ما إذا خرج بإذن مولاه أو لحاجته بأمره فأسلم عندنا، فإن حكمه أن يبيعه الإمام ويحفظ ثمنه لمولاه الحربي، كذا في «البحر»).
(Like a slave of theirs who converts to Islam there and comes to us, or we overcome them – the removal (of ownership) is upon them. They only own him by taking possession (ihraz), unlike our case, for they owned him by purchase, so removal of ownership is established by placing the condition of removal in place of the cause.)
[1] His statement: "Like a slave" – meaning a slave is freed who converts to Islam and comes to us in Dar al-Islam, or joins our army in Dar al-Harb, or we conquer their territory. This is because he has taken possession of himself by coming out to us in defiance of his master in the first two scenarios, or by joining the Muslim force in the third.
The basis for this is that when the Prophet ﷺ besieged Ta'if, Abu Bakra al-Thaqafi and others among their slaves came out to them as Muslims. The Prophet ﷺ said: "They are the freed ones of Allah." Reported by al-Bayhaqi and others.
[2] His statement: "There" – meaning in Dar al-Harb. This is a contingent condition. For if he comes out in defiance of his master and converts in our territory, the same ruling applies. But if he comes out with his master's permission or for his needs by his command and then converts among us, the ruling is that the Imam sells him and preserves his price for his warring master. Thus in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the ruling of a slave who converts to Islam and comes to Muslim territory. The text establishes that such a slave becomes free (عَتِقَ). The reasoning is that by coming to Muslims in defiance of his master, the slave effectively takes possession of himself (أحرز نفسه). The proof is the hadith of Abu Bakra al-Thaqafi and others who came out from Ta'if to the Prophet ﷺ as Muslims, and the Prophet declared them 'the freed ones of Allah' – meaning they are free by virtue of their conversion and joining the Muslims. However, there is a condition: if the slave came out with his master's permission (e.g., for trade) and then converted, he does not become free; rather, the Imam sells him and returns the price to his warring master.

Key Terms
عتقاء الله (utqa' Allah): The freed ones of Allah; slaves who become free by converting to Islam and joining the Muslim community without their master's permission.
مراغماً (muraghaman): In defiance of his master; leaving the master's authority without permission and with the intention of joining the Muslims.
إحراز (ihraz): Taking possession; here it means the slave takes possession of himself by escaping to Muslim territory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a slave who, with his master's permission, leaves Dar al-Harb for trade, then converts to Islam and stays in Dar al-Islam?
A) He becomes free immediately.
B) He is returned to his master.
C) The Imam sells him and gives the price to his master.
D) He remains a slave but is not returned.

Key Takeaway
A slave who defiantly joins the Muslims after conversion becomes free; one who leaves with permission does not.

The Ruling on Harming a Musta'min: Prohibition and Exception
Stating the ruling of not harming a musta'min's life and property unless the enemy ruler breaches the covenant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on harming a musta'min, identify the exception when it becomes permissible, and distinguish it from the case of a captive.

Core Idea
Harming a musta'min is treachery (ghadr) forbidden by text; only permitted when the enemy initiates the breach.

This passage from the chapter on the musta'min establishes a key principle in Islamic international law: a person granted safe conduct (aman) is inviolable. The ruling covers both Muslims entering enemy territory and non-Muslims entering Muslim lands. The text then provides a critical exception—if the enemy ruler or his authority breaks the covenant first, the protection lifts. Understanding this exception is crucial to avoid falling into treachery oneself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is harming a musta'min's life, property, or honor. The ruling is that it is haram (forbidden).
The musta'min includes both a Muslim entering Dar al-Harb with aman and a harbi entering Dar al-Islam with aman.
2
Understand the basis of prohibition
Harming is treachery (ghadr) because aman creates a guarantee of safety. Ghadr is prohibited by a clear scriptural text.
The text distinguishes between this and legitimate deception in war.
3
Note the exception
If the enemy ruler or his deputy (with his knowledge) first commits treachery against the musta'min—by imprisoning him, taking his property, or similar—then it becomes permissible for the musta'min to harm them.
The covenant is broken from their side, so the prohibition lifts.
4
Contrast with a captive
A Muslim captive in Dar al-Harb has no aman, so no covenant exists. Therefore, tte chery (ghadr) is not prohibited; he may kill or take property if able.
This highlights the binding nature of the covenant for those under aman.
5
Apply the sub-rule on taking property
If the musta'min takes property from the enemy after the breach, he must physically remove it from their territory to own it. Even then, ownership is corrupt (mal khabith), so a female slave cannot be used for intercourse or sold for that purpose.
This condition ('what he has taken out') is an additional detail from the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المستأمن
لا يتعرض تاجرنا ئمة لدمِهم ومالهم إلا إذا أخذَ مَلِكُهم ماله ، أو حبسه ، أو غيره بعلمه، وما أخرجه
باب المستأمن
هو يشمل مسلما دخل دارهم بأمان، وكافراً دخل دارنا بأمان. (لا يتعرض) تاجرنا ئمة لديهم ومالهم" إلا إذا أَخذَ مَلِكُهم ماله ، أو حبسه ، أو غيره بعلمه ، وما أخرجه
[ 1 ] قوله : لا يتعرّض ؛ أَي حَرُمَ تعرُّضُ تاجرنا الذي دخل دارهم بأمان لشيء من دم ومال وفرج ؛ لأنه ضُمِنَ بالاستئمان أن لا يتعرّض لهم بالقتل وإهلاك المال وغير ذلك، فيكون التعرض غدراً، وهو ممنوع بالنصّ على ما ذكره مع الفرق بينه وبين الخداع في الحرب. نعم ؛ إذا غدر به سلطانهم أو غيره بإذنه بالحبس أو أخذ المال أو نحو ذلك، فإنه يجوز له حينئذ التعرُّضُ ؛ لأن نقض العهد حينئذ من جانبهم، وهذا بخلاف الأسير في دار الحرب، فإنّ الغدر ليس بحرام عليه ؛ لأنه ليس بينه وبينهم تعامل التوافق ، فلو تمكن الأسير من قتلهم أو أخذ مالهم فعل، كذا في «النهاية».
[٢] قوله ومالهم ؛ بخلاف ذراري المسلمين المأسورين عندهم، فإن له تخليصهم من أيديهم إذا قدر، كذا في البحر».
[٣] قوله : إلا ؛ استثناء من قوله : «لا يتعرّض» ؛ أي يحرم عليه التعرّض في جميع الأحوال إلا حال ما ابتدأ الغدر منهم.
(٤) قوله : وما أخرجه ؛ قيد بالإخراج ؛ لأنه لو غصب شيئاً ولم يخرجه وجب ردُّهُ عليهم للغدر، وبعد الإخراج يملكه ملكاً خبيثاً لحصوله بالغدر حتى لو أخرج جارية لا يحل له وطؤها ولا للمشتري منه.
Chapter of the Musta'min
Our merchant shall not harm their lives and property unless their king takes his property, or imprisons him, or other similar actions with his knowledge, and what he has taken out.
Chapter of the Musta'min
It includes a Muslim who enters their territory with aman, and a disbeliever who enters our territory with aman. (Shall not harm) our merchant their lives and property unless their king takes his property, or imprisons him, or other similar actions with his knowledge, and what he has taken out.
[1] His statement: "Shall not harm" – meaning it is prohibited for our merchant who enters their territory with aman to harm any of their life, property, or private parts. Because by granting aman (safe conduct), it is guaranteed that he will not harm them by killing, destroying property, etc. So harming would be treachery, and it is forbidden by text as mentioned, with a distinction between this and deception in war. However, if their ruler or someone with his authorization betrays him by imprisonment, taking property, or the like, then it becomes permissible for him to harm them because the breach of covenant is from their side. This is unlike a captive in Dar al-Harb, for treachery is not prohibited for him because there is no mutual agreement between him and them. So if the captive is able to kill them or take their property, he does so. Thus in al-Nihayah.
[2] His statement: "and their property" – unlike the children of Muslims who are captives among them, for he is allowed to rescue them from their hands if he can. Thus in al-Bahr.
[3] His statement: "except" – an exception from his statement "shall not harm" i.e., it is prohibited for him to harm in all circumstances except when they initiate treachery.
(4) His statement: "and what he has taken out" – a condition of taking it out; because if he usurps something and does not take it out, he must return it to them due to treachery; after taking it out, he owns it as a corrupt ownership because it was obtained by treachery, such that if he takes out a female slave, it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with her, nor for the one who buys from him.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on the musta'min, which covers both Muslims entering enemy territory with aman and non-Muslims entering Muslim territory with aman. The core ruling is that a Muslim merchant who has entered enemy territory under aman is absolutely prohibited from harming the enemy's life, property, or honor. This is because the aman (safe conduct) creates a guarantee of safety; harming would be an act of treachery (ghadr), which is forbidden by the text. However, there is one critical exception: if the enemy ruler or someone with his authority commits treachery against the merchant first—by imprisoning him, taking his property, etc.—then it becomes permitted for the merchant to harm them because the covenant is broken by the enemy's side. This is contrasted with a Muslim captive in Dar al-Harb, who is not bound by any agreement and is thus allowed to harm the enemy if he can. Additionally, the text notes that if a musta'min takes property from the enemy through treachery (e.g., by force after the enemy's breach), he must physically remove it from their territory to own it; otherwise, he must return it. Even after removal, his ownership is considered corrupt (mal khabith), so if it is a female slave, he may not have intercourse with her or sell her for that purpose. Finally, the exception does not apply to rescuing Muslim captives; one may rescue them even without the enemy's breach, as that is a greater good.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A person (Muslim or non-Muslim) who has been granted aman (safe conduct) to enter enemy or Muslim territory safely.
غدر (ghadr): Treachery; breaking a covenant or promise of safety.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is it permissible for a Muslim merchant who entered enemy territory with aman to harm the enemy's property?
A) Whenever he suspects they are planning an attack.
B) Only if the enemy ruler or his authority first takes his property or imprisons him.
C) If he is a captive, he may always harm them.
D) He can never harm them under any circumstance.

Key Takeaway
Harm to a musta'min is treachery forbidden by sacred law, but the covenant is reciprocal: if the enemy breaches first, the prohibition lifts and the musta'min may retaliate.
